	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Hugo W. Koehler</h1>

<p><b>Hugo William Koehler</b> (July 19, 1886 – June 17, 1941) (pronounced KAY-ler) was a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/Commander" title="Commander">commander</a>, <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">secret agent</a> and socialite. Following the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>, he served as an <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Naval_Intelligence" title="Office of Naval Intelligence">Office of Naval Intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/State_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="State Department">State Department</a> operative in Russia during its <a href="/wiki/Russian_Civil_War" title="Russian Civil War">civil war</a>, and later as <a href="/wiki/Naval_attach%C3%A9" class="mw-redirect" title="Naval attaché">naval attaché</a> to <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>. He was rumored to be the illegitimate son of the <a href="/wiki/Crown_Prince" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown Prince">Crown Prince</a> of <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> and to have assisted the <a href="/wiki/Romanovs" class="mw-redirect" title="Romanovs">Romanovs</a> in fleeing Russia following the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">revolution</a> of 1917.  He was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Navy_Cross" title="Navy Cross">Navy Cross</a> for his service during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and was the step-father of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senator" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senator">United States Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/Claiborne_Pell" title="Claiborne Pell">Claiborne Pell</a> (1918–2009).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_family_history">Early life and family history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and family history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oscar_C._Koehler.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Oscar_C._Koehler.jpg/220px-Oscar_C._Koehler.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="308" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/Oscar_C._Koehler.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="420" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oscar_C._Koehler.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oscar C. Koehler</div></div></div>
<p>Hugo W. Koehler was born on July 19, 1886, in <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis, Missouri</a>, and named after a paternal uncle. His father, Oscar C. Koehler (1857–1902), was a first-generation German-American and second-generation St. Louis and <a href="/wiki/Davenport,_Iowa" title="Davenport, Iowa">Davenport, Iowa</a> <a href="/wiki/Brewer" class="mw-redirect" title="Brewer">brewer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneur" class="mw-redirect" title="Entrepreneur">entrepreneur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Although it was privately rumored for much of his life that Hugo was the illegitimate son of <a href="/wiki/Rudolf,_Crown_Prince_of_Austria" title="Rudolf, Crown Prince of Austria">Rudolf, Crown Prince of Austria</a>, who is generally believed to have died with his teenage mistress, <a href="/wiki/Baroness_Mary_Vetsera" title="Baroness Mary Vetsera">Baroness Mary Vetsera</a>, in a <a href="/wiki/Murder-suicide" class="mw-redirect" title="Murder-suicide">murder-suicide</a> pact in January 1889 (the "<a href="/wiki/Mayerling_incident" title="Mayerling incident">Mayerling incident</a>"), no corroborating evidence of this ancestry has been established.  The speculation was fueled by a few factors: 1) Koehler, who was reputed to be the "wealthiest officer" in the American navy in the 1920s, was apparently the beneficiary of a substantial <a href="/wiki/Trust_fund" class="mw-redirect" title="Trust fund">trust fund</a>. It was further speculated that this trust had been established by the <a href="/wiki/Habsburgs" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburgs">Habsburgs</a> with approval of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a> when Hugo Koehler was a child, to provide for his support and maintenance; 2) As a child, Hugo made several European visits with his paternal grandfather, Heinrich (Henry) Koehler, Sr.] (1828-1909), where he was introduced to aristocracy and elites;<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and 3) Koehler had the distinctive Habsburg chin.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ironically, in 1945, <i>The New York Times</i> published a story that a German lithographer by the name of "Hugo Koehler", following his death at the age of 93, was revealed to be <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Johann_Salvator_of_Austria" title="Archduke Johann Salvator of Austria">Archduke Johann Salvator of Austria</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II,_Grand_Duke_of_Tuscany" title="Leopold II, Grand Duke of Tuscany">Leopold II, Grand Duke of Tuscany</a> and a member of the Imperial Austrian Habsburg family. Johann had been a witness to the tragic events at <a href="/wiki/Mayerling" title="Mayerling">Mayerling</a> in 1889.  Rudolf, unsatisfied in his marriage to <a href="/wiki/Princess_St%C3%A9phanie_of_Belgium" title="Princess Stéphanie of Belgium">Stephanie</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II_of_Belgium" title="Leopold II of Belgium">Leopold II of Belgium</a> had found romance with Mary, the younger daughter of a Hungarian earl.  Taking her along on a party to his hunting lodge at Mayerling, the young woman soon became excessively animated and sought to interject herself into court politics and intrigue.  Rudolf snubbed her for this, and in a fit of rage, the 17-year old baroness smashed a bottle of champagne over Rudolf's head, killing him.  Hearing the fracas and then a gunshot, Johann and other revelers on a nearby balcony, rushed into the salon to discover that Rudolf's valet had avenged his master's death with a single, fatal gunshot to Mary Vetsera.  The event was immediately <a href="/wiki/Cover-up" title="Cover-up">covered-up</a>, and the story of a murder-suicide was fabricated.  Enemies of both Rudolf and Johann, began spreading innuendo that Johann was implicated in Rudolf's death.  When <a href="/wiki/Franz_Joseph_I_of_Austria" title="Franz Joseph I of Austria">Emperor Franz Joseph</a> questioned the young <a href="/wiki/Archduke" title="Archduke">archduke</a> about these rumors in 1889, Johan became furious and broke his sword over his knee. Tearing the <a href="/wiki/Epaulets" class="mw-redirect" title="Epaulets">epaulets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military_awards_and_decorations" title="Military awards and decorations">decorations</a> off his uniform, Johann threw them at the feet of the emperor, and renounced his right to the throne until such time as he might be cleared of the false accusations.  The Emperor, indignant at Johan's actions, sentenced him to lose his princely rights and succession for twenty years.  Johan then left the country and assumed his new life and the name "John Orth".  Orth and his wife, an actress, were believed lost at sea in a shipwreck off <a href="/wiki/Cape_Horn" title="Cape Horn">Cape Horn</a> in 1890 and were declared dead <a href="/wiki/In_absentia" title="In absentia">in absentia</a> in 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The 1945 story of Johan's (Orth's) survival has not been established as true.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heinrich_Koehler.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/Heinrich_Koehler.jpg/220px-Heinrich_Koehler.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="316" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/Heinrich_Koehler.jpg/330px-Heinrich_Koehler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d6/Heinrich_Koehler.jpg 2x" data-file-width="418" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heinrich_Koehler.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Henry (Heinrich) Koehler</div></div></div>
<p>Koehler's grandfather, Heinrich, was born in the state of <a href="/wiki/Hesse" title="Hesse">Hesse</a> in Germany. A master-brewer trained at <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a>, he had immigrated to America at the age of twenty-one, quickly making his way to St. Louis, where German-Americans accounted for nearly one-third the population of 78,000 in 1850.  Working his way up to <a href="/wiki/Shop_foreman" title="Shop foreman">foreman</a> at the Lemp brewery, by 1851 Koehler had saved enough <a href="/wiki/Financial_capital" title="Financial capital">capital</a> to start his own <a href="/wiki/Brewery" title="Brewery">brewery</a>, and moved 225 miles upriver on the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi</a> to <a href="/wiki/Fort_Madison,_Iowa" title="Fort Madison, Iowa">Fort Madison, Iowa</a>, where he purchased a small, but established brewery and introduced <a href="/wiki/Lager_beer" class="mw-redirect" title="Lager beer">lager beer</a> to the surrounding area.  Around 1863, Heinrich, who now went by "Henry", leased his brewery to his father-in-law, and moved back to St. Louis where he bought a partnership interest in the Excelsior Brewery from his younger brother, Casper (1835–1910).  By then the city's population had swelled to more than 300,000 and the brothers aimed to increase beer production and sell to the thousands of Federal troops stationed in the city environs.  While the brewery flourished, the brothers' relationship did not.  In 1871, Henry sold his interest to Casper and moved his family upriver to <a href="/wiki/Davenport,_Iowa" title="Davenport, Iowa">Davenport, Iowa</a>.  Davenport was then part of the <a href="/wiki/Quad_Cities" title="Quad Cities">Tri-cities</a> metropolitan area, including the neighboring riverfront cities of <a href="/wiki/Moline,_Illinois" title="Moline, Illinois">Moline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rock_Island,_Illinois" title="Rock Island, Illinois">Rock Island, Illinois</a>.  A year later, he bought the established Arsenal Brewery with his brother-in-law, Rudolph Lange.  The business was also known as Koehler and Lange, after its owners.  With a growing, largely Germanic <a href="/wiki/Immigrant" class="mw-redirect" title="Immigrant">immigrant</a> population that regarded beer as a <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple</a>, Davenport had cheaper <a href="/wiki/Operating_costs" class="mw-redirect" title="Operating costs">operating costs</a> than St. Louis, including river and rail transportation with caves along the river where the beer could be stored. The area proved ideal to expand the business.  Weathering obstacles, including the rising <a href="/wiki/Temperance_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperance Movement">Temperance Movement</a>, in 1884 the partners were industry forerunners when they introduced a non-alcoholic beer, called <i>Mumm</i>, derived from a German word meaning disguise or mask.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oscar_C._Koehler_&#124;_Immigrant_Entrepreneurship_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oscar_C._Koehler_|_Immigrant_Entrepreneurship-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Sect_Wine_Company_1890.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bc/The_Sect_Wine_Company_1890.jpg/300px-The_Sect_Wine_Company_1890.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bc/The_Sect_Wine_Company_1890.jpg/450px-The_Sect_Wine_Company_1890.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/bc/The_Sect_Wine_Company_1890.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="559" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Sect_Wine_Company_1890.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sect Wine Company, St. Louis, Missouri, 1888</div></div></div><p>In 1875, seventeen-year old Oscar Koehler, the oldest of Henry's seven children, obtained a passport to study in Germany.  After completing studies at the Academy of Brewing in <a href="/wiki/Worms,_Germany" title="Worms, Germany">Worms</a>, he entered the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Leipzig" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Leipzig">University of Leipzig</a> where he obtained a <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">doctorate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> four years later.  Returning to America, he was exceptionally trained for the brewing industry. After a brief stint at his father's brewery in Davenport, Oscar moved to St. Louis where he became secretary of the Henry Koehler Brewing Assoc., a business his father established in 1880. The following year, Oscar was joined by his younger brother, Henry Jr. (1863–1912), and by 1883 the business was sold to the St. Louis Brewing Assoc.  As that business was winding down, the Koehler brothers were setting up their successor business, "The Sect Wine Company", which Oscar and his father had started preparing for in 1880 on a stock offering of $135,000 ($3.4 mil. in 2010). With the stated purpose "to deal in nothing but strictly pure wines, recognizing that he who appreciates a fair article will willingly pay a fair price to obtain it", the business occupied a two-story distillery and winery of 80,000 square feet at 2814-24 South Seventh Street in St. Louis. Production of the company's signature <a href="/wiki/Champagne" title="Champagne">champagne</a> "Koehler's <a href="/wiki/Sparkling_wine" title="Sparkling wine">Sect</a>" and its still wines was overseen by experienced winemakers from <a href="/wiki/Rheims,_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Rheims, France">Rheims, France</a>, and marketed by a staff of travelling <a href="/wiki/Salesmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Salesmen">salesmen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mathilda_Follenius_Lange_Koehler.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/73/Mathilda_Follenius_Lange_Koehler.jpg/200px-Mathilda_Follenius_Lange_Koehler.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/73/Mathilda_Follenius_Lange_Koehler.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="228" data-file-height="242" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mathilda_Follenius_Lange_Koehler.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mathilda Lange Koehler</div></div></div><p>
The Koehler's expansion into the winemaking business, particularly the popular "Koehler's Sect", enhanced both their wealth and prestige in the St. Louis business community.  Their marriages and social activities further solidified their elite standing.  In 1885, Oscar married Mathilda Lange (1866–1947), daughter of William Lange, president of the National Bank of St. Louis.  In 1888, two years after his marriage to Mathilda, Oscar joined the Germania Club, an exclusive club open only to St. Louis residents who had graduated from German universities. Oscar was one of only sixty-one members.<sup id="cite_ref-Oscar_C._Koehler_&#124;_Immigrant_Entrepreneurship_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oscar_C._Koehler_|_Immigrant_Entrepreneurship-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In 1897, Henry Jr. married the California actress Margaret Craven in a San Francisco society wedding that was publicized in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a>, the former writing that Craven had married "Henry Koehler, a rich brewer of St. Louis"<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and the latter reporting that "Miss Craven is a professional actress and is noted for her beauty. The groom is 35 years old and a millionaire."<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>  Oscar and Henry Koehler, Jr., remained in the wine business until 1890. In 1887, they were joined by their younger brother, Hugo (1868–1939), who had originally moved to the city to attend <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Medical_College" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis Medical College">St. Louis Medical College</a>.  In January 1890, the brothers began their most successful venture in the series of business ventures that ultimately amassed them millions of dollars, when they organized the American Brewing Company (A.B.C.) with $300,000 ($7.42 million in 2010) in capital.  Constructed on the site of the Sect Wine Company and other Koehler businesses on South Seventh Street in St. Louis, the huge plant was praised for its architectural design and function, employing the latest in brewing technology including the largest copper brew kettles ever made. As with their champagne and wines, the Koehler's <a href="/wiki/Mission_statement" title="Mission statement">mission statement</a> was "to produce beers of the highest class only, and to obtain patronage by furnishing only such an article."  Before long, the Koehlers' beer, particularly their A.B.C. Bohemian was available through wholesale distributors (typically German-Americans) in locations as far away as <a href="/wiki/Albuquerque,_New_Mexico" title="Albuquerque, New Mexico">Albuquerque, New Mexico</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Birmingham,_Alabama" title="Birmingham, Alabama">Birmingham, Alabama</a>.  Ultimately, the brewery expanded into foreign distribution, including <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, before closing in 1940, never recovering from the negative business impact caused by <a href="/wiki/Prohibition" title="Prohibition">Prohibition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Koehler_Bros._American_Brewing_Company.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/Koehler_Bros._American_Brewing_Company.jpg/300px-Koehler_Bros._American_Brewing_Company.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/Koehler_Bros._American_Brewing_Company.jpg/450px-Koehler_Bros._American_Brewing_Company.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/Koehler_Bros._American_Brewing_Company.jpg/600px-Koehler_Bros._American_Brewing_Company.jpg 2x" data-file-width="765" data-file-height="508" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Koehler_Bros._American_Brewing_Company.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Koehler Bros.' American Brewing Company (A.B.C. Brewing Co.), St. Louis, Missouri</div></div></div><p>
In 1894, Oscar left the American Brewing Company and St. Louis, to move back to Davenport, where he took over his father's interest in the Arsenal Brewery (Koehler and Lange).  The change was prompted by Henry's decision to take an extended trip to Europe, where he stayed for two years. In 1895, Oscar commissioned the architect <a href="/wiki/Frederick_G._Clausen" title="Frederick G. Clausen">Friedrich Clausen</a> to construct a 5-bedroom, 3,700 sq. ft. <a href="/wiki/Queen_Anne_style_architecture_in_the_United_States" title="Queen Anne style architecture in the United States">Queen Anne</a> residence at 817 W. 7th Street, overlooking the bluffs of the Mississippi River on Davenport's <a href="/wiki/Hamburg_Historic_District_(Davenport,_Iowa)" title="Hamburg Historic District (Davenport, Iowa)">Gold Coast</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oscar_C._Koehler_&#124;_Immigrant_Entrepreneurship_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oscar_C._Koehler_|_Immigrant_Entrepreneurship-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>  Young Hugo lived his childhood and adolescence with wealth and privilege.  The oldest of six children (Elise (1887-1971), Herbert (1888-1945), Otillie (1894- 1975), Eda (1900-1958), and Hildegarde (1901-1926)), his childhood memories were of preferential treatment over his brother and sisters. Never being disciplined and having the best pony and cart, engendered the notion in Hugo that he was "different".  A prankster in his youth, he delighted in trying the limits of his grandfather's patience. A wine collector, Henry would organize wine-tasting dinners for his friends, who were asked to identify the wine and vintage after trying it.  Once, Hugo decided to substitute inferior wines, believing his grandfather and his friends could not tell the difference. Hiding behind the curtains, Hugo was surprised to see his grandfather's reaction on sampling the wine, as he rose to apologize to his guests that there "must have been some mistake".  When the boy confessed to the deceit later, his grandfather was more hurt than angry. For him, there were certain lines that no gentleman would cross.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Hugo journeyed to <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> with his grandfather, where he was introduced to the <a href="/wiki/Human_physiology" class="mw-redirect" title="Human physiology">human physiologist</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Scott_Haldane" title="John Scott Haldane">John Scott Haldane</a>, statesman-naturalist <a href="/wiki/Edward_Grey,_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon" title="Edward Grey, 1st Viscount Grey of Fallodon">Lord Grey of Fallodon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Empress_Elisabeth_of_Austria" title="Empress Elisabeth of Austria">Empress Elisabeth</a>. In Austria, the boy was introduced to the court of Emperor Franz Josef.  As Koehler put it years later, "After all that, water pistols were not very interesting."<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Davenport_Malting_Company.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/29/Davenport_Malting_Company.jpg/300px-Davenport_Malting_Company.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="350" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/29/Davenport_Malting_Company.jpg/450px-Davenport_Malting_Company.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/29/Davenport_Malting_Company.jpg/600px-Davenport_Malting_Company.jpg 2x" data-file-width="655" data-file-height="765" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Davenport_Malting_Company.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Davenport Malting Co., Davenport, Iowa, 1904</div></div></div><p> On one European summer vacation, Henry asked the youth what he might do with his life.  Hugo responded that he would like to be a philosopher, or a <a href="/wiki/Jesuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> or a naval officer. His grandfather responded that the boy was already somewhat of a philosopher and observed that for his grandson, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> priesthood would involve "more disadvantages than advantages." He concluded, "if you want to be a naval officer, you will have to get some education first, for the Naval Academy gives you only a training."<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the consolidation trend in the brewing industry, in October 1894, Hugo's father, Oscar, merged the Arsenal Brewery with four other breweries into a single firm, Davenport Malting Company, becoming president of the new concern. The <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_scale" class="mw-redirect" title="Economy of scale">economy of scale</a> enabled the firm to bar outside competition that could not compete with the low production costs.  In 1895, the company built a new brewery with modern equipment and expansive storage facilities on the site of the former Lehrkind Brewery on West Second Street. Its <a href="/wiki/Product_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Product line">product line</a> included Davenport Malt Standard (keg beer), Muenchener (both <a href="/wiki/Keg" title="Keg">keg</a> and bottled), and Pale Export (bottled). Strong sales of Pale Export and the Muenchener beers provided the revenue for the addition of a state-of-the-art ice plant in 1896.  In the spring of 1897, the company expanded its plant again, adding a new racking room, wash house, and <a href="/wiki/Hops" title="Hops">hop</a> storage area.  By 1898 sales were so strong that the company increased its <a href="/wiki/Capital_stock" class="mw-redirect" title="Capital stock">capital stock</a> from $50,000 to $150,000 ($1.36 million to $4.07 million in 2010) that provided the funding for a <a href="/wiki/Malt" title="Malt">malt</a> house.  The Davenport Malting Company was the second largest in the state of Iowa, employing sixty men and with fifteen distribution agencies scattered throughout the state. The plant had a capacity of 100,000 barrels, reportedly selling over 50,000 barrels in 1899.  Unfortunately, Oscar Koehler did not live to see the success of the brewery continue into the 20th century, largely due to his effort.  After months of declining health, in 1902 he died at St. Louis, where he had gone for treatment of <a href="/wiki/Bright%27s_Disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Bright&#39;s Disease">Bright's Disease</a>, a form of kidney failure.  Following his father's death, fifteen-year old Hugo's education was undertaken by his grandfather, Henry, who sent the boy to the prestigious <a href="/wiki/Phillips_Exeter_Academy" title="Phillips Exeter Academy">Phillips Exeter Academy</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a> for his senior year in 1902.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Harvard_and_Annapolis">Harvard and Annapolis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Harvard and Annapolis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 220px"><div style="width: 220px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 218px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler,_USN,_1909_%22Lucky_Bag%22.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/56/Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN%2C_1909_%22Lucky_Bag%22.jpg/327px-Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN%2C_1909_%22Lucky_Bag%22.jpg" decoding="async" width="218" height="300" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/56/Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN%2C_1909_%22Lucky_Bag%22.jpg/490px-Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN%2C_1909_%22Lucky_Bag%22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/56/Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN%2C_1909_%22Lucky_Bag%22.jpg/654px-Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN%2C_1909_%22Lucky_Bag%22.jpg 2x" data-file-width="781" data-file-height="1075" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Midshipman Hugo W. Koehler, USN, in the 1909 <a href="/wiki/Lucky_Bag" title="Lucky Bag">Lucky Bag</a>
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Koehler attended <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a> for his <a href="/wiki/Freshman" title="Freshman">freshman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sophomore" title="Sophomore">sophomore</a> years, before entering the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Academy" title="United States Naval Academy">United States Naval Academy</a> in 1905.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In the summer between Harvard and Annapolis, Koehler gave a preview of his brazen ability to bluff that would serve him well in war-ravaged Germany and Russia fifteen years later.  Hungry and broke, he and three friends drove to <a href="/wiki/New_York_city" class="mw-redirect" title="New York city">New York city</a> from <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts" title="Cambridge, Massachusetts">Cambridge, Massachusetts</a>, after changing three tires along the way. Arriving in the city, Koehler suggested a trip to the theater.  Marching up to the ticket window, he barked, "I say, gimmi mi tickets."  The ticket-man politely asked "What name?" and Koehler gave his last name.  Knowing the ticket-man would not find any tickets under "Koehler" and that the ticket agencies were closed, Koehler fumed, "call Tyson and see about it."  Koehler's ploy worked, and the ticket agent offered them choice tickets that Koehler condescendingly took. Coming out of the theater later and still famished, the group headed up <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">Fifth Avenue</a> and bumped into one of Koehler's best friends who staked them for a meal.  "We paid for the tickets the next day, for we thought we might want to work the same game again."<sup id="cite_ref-:1_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Koehler wrote in a letter shortly after arriving at Annapolis, "Everybody in town, officers, professors, and 'cits', (fellow midshipmen) thinks it most extraordinary that I should go into the Academy ... they say that for a man who has tasted life at Harvard, it is a mighty hard thing to buckle down to the exact routine [and] discipline... in practice here [but] I am going to do my very best here, to make up for my past, and to do something for my future and for the future of the ones I love." What in the eighteen-year old Koehler's past he felt that he had to make up for is unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>  The stories of his arrival at Annapolis vary only in their "outrageousness": that he arrived with a horse and valet; another recounts a horse and cook. While at the Academy, he maintained a <a href="/wiki/Pied_a_terre" class="mw-redirect" title="Pied a terre">pied a terre</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Majordomo" title="Majordomo">steward</a>, where hot food was always available to all comers.  He was known for his success as a <a href="/wiki/Lothario" title="Lothario">ladies' man</a>, often sending <a href="/wiki/Rosa_%27American_Beauty%27" title="Rosa &#39;American Beauty&#39;">American Beauty roses</a> to the objects of his affection.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>  At Annapolis, Koehler qualified as a rifle expert shot in 1908, the highest level, and was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Marksmanship_badges_(United_States)" title="Marksmanship badges (United States)">Navy Sharpshooter's Badge</a>. Somewhat presciently, the page entry for Koehler in the Naval Academy <a href="/wiki/Annual_publication" title="Annual publication">annual</a> <a href="/wiki/Lucky_Bag" title="Lucky Bag">Lucky Bag</a> contains two quotes from Shakespeare that were placed by the editors and intended to capture his <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a>, "The glass of fashion and the mold of form, the observed of all observers", and "I am not in the roll of common men." The yearbook editors' words are equally descriptive, "A capable, conceited man who cannot be bluffed."  During the winter before Koehler graduated from Annapolis, his grandfather passed away. Before he died, Henry revealed the purported secret of Hugo's birth.  He told Hugo that his father was not Oscar Koehler, but Rudolph, crown prince of Austria.  There is no record of how Hugo responded to this shocking disclosure. But for the rest of his life, Koehler searched for some proof of this. He revealed the story to only his closest confidants, and speculated that the truth was locked in the archives of the Vatican, that could not be opened for 99-years, until 1987, long after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the terms of Oscar Koehler's <a href="/wiki/Will_and_testament" title="Will and testament">will</a>, the Koehler residence on Seventh Street passed to his wife, Mathilda Koehler, with the remainder of the estate held in trust by the executors, who were to invest the money and pay the interest to Mathilda, on a semi-annual basis for the rest of her life. Following her death, the remaining trust was to be divided equally among the children when they came of age.<sup id="cite_ref-Oscar_C._Koehler_&#124;_Immigrant_Entrepreneurship_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oscar_C._Koehler_|_Immigrant_Entrepreneurship-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This would not explain the mysterious "Austrian trust" with Hugo Koehler as beneficiary, or the inexplicable source of income that gave him the reputation as the "richest officer" in the U.S. Navy. "[K]oehler seemed to possess a beneficent parachute that popped open precisely when he needed it."<sup id="cite_ref-:1_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Koehler had been taught to speak German and French, along with English.  He spoke British English with a slight German accent, from his <a href="/wiki/Midwest" class="mw-redirect" title="Midwest">Midwest</a> roots. During his four-years at Annapolis, Koehler's performance varied wildly. In May 1908, he ranked 12th in seamanship, 16th in the three separate categories of math, mechanics, and ordinance; 93rd in efficiency; and 161st in marine engines.  He had probably enjoyed the bumps along the road to 215 demerits.  One time, he and his roommate, future rear admiral Ernest Ludolph Gunther (1887–1948) fought so savagely they both ended up in the hospital. Amazingly, the <i>Lucky Bag</i> reported, Gunther "managed to room with Hugo for four years and still maintains his equilibrium of mind." Ultimately, Koehler finished in the exact middle of his class.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Navy_career">Navy career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Navy career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yangtze_Patrol">Yangtze Patrol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Yangtze Patrol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NH_84449_Paymaster_William_J._Littell,_USN_(L),_Lt._JG_Calvin_P._Page,_USN_(center),_midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler,_USN_at_Kuling,_China,_c._1912-1914.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/NH_84449_Paymaster_William_J._Littell%2C_USN_%28L%29%2C_Lt._JG_Calvin_P._Page%2C_USN_%28center%29%2C_midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN_at_Kuling%2C_China%2C_c._1912-1914.jpg/300px-NH_84449_Paymaster_William_J._Littell%2C_USN_%28L%29%2C_Lt._JG_Calvin_P._Page%2C_USN_%28center%29%2C_midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN_at_Kuling%2C_China%2C_c._1912-1914.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/NH_84449_Paymaster_William_J._Littell%2C_USN_%28L%29%2C_Lt._JG_Calvin_P._Page%2C_USN_%28center%29%2C_midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN_at_Kuling%2C_China%2C_c._1912-1914.jpg/450px-NH_84449_Paymaster_William_J._Littell%2C_USN_%28L%29%2C_Lt._JG_Calvin_P._Page%2C_USN_%28center%29%2C_midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN_at_Kuling%2C_China%2C_c._1912-1914.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/NH_84449_Paymaster_William_J._Littell%2C_USN_%28L%29%2C_Lt._JG_Calvin_P._Page%2C_USN_%28center%29%2C_midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN_at_Kuling%2C_China%2C_c._1912-1914.jpg/600px-NH_84449_Paymaster_William_J._Littell%2C_USN_%28L%29%2C_Lt._JG_Calvin_P._Page%2C_USN_%28center%29%2C_midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN_at_Kuling%2C_China%2C_c._1912-1914.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1220" data-file-height="852" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NH_84449_Paymaster_William_J._Littell,_USN_(L),_Lt._JG_Calvin_P._Page,_USN_(center),_midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler,_USN_at_Kuling,_China,_c._1912-1914.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Paymaster William J. Littell, USN, Lt. jg Calvin P. Page, USN, Ensign Hugo W. Koehler, USN at <a href="/wiki/Guling,_Jiangxi" title="Guling, Jiangxi">Kuling</a>, China, circa 1912–1913</div></div></div><p>
Koehler graduated from the Naval Academy in June 1909 and as a <a href="/wiki/Passed_midshipman" title="Passed midshipman">passed midshipman</a> was attached to the <a href="/wiki/Armored_cruiser" title="Armored cruiser">armored cruiser</a> <a href="/wiki/USS_New_York_(ACR-2)" title="USS New York (ACR-2)">USS&#160;<i>New York</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(ACR-2)</span></a> on October 29 of that year.  During his time aboard, <i>New York</i> made two cruises to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and steamed to the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.  <i>New York</i> was renamed <i>Saratoga</i> in February 1911.  In August, he was attached to the gunboat <a href="/wiki/USS_Villalobos_(PG-42)" title="USS Villalobos (PG-42)">USS&#160;<i>Villalobos</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(PG-42)</span></a> which was assigned to the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_River_Patrol" class="mw-redirect" title="Yangtze River Patrol">Yangtze River Patrol</a> in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> "on the broad principle of extending American protection to wherever this country's nationals resided for Gold, Glory or Gospel," observed Koehler. Fifty years later, <i>Villalobos</i> was the inspiration for the fictional <a href="/wiki/USS_San_Pablo_(AVP-30)" title="USS San Pablo (AVP-30)">USS&#160;<i>San Pablo</i></a> in <a href="/wiki/Richard_McKenna" title="Richard McKenna">Richard McKenna</a>'s novel and later movie, "<a href="/wiki/The_Sand_Pebbles" title="The Sand Pebbles">The Sand Pebbles</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>  But in 1911 when Koehler shipped aboard, it was only months before the "Last Emperor", <a href="/wiki/Pu-Yi" class="mw-redirect" title="Pu-Yi">Pu-Yi</a> <a href="/wiki/Abdicated" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdicated">abdicated</a> the throne in January 1912. Before joining <i>Villalobos</i>, Koehler visited the battlefields of <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Port_Arthur" title="Siege of Port Arthur">Port Arthur</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> had battled <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> six years earlier in the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a>. In <i>Villalobos's</i> logbook for October 11, 1911, Koehler wrote, "At 0800 Mids. H.W. Koehler, U.S. Navy, boarded the Chinese gunboat to obtain further information regarding the situation at <a href="/wiki/Wuchang" class="mw-redirect" title="Wuchang">Wuchang</a>... Wuchang... was entirely in the hands of rebels and there was no possibility of communication with the American residents." Two days later, the log continues, "Midshipman Koehler attended a conference of the consular body at which the Japanese rear admiral Reijiro Kawashima presided, the meeting having been called to consider communications of the rebel commander in chief, in which he stated the aims of the rebel party, and offered to send troops to protect the foreign concessions.  The offer was not accepted... at 1815 sent ashore two Colt automatic guns to American consulate in order to facilitate landing of armed party.... Incendiary fires in Wuchang throughout the night." </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rear_Admiral_R._F._Nicholson,_USN_and_Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler,_USN_on_excursion,_Kuling_China_1912-14.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/Rear_Admiral_R._F._Nicholson%2C_USN_and_Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN_on_excursion%2C_Kuling_China_1912-14.jpg/300px-Rear_Admiral_R._F._Nicholson%2C_USN_and_Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN_on_excursion%2C_Kuling_China_1912-14.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/Rear_Admiral_R._F._Nicholson%2C_USN_and_Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN_on_excursion%2C_Kuling_China_1912-14.jpg/450px-Rear_Admiral_R._F._Nicholson%2C_USN_and_Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN_on_excursion%2C_Kuling_China_1912-14.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/Rear_Admiral_R._F._Nicholson%2C_USN_and_Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN_on_excursion%2C_Kuling_China_1912-14.jpg/600px-Rear_Admiral_R._F._Nicholson%2C_USN_and_Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler%2C_USN_on_excursion%2C_Kuling_China_1912-14.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1191" data-file-height="689" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rear_Admiral_R._F._Nicholson,_USN_and_Midshipman_Hugo_W._Koehler,_USN_on_excursion,_Kuling_China_1912-14.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>R. Adm. <a href="/wiki/Reginald_F._Nicholson" title="Reginald F. Nicholson">Reginald F. Nicholson</a>, USN, Commander in Chief, Asiatic Fleet (CINCAF), with Ens. Hugo W. Koehler, USN en route <a href="/wiki/Guling,_Jiangxi" title="Guling, Jiangxi">Kuling</a>, China, c. 1912–13</div></div></div><p>By mid-November, the Yangtze and all of China was in control of the rebels and "the gun-boaters could get back to the normal routine of golf, billiards, tennis and light conversation."  Koehler was promoted to ensign on March 28, 1912 to date from June 5, 1911. Koehler was equally at-ease and engaging with the unwashed masses as he was with the aristocracy of his time.  He wrote to his mother about a "wonderful tiger hunting expedition" in Mongolia that he made in November 1912 when he was on leave.  The twenty-six year old ensign was already accepted as an equal by the likes of his hunting partners: Prince <a href="/wiki/Pappenheim" title="Pappenheim">Pappenheim</a>, first cousin to <a href="/wiki/King_Edward_VII" class="mw-redirect" title="King Edward VII">King Edward VII</a>; Baron Cottu, financier of the <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou%E2%80%93Hankou_railway" title="Guangzhou–Hankou railway">Canton-Hankow-Peking railway</a><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and Count Riccardo Fabbricotti, "the best looking man in Europe, the wildest and greatest <a href="/wiki/Rake_(stock_character)" title="Rake (stock character)">rake</a>", married to <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>'s cousin, Cornelia Roosevelt Scovel.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Koehler described Fabbricotti's wife as "perfectly lovely, adores him and understands him not at all", which could equally describe the woman Koehler married fifteen years later.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
Koehler was detached from <i>Villalobos</i> and to <i>Saratoga</i> (ex- USS <i>New York</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Asiatic_Fleet" class="mw-redirect" title="Asiatic Fleet">Asiatic Fleet</a> <a href="/wiki/Flagship" title="Flagship">flagship</a>, on January 2, 1913, Commander <a href="/wiki/Henry_A._Wiley" title="Henry A. Wiley">Henry A. Wiley</a>, commanding. Koehler was already coveting the billet of naval attaché to Moscow, when he was assigned as translator for an extended tour of Asian ports by <a href="/wiki/Rear_Admiral" class="mw-redirect" title="Rear Admiral">Rear Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/Reginald_F._Nicholson" title="Reginald F. Nicholson">Reginald F. Nicholson</a>, Commander in Chief, Asiatic Fleet (CINCAF), including <a href="/wiki/Saigon" class="mw-redirect" title="Saigon">Saigon</a>, the French port and <a href="/wiki/Tsingtao" class="mw-redirect" title="Tsingtao">Tsingtao</a>, the German port.  As Koehler wrote, "I have been studying hard, for I intend to learn Russian. So many of my friends are Russian, and I like the Russians, so ... you see I am still thinking of my billet as attaché."<sup id="cite_ref-:3_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Saratoga_ship%27s_company_at_Shanghai_1913.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/14/USS_Saratoga_ship%27s_company_at_Shanghai_1913.jpg/220px-USS_Saratoga_ship%27s_company_at_Shanghai_1913.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/14/USS_Saratoga_ship%27s_company_at_Shanghai_1913.jpg/330px-USS_Saratoga_ship%27s_company_at_Shanghai_1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/14/USS_Saratoga_ship%27s_company_at_Shanghai_1913.jpg/440px-USS_Saratoga_ship%27s_company_at_Shanghai_1913.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1705" data-file-height="1337" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Saratoga_ship%27s_company_at_Shanghai_1913.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>USS Saratoga (ACR-2) ship's company at Shanghai, 1912-13. Cdr. Henry Wiley is seated fourth from left. Ens. Hugo Koehler is second row, standing, fourth from left</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lt._CDR_Lewis_Coxe_USN.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d3/Lt._CDR_Lewis_Coxe_USN.jpg/220px-Lt._CDR_Lewis_Coxe_USN.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d3/Lt._CDR_Lewis_Coxe_USN.jpg/330px-Lt._CDR_Lewis_Coxe_USN.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d3/Lt._CDR_Lewis_Coxe_USN.jpg/440px-Lt._CDR_Lewis_Coxe_USN.jpg 2x" data-file-width="603" data-file-height="637" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lt._CDR_Lewis_Coxe_USN.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lt. Cdr. Lewis Coxe, USN</div></div></div><p>On January 26, 1914, Ensign Koehler was given command of the <a href="/wiki/USS_Piscataqua_(1866)" title="USS Piscataqua (1866)">USS&#160;<i>Piscataqua</i></a>, a fleet tug based at <a href="/wiki/Olongapo_Naval_Station" class="mw-redirect" title="Olongapo Naval Station">Olongapo Naval Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Philippine Islands">Philippine Islands</a>. Under him were three other ensigns and forty enlisted men.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> At one point during his command, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> failed to pass a naval budget appropriation and Koehler paid the men on his ship and several others from his own pocket.  As another eccentricity, Koehler kept a <a href="/wiki/Menagerie" title="Menagerie">menagerie</a> on his ship.  Tragically, his pet <a href="/wiki/Boa_constrictor" title="Boa constrictor">boa constrictor</a> swallowed his small <a href="/wiki/Spotted_deer" class="mw-redirect" title="Spotted deer">spotted deer</a>. Both died when the deer's sharp hooves pierced the snake's side.  During the week of June 1–6, 1914, Lt. Cdr. Lewis Coxe, commanding the Naval Station at Olangapo, ordered Koehler to tow a naval coal <a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">barge</a> that Koehler subsequently deemed <a href="/wiki/Unseaworthy" class="mw-redirect" title="Unseaworthy">unseaworthy</a> after his ship started the tow and discovered the barge was taking on water. Koehler returned to port; however, Coxe insisted that he carry out the order. Koehler did, the barge and its $10,000 cargo capsized, Coxe was <a href="/wiki/Court-martial" title="Court-martial">court-martialed</a>, found guilty of neglect of duty and culpable inefficiency, and Koehler was <a href="/wiki/Exonerated" class="mw-redirect" title="Exonerated">exonerated</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> On June 5, 1914, Koehler was promoted to lieutenant (junior grade).<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> That September, <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of the Navy">Secretary of the Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/Josephus_Daniels" title="Josephus Daniels">Josephus Daniels</a> quoted Koehler's commanding officer and said, "This <a href="/wiki/Koehler" title="Koehler">Koehler</a> is very adaptable and possesses a military spirit unusual in one of his rank.  At the same time he has produced a smart ship, a contented crew, and the ship in general and the engine room are the cleanest I have ever seen."<sup id="cite_ref-:3_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Piscataqua_off_Olongapo,_Philippines_1910%27s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/USS_Piscataqua_off_Olongapo%2C_Philippines_1910%27s.jpg/300px-USS_Piscataqua_off_Olongapo%2C_Philippines_1910%27s.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/USS_Piscataqua_off_Olongapo%2C_Philippines_1910%27s.jpg/450px-USS_Piscataqua_off_Olongapo%2C_Philippines_1910%27s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/USS_Piscataqua_off_Olongapo%2C_Philippines_1910%27s.jpg/600px-USS_Piscataqua_off_Olongapo%2C_Philippines_1910%27s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="738" data-file-height="526" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Piscataqua_off_Olongapo,_Philippines_1910%27s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/USS_Piscataqua" title="USS Piscataqua">USS&#160;<i>Piscataqua</i></a> off <a href="/wiki/Olongapo" title="Olongapo">Olongapo</a>, Philippines, 1910's</div></div></div><p> Koehler detached from <i>Piscataqua</i> in June 1915, and returned to the <a href="/wiki/Continental_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Continental United States">Continental United States</a> aboard the SS Chiyo Maru from Yokohama via Honolulu to San Francisco, and listed on the <a href="/wiki/Manifest_(transportation)" title="Manifest (transportation)">manifest</a> as "Capt. H.W. Koehler".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Before sailing from Japan, Koehler used his Germanic looks and "Kaiser Willie" <a href="/wiki/Mustache" class="mw-redirect" title="Mustache">mustache</a> to bait the Japanese into arresting him as a suspected German spy. With no <a href="/wiki/Identity_cards" class="mw-redirect" title="Identity cards">ID card</a>, commonplace for the time, Koehler spent several days in a Japanese jail, happy to learn what the Japanese were interested in, until the U.S. naval attaché, received a cabled description of Koehler from Washington. More than fifteen years before Japanese imperialism blighted Asia, Koehler wrote, "with Japan and things Japanese... I detest them more each day".<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Returning to the <a href="/wiki/Receiving_ship" class="mw-redirect" title="Receiving ship">receiving ship</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Navy_Yard" title="Brooklyn Navy Yard">Brooklyn Navy Yard</a>, Koehler was briefly attached to the battleship <a href="/wiki/USS_Michigan_(BB-27)" title="USS Michigan (BB-27)">USS&#160;<i>Michigan</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(BB-27)</span></a> with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atlantic_Fleet" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Atlantic Fleet">Atlantic Fleet</a> from September 16,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> until October 5, 1915, when he was attached to her <a href="/wiki/Sister_ship" title="Sister ship">sister ship</a> <a href="/wiki/USS_South_Carolina_(BB-26)" title="USS South Carolina (BB-26)">USS&#160;<i>South Carolina</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(BB-26)</span></a>.  During this time <i>South Carolina</i> operated on the <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="East Coast of the United States">East Coast of the United States</a>, spending summers in maneuvers off <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport, Rhode Island</a> and winters in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_One">World War One</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: World War One">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the United States entry into <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, Koehler was promoted to lieutenant on June 5, 1917.  He detached from <i>South Carolina</i> to serve as an aide to <a href="/wiki/Captain" title="Captain">Captain</a> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_J._Hepburn" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur J. Hepburn">Arthur J. Hepburn</a>, Commander of the <a href="/wiki/Submarine_chaser" title="Submarine chaser">Submarine Chaser Division</a> at the <a href="/wiki/New_London_Navy_Yard" class="mw-redirect" title="New London Navy Yard">New London Navy Yard</a> in <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a> on January 30, 1918.  While in New London he shared an apartment with Lieutenant (j.g.) <a href="/wiki/Harold_S._Vanderbilt" class="mw-redirect" title="Harold S. Vanderbilt">Harold S. "Mike" Vanderbilt</a> whose family fortune stemmed from the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Central_Railroad" title="New York Central Railroad">New York Central Railroad</a>.  When Vanderbilt left Koehler a note which appointed him "official furniture mover for the house we are about to lease. /s/ H.S. Vanderbilt", Koehler, not to be outdone, replied with a note which described an incident in which Roman political philosopher <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> was appointed as garbage remover of Rome by his enemies in order to discredit and discourage him.  Quoting Cicero's reply to his malefactors, "If the office will not bring me honor, I will bring honor to the office," Koehler <a href="/wiki/Wit" title="Wit">quipped</a>, "Rome was very badly in need of a garbage collector. Cicero became the most efficient garbage collector that Rome had ever known, and as a result Cicero was made <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consul</a> and his fame has descended down through the generations.  /s/ Hugo W. Koehler"<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_SC-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/USS_SC-2.jpg/400px-USS_SC-2.jpg" decoding="async" width="400" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/USS_SC-2.jpg/600px-USS_SC-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/USS_SC-2.jpg/800px-USS_SC-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="558" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_SC-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/SC-1-class_submarine_chaser" title="SC-1-class submarine chaser"><i>SC-1</i>-class World War One era submarine chaser</a></div></div></div><p>On June 15, 1918, Koehler followed Captain Hepburn when he was ordered to <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> to take command of the Submarine Chaser Division for the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a> headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Queenstown,_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Queenstown, Ireland">Queenstown, Ireland</a>.  Koehler was promoted to temporary lieutenant commander on July 1, 1918, and given command of a detachment of <a href="/wiki/Submarine_chasers" class="mw-redirect" title="Submarine chasers">submarine chasers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>  Vanderbilt, who arranged for Koehler's entry into the <a href="/wiki/Upper_class" title="Upper class">upper crust</a> British society, was also ordered to Queenstown.  Koehler remained lifelong friends with Vanderbilt, and good-naturedly described his chum as the "boy <a href="/wiki/Navigator" title="Navigator">navigator</a>". Recalling the trans-Atlantic passage to Ireland, Koehler noted that Vanderbilt carried around a <a href="/wiki/Sextant" title="Sextant">sextant</a> "everywhere, and even slept with it I have no doubt... However, he really did take a sight on the way over, and I am creditably informed (by no less person than himself) that the sight actually showed our position as somewhere in the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic">North Atlantic</a>, and not on the top of a mountain, as is sometimes the case with boy navigators.  But at any rate, the captain of the ship did not take his advice too seriously and so we arrived safely."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>  Unlike 1915 Japan, 1918 Ireland welcomed Koehler's Germanic appearance, because it implied anti-<a href="/wiki/England" title="England">Britain</a>, a mistaken assumption that was far from Koehler's actual, lifelong <a href="/wiki/Anglophile" title="Anglophile">anglophilia</a>. One lieutenant remembered him as "a very <a href="/wiki/Teutons" title="Teutons">Teutonic</a>-looking man and we knew that he went to <a href="/wiki/Sinn_Fein" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinn Fein">Sinn Fein</a> meetings, but just who he pretended to be we never knew."  Mike Vanderbilt, who rented a mansion with Koehler in Queenstown, watched as he "left the table and a few moments later, I saw him sneaking out the front door in civilian clothes, made up to be ever more Germanic than usual."  Sinn Féin meetings were off limits to U.S. personnel.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Koehler spent considerable energy and time to study "both sides of the Irish question."  He concluded, "But my sympathies are wavering and are now all for the British... But how to satisfy a people split into <a href="/wiki/Political_faction" title="Political faction">factions</a>, each faction demanding something different.... The <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Home Rule">Home Rule</a> has its stronghold in the South and is solidly <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>.  The Ulsterites or <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">North Irish party</a>, almost as solidly <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a>, are opposed to Home Rule for the simple reason they realize the minute the South Irish party takes charge, all industries of Ireland—about 95% of which are in the north—will shortly be taxed out of existence.... I have never yet been able to discover just what the Sinn Feiners want, nor has anybody else.... Taxes here are much less than in England. Irish <a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per capita</a> representation in Parliament is greater than that of any other country in England.  Food is plentiful.  There are no food restrictions and the English supply them with more food than they give their own people. And yet they have a hymn of hate for the British no less venomous than the German hymn... The great point of difference between the English and the Irish is after all the English have no sense of humor and the Irish have the keenest sense of humor in the world. It is this difference that makes it impossible for Englishmen to understand Irishmen, and it is an <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axiom</a> that one must first understand those whom one would govern....  One of the most dangerous symptoms lies in the fact that the whole Sinn Féin movement is tinged with the idea of <a href="/wiki/Martyrdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Martyrdom">martyrdom</a>, and it seems to derive considerable strength just from the fact of its hopelessness.  The only parallel I have ever seen was in the Southern Philippines, where native priests told the <a href="/wiki/Moros" title="Moros">Moros</a> that the greatest good that could come of them would be to kill a <a href="/wiki/Pagan" class="mw-redirect" title="Pagan">heathen</a> American and be shot down in turn, for only then would the gods transport them to <a href="/wiki/Seven_Heavens" title="Seven Heavens">Seventh Heaven</a>.  Irishmen are rapidly getting the same sort of <a href="/wiki/Fanaticism" title="Fanaticism">fanaticism</a> and openly declare that they neither fear nor would they regret being shot down for their cause, since every Irishman shot down by the police or soldiery means a distinct gain for Ireland, because 20 men will be inspired by the example of the one who has fallen.... So hurrah for Ireland and the Irish, say I! It's the devil of a country to live in or work in but a fine place to play in. And the Irish? I love them as friends and playmates, but oh! how I'd hate to have them as compatriots or relations."<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Writing to his mother, Koehler described as "murder" the roughness of patrols on the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a>, the constant smell of <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> and twenty-four hours of "drifting patrol", without engines so as to avoid detection by German <a href="/wiki/Submarines" class="mw-redirect" title="Submarines">submarines</a>. The subchasers could determine the direction of a submarine, but not the distance, so they operated in a line of three, each independently determining the direction, with the three lines then plotted on a chart. The point of intersection was the location of the sub, which would have moved on by then, necessitating a new <a href="/wiki/Trilateration" class="mw-redirect" title="Trilateration">trilateration</a> when they reached the last known point, and in that manner "like a hare and hound chase" the subchasers worked in unison with patrol aircraft launched from four airfields on the Irish Coast, that transmitted the location as soon as a <a href="/wiki/Wake" title="Wake">wake</a> or sub was sighted.  "This cooperation of aircraft with subchasers multiplied the usefulness of both, as subchasers became the ears for the aircraft and aircraft became the eyes for the subchasers," Koehler observed.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Navy_Cross" title="Navy Cross">Navy Cross</a> in 1920, for his World War I duty, with the following citation: "The Navy Cross is presented to Hugo W. Koehler, Lieutenant Commander, U.S. Navy, for distinguished services in the line of his profession for duty in connection with preparation of submarine chasers for duty in the war zone and subsequently their operation in the Irish Sea and off the coast of Ireland."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post_Armistice_England">Post Armistice England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Post Armistice England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lord_Hugh_and_Lady_Dorothy_Gladstone_wedding_photos_1901.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/Lord_Hugh_and_Lady_Dorothy_Gladstone_wedding_photos_1901.jpg/300px-Lord_Hugh_and_Lady_Dorothy_Gladstone_wedding_photos_1901.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/Lord_Hugh_and_Lady_Dorothy_Gladstone_wedding_photos_1901.jpg/450px-Lord_Hugh_and_Lady_Dorothy_Gladstone_wedding_photos_1901.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/Lord_Hugh_and_Lady_Dorothy_Gladstone_wedding_photos_1901.jpg/600px-Lord_Hugh_and_Lady_Dorothy_Gladstone_wedding_photos_1901.jpg 2x" data-file-width="887" data-file-height="597" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lord_Hugh_and_Lady_Dorothy_Gladstone_wedding_photos_1901.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lord <a href="/wiki/Herbert_John_Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbert John Gladstone">Herbert John Gladstone</a> and Lady Dorothy (Dolly) Paget Gladstone, wedding photos, 1901</div></div></div><p> After the <a href="/wiki/Armistice" title="Armistice">Armistice</a> was signed on November 11, 1918, Koehler was detached to the staff of Admiral <a href="/wiki/William_S._Sims" class="mw-redirect" title="William S. Sims">William S. Sims</a>, commanding U.S. Naval forces in Europe, with <a href="/wiki/Headquarters" title="Headquarters">headquarters</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>.  The two months that Koehler spent on Sims' staff were far removed from the rough seas and fumes of the subchasers.  "I have landed among the very smart and fashionable and the diplomatic set," he wrote his mother, "many of whom I already knew officially: and on the other hand through <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Scott" title="Kathleen Scott">Lady Scott</a> (wife of Captain <a href="/wiki/Robert_Falcon_Scott" title="Robert Falcon Scott">Scott</a> who discovered the <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">South Pole</a>), whom I have known a long time, I see a great deal of men like <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">Bernard Shaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Bennett" title="Arnold Bennett">Arnold Bennett</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Barrie" class="mw-redirect" title="James Barrie">James Barrie</a>, so I don't get too narrow in my ideas about things English.  Mike Vanderbilt's sister, the <a href="/wiki/Consuelo_Vanderbilt" title="Consuelo Vanderbilt">Duchess of Marlborough</a>, has been chaperoning me very carefully and on two occasions has had me all but married without consulting either me or the lucky girl! Rumors of this may reach you, but you are not to take them seriously. Of course, I always take all lovely women seriously--"<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>  Koehler spent <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a> 1918 at the <a href="/wiki/Baronial" class="mw-redirect" title="Baronial">baronial</a>, English manor "Cranmore", as the guest of Lady Muriel Paget with a party of thirty-seven "diverse personalities" including <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a>, former, <a href="/wiki/Conservative" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative">conservative</a> <a href="/wiki/British_Prime_Minister" class="mw-redirect" title="British Prime Minister">British Prime Minister</a>, Barrie, author of <i><a href="/wiki/Peter_Pan" title="Peter Pan">Peter Pan</a></i> and Lord <a href="/wiki/Herbert_John_Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbert John Gladstone">Herbert John Gladstone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Europe" title="Liberalism in Europe">liberal</a> British statesman, <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament">member of Parliament</a> and first <a href="/wiki/Governor_general" class="mw-redirect" title="Governor general">governor general</a> and high commissioner for <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.  Gladstone, the son of "The Grand Old Man", Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="William Gladstone">William Gladstone</a>, remained a close-friend of Koehler for fifteen years, and circumspectly countenanced an ongoing affair of similar duration between his much younger wife, Dolly, and Koehler.  Over the years of their friendship that lasted until Gladstone's death, his salutations evolved from, "My dear Commander", to "My dear Hugo", to "My dear Old Fellow", to "Dear Old Boy".<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>  In a letter to Mathilda, Koehler described the festivities, "One evening at dinner, James Barrie was told to construct the most wonderful play he had ever written.  He did so the following morning; in the afternoon the play was rehearsed, and in the evening it was produced in seven acts, a tremendous success. It was wonderfully clever, for most of the actors had simply to act their own characters.  Mr. Balfour took the part of a Foreign Minister; Lord Gladstone took the part of a <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">Colonial</a> Governor (he had just been <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_South_Africa" title="Governor-General of South Africa">Governor of South Africa)</a> and I was a dashing (sic!) young naval officer. The result was a screaming farce.  We were there five days and were idle not a solitary moment. Almost every day we had a football game in which everybody took part, men, women and children, and indeed it was a lesson in poise to see the splendid way in which the most dignified old statesmen and haughty <a href="/wiki/Dowager" title="Dowager">dowagers</a> dashed about and scrambled in the mud."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:382px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Siberechts-ViewovLongleat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Siberechts-ViewovLongleat.jpg/380px-Siberechts-ViewovLongleat.jpg" decoding="async" width="380" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Siberechts-ViewovLongleat.jpg/570px-Siberechts-ViewovLongleat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Siberechts-ViewovLongleat.jpg/760px-Siberechts-ViewovLongleat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1956" data-file-height="1281" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Siberechts-ViewovLongleat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>A View of <a href="/wiki/Longleat" title="Longleat">Longleat</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Jan_Siberechts" title="Jan Siberechts">Jan Siberechts</a>, 1675</div></div></div><p>The day after Christmas, Koehler returned to Cranmore from a <a href="/wiki/Partridge" title="Partridge">partridge</a> <a href="/wiki/Shooting" title="Shooting">shoot</a> and was captivated by the dinner guests seated on either side of him, the daughters of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Thynne,_5th_Marquess_of_Bath" title="Thomas Thynne, 5th Marquess of Bath">Thomas Thynne, 5th Marquess of Bath</a>.  Recognizing that "two beautiful women at the same time are always a difficulty", Koehler "at once" made the "dreadful decision" and between Lady Emma Margery Thynne (1893–1980) and Lady Alice Kathleen Violet Thynne (1891–1977), chose Emma as the object of his pursuit.  The following day, Koehler, was scheduled to go to <a href="/wiki/Maiden_Bradley" title="Maiden Bradley">Maiden Bradley</a> to hunt with Sir <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_16th_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 16th Duke of Somerset">Edward Seymour, 16th Duke of Somerset</a>.  He devised a scheme with the cooperation of his hostess, Lady Paget.  Returning from the hunting trip, they would have a tire "puncture" in front of <a href="/wiki/Longleat" title="Longleat">Longleat</a>, the ancestral home of the Marquess of Bath. The enterprising Koehler would present himself at the door and further sojourn with Emma would be his.  Koehler showed Balfour and the duke the note of his plan he had scribbled to Lady Paget. They "roared" and asked Koehler what he was going to do about it. "Get them of course", was the response to more laughter.  The duke and Koehler made a wager, his gun for the duke's <a href="/wiki/Greatcoat" title="Greatcoat">greatcoat</a>, that Koehler would not succeed.  Koehler executed his plan, and "lifting the enormous knocker, [to] beat a bold tattoo on a door about the size of the gates of <a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St. Peter&#39;s Basilica">St. Peter's</a>," he was ushered into Longleat.  Only then did he discover that while he was hunting, Emma had sent word to Cranmore inviting him to spend the weekend at Longleat, and so his suitcase had been packed and put in his car unbeknownst to him. "At any rate I have the duke's greatcoat. Since then all my weekends save one, when I went back to the old duke, have been spent at Longleat".<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lady_Emma_Margery_Thynne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Lady_Emma_Margery_Thynne.jpg/220px-Lady_Emma_Margery_Thynne.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Lady_Emma_Margery_Thynne.jpg/330px-Lady_Emma_Margery_Thynne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/1b/Lady_Emma_Margery_Thynne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="371" data-file-height="433" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lady_Emma_Margery_Thynne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lady Emma Margery Thynne</div></div></div><p>While he lost Emma to <a href="/wiki/William_Compton,_6th_Marquess_of_Northampton" title="William Compton, 6th Marquess of Northampton">William Compton, 6th Marquess of Northampton</a>, who married her in 1921, Koehler reflected in a letter to his mother dated February 5, 1919, while embarked on <a href="/wiki/HMS_Comus_(1914)" title="HMS Comus (1914)">HMS&#160;<i>Comus</i></a> for the northern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> <a href="/wiki/Anchorage" class="mw-redirect" title="Anchorage">anchorage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scapa_Flow" title="Scapa Flow">Scapa Flow</a>, "I shall grieve as much as any Englishman when the old order changes, for I cannot help thinking that when all the land is cut up into small farms and the great estates pass out of existence, England will lose a lot more than she will gain by any improvement that may come from men working their own farms instead of being tenants. For instance, Longleat comprises some 68,000 acres.  Of course, that seems a tremendous big corner of a small country like England for one man to own. But no one will ever provide for that land and all the people on it as carefully as have the Thynnes for many generations.  It takes 68,000 acres to support it.  And those aristocrats of England, they deserve well of their country- they've poured out their blood and their treasure unsparingly in this war.  Take the Thynnes, for example. Every one of them did splendidly: The oldest son, Viscount Weymouth (2nd Lt. John Alexander Thynne, 1895–1916), went to France the first week of the war, distinguished himself, and was killed in action a week after he got the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Cross" title="Victoria Cross">Victoria Cross</a>.  And the girls saw their job and did it. It was necessary that women, who had never worked before, should go into factories. The quickest way to bring about so revolutionary a change was to make it fashionable. So these girls, with the proudest blood in England in their veins, promptly took jobs in a munition factory.  They stuck at it, and for long hours each day, for month upon month, they worked at their <a href="/wiki/Lathes" class="mw-redirect" title="Lathes">lathes</a> with a thousand other men and girls about them.  In the meantime, their mother, the Marchioness of Bath, converted her home into a hospital and ran it herself, having asked the government for nothing but medicines- she provided doctors and nurses and all else.  So bravo for them, I say! They saw their duty and did it."<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Koehler's commanding officer at Queenstown, Captain Hepburn attended the December 18, 1918 meeting that organized the Allied Naval Armistice Commission. When Hepburn returned to the U.S. in early 1919, Koehler was attached as <a href="/wiki/Translator" class="mw-redirect" title="Translator">translator</a> for Vice Admiral <a href="/wiki/Samuel_S._Robison" class="mw-redirect" title="Samuel S. Robison">Samuel S. Robison</a>, the ranking American representative on the commission.  For one month there were dinner parties every night and on weekends at Longleat, before the commission headed to <a href="/wiki/Scapa_Flow" title="Scapa Flow">Scapa Flow</a> in early February 1919, where nearly 80 ships of the <a href="/wiki/German_High_Seas_Fleet" class="mw-redirect" title="German High Seas Fleet">German High Seas Fleet</a> had been interned while awaiting disposition through peace treaty negotiations.  From Scapa Flow the commission headed to various German ports to assess the condition of German warships and merchantmen.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
