<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post_war_intelligence_gathering">Post war intelligence gathering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post war intelligence gathering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The most interesting period of Koehler's life was during the four years immediately following the First World War.  His naval assignments brought him to Germany, France, Russia and Poland. He had a front row seat during the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Civil_War" title="Russian Civil War">Russian Civil War</a>, as second-in-command to Rear Admiral <a href="/wiki/Newton_A._McCully" title="Newton A. McCully">Newton A. McCully</a>'s nine-man, Mission to South Russia for the U.S. State Department. Koehler was often in disguise and sometimes engaged in combat between the warring factions, that would doubtless have brought him the swift execution accorded a <a href="/wiki/Spy" class="mw-redirect" title="Spy">spy</a> if he had been captured. By embedding himself in the conflict, Koehler was able to report on the rapidly-changing developments with a unique perspective. He travelled in Crimea and the Ukraine, where he obtained an insightful understanding of the military and political conflicts and the dire economic conditions.  Koehler met many of the major military figures of both the <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_movement" title="White movement">White Russian</a> factions, including General <a href="/wiki/Anton_Denikin" title="Anton Denikin">Anton Denikin</a>, Lt. General <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Kutepov" title="Alexander Kutepov">Alexander Kutepov</a> and General (Baron) <a href="/wiki/Pyotr_Wrangel" title="Pyotr Wrangel">Pyotr Wrangel</a>, notably taking part in Wrangel's raid into <a href="/wiki/Taurida" class="mw-redirect" title="Taurida">Taurida</a>, where Koehler narrowly escaped capture at <a href="/wiki/Melitopol" title="Melitopol">Melitopol</a>. He was awarded three of the small number of Russian Imperial military decorations that were distributed during this time<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> and also recommended for award of the <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Service_Medal" title="Distinguished Service Medal">Distinguished Service Medal</a> by his commanding officer, Rear Admiral McCully.<sup id="cite_ref-Capelotti._pg._210,_fn._5_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capelotti._pg._210,_fn._5-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Allied_Naval_Armistice_Commission.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6f/Allied_Naval_Armistice_Commission.jpg/350px-Allied_Naval_Armistice_Commission.jpg" decoding="async" width="350" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6f/Allied_Naval_Armistice_Commission.jpg/525px-Allied_Naval_Armistice_Commission.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/6f/Allied_Naval_Armistice_Commission.jpg 2x" data-file-width="672" data-file-height="515" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Allied_Naval_Armistice_Commission.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Allied Naval Armistice Commission, aboard <a href="/wiki/HMS_Hercules" title="HMS Hercules">HMS&#160;<i>Hercules</i></a>. L to R, Vice Admiral Maurice F.A. Grasset, Vice Admiral <a href="/wiki/Samuel_S._Robison" class="mw-redirect" title="Samuel S. Robison">Samuel S. Robison</a>, U.S.N, <a href="/wiki/Montague_Browning" title="Montague Browning">Vice Admiral Sir Montague E. Browning</a> K.C.B, M.C.O., R.N, (unknown officer), Captain Ryozo Nakamura, I.J.N.</div></div></div><p>The Germany that Koehler observed in early 1919 was a defeated and bitter nation.  The losers of the killing fields and forests in France were poor and demoralized, beset by a vengeful France plundering from the west and <a href="/wiki/Bolshevism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevism">bolshevism</a> looming from the east.  Germans were outraged by what they saw as the "<a href="/wiki/Versailles" class="mw-redirect" title="Versailles">Versailles</a> <a href="/wiki/Diktat" title="Diktat">diktat</a>", as a German "Red Soldiers" League fought and defeated a <a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionary</a> <a href="/wiki/Freikorps" title="Freikorps">Freikorps</a> of ex-soldiers that for a time threatened a Bolshevik revolution in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Kiel_mutiny" title="Kiel mutiny">Kiel mutiny</a> of German sailors at the beginning of November 1918 had been the death knell of the German empire, bringing about the Armistice a couple weeks later.  Koehler's instincts were <a href="/wiki/Aristocratic" class="mw-redirect" title="Aristocratic">aristocratic</a> but his impulses were <a href="/wiki/Proletarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Proletarian">proletarian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Capelotti._pg._15_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capelotti._pg._15-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>  During his initial six week inspection tour of various German port cities and towns, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Wilhelmshaven" title="Wilhelmshaven">Wilhelmshaven</a> and on to <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bremen" title="Bremen">Bremen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kiel" title="Kiel">Kiel</a>, he observed conditions of both the German capital ships and the general population.  "We found the German ships all in a frightful state, both as regards cleanliness and preservation.  They had evidently been hastily put out of commission for there was no one on board... The ships that were in commission, as for example the new light cruiser <a href="/wiki/SMS_K%C3%B6nigsberg_(1915)" title="SMS Königsberg (1915)">Koenigsberg</a> were also in hopeless condition, although they had large crews on board... As this rabble came out on deck, where we were patiently waiting, the men crowded around us so it was necessary to ask the captain to have them withdraw sufficiently to allow us breathing space... The men moved slowly away and sulked and muttered. We also inspected the destroyers, submarines and the aircraft station.  Conditions were everywhere alike: everything unspeakably filthy, no work being done, everything going to rack and ruin..."<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In his report to Admiral Sims, Koehler wrote, "The one sure thing about the German navy is that it is finished-finished far more effectively than if every officer and man and ship had been sunk.  With the exception of U-Boat men, the navy and everyone in it is in disgrace. The U-Boat men were loyal throughout the whole revolution, and are loyal to the central government today, but even they appear ashamed of the navy for many of them wear soldier uniforms. Hardly anywhere does anyone see a sailor in uniform.  So thoroughly is the shame of the navy felt that the blue uniform is considered almost a badge of disgrace, and except for the uniforms of men of the few ships still in commission one never sees any blue, although the streets are crowded with men in the forest gray of the infantry."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>  At Wilhelmshaven, he put in "a good many hours" reading newspapers, handbills and political pamphlets distributed by the "Workmen's Council", the <a href="/wiki/Socialists" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialists">Socialists</a> and the civil government, with titles such as, "<a href="/wiki/Alfred_von_Tirpitz" title="Alfred von Tirpitz">Tirpitz</a> the Grave Digger of the German Navy". Koehler saw the outcries of the masses expressed in placards that read, "We have a right to something besides work." "We have a right to bread."  "Labor, which alone produces wealth, alone has the right to wealth." "No more profit."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:German_World_War_I_officer,_ca._1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/df/German_World_War_I_officer%2C_ca._1920.jpg/300px-German_World_War_I_officer%2C_ca._1920.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/df/German_World_War_I_officer%2C_ca._1920.jpg/450px-German_World_War_I_officer%2C_ca._1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/df/German_World_War_I_officer%2C_ca._1920.jpg/600px-German_World_War_I_officer%2C_ca._1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1011" data-file-height="703" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:German_World_War_I_officer,_ca._1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German WW I officer, ca. 1920</div></div></div><p>"I asked the people about the <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II,_German_Emperor" title="Wilhelm II, German Emperor">Kaiser (Wilhelm II)</a>, the sum of all their answers was that the Kaiser today, even if not openly popular, nevertheless has a greater hold on the respect and affections of the Germans as a whole than has any other in the Empire.  The crown prince is not popular. They say the Kaiser was badly advised, but that after all, no man in Germany has the interests of Germany so close at heart or worked so hard or tirelessly as did the Kaiser.  Another idea which is also very widespread is that the great <a href="/wiki/General_staff" class="mw-redirect" title="General staff">general staff</a> which he reared so carefully became a car of <a href="/wiki/Juggernaut" title="Juggernaut">Juggernaut</a> and rolled over him.  They all agree that he was weak about the use of gas. They say that he forbade the use of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_weapons_in_World_War_I" title="Chemical weapons in World War I">gas</a>, but was later overruled by the <a href="/wiki/Militarists" class="mw-redirect" title="Militarists">militarists</a>.  No one thinks that the use of gas was wrong, though quite a number think it was very foolish to start off on a small scale.  They argue that if the Kaiser had allowed the use of gas on a tremendous scale, as the general staff wanted to at the beginning, they would have won the war before the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">allies</a> could have provided themselves with gas masks.  It is also said that the kaiser opposed air raids on undefended towns in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, but was overruled by the general staff. I have heard a great many bitter criticisms of the German chemists who could not discover a nonflammable gas for <a href="/wiki/Zeppelins" class="mw-redirect" title="Zeppelins">Zeppelins</a>, while American chemists did."<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mobile_Soup_Kitchen_Germany_1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/37/Mobile_Soup_Kitchen_Germany_1920.jpg/300px-Mobile_Soup_Kitchen_Germany_1920.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/37/Mobile_Soup_Kitchen_Germany_1920.jpg/450px-Mobile_Soup_Kitchen_Germany_1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/37/Mobile_Soup_Kitchen_Germany_1920.jpg/600px-Mobile_Soup_Kitchen_Germany_1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="792" data-file-height="555" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mobile_Soup_Kitchen_Germany_1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mobile soup field kitchen in Germany ca. 1920</div></div></div><p>Koehler assessed the two needs of Germany in the few months following the Armistice: food and raw materials.  Food to keep her poor people from suffering and actual starvation within weeks, since they had no money to purchase food at <a href="/wiki/Profiteering_(business)" title="Profiteering (business)">profiteer</a> prices, and to keep her workmen out of <a href="/wiki/Bolshevism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevism">bolshevism</a>.  Raw materials were needed partly for the same reason, to block bolshevism and to help Germany to regain her place in the commercial world and also to repay the enormous war debt saddled on her by the Kaiser and his military advisors. "The Germans are not starved yet, but they are pretty hungry. One sees a good many double chins and paunches still, that is among the men. The women look really emaciated. They have suffered from the food shortage a great deal more than the men. The saddest thing, however; is the suffering among children.  They show it markedly. Infant mortality has been very high and still is, though not as bad as in the winter of 1916–17.  Meat sells at from 29 to 30 marks a pound, including the bone.  To illustrate the scarcity, during an attack of Spartacans in Wilhelmshaven, a horse in a passing cart was shot. Within 20 minutes after it fell, every shred of flesh had been stripped from its bones. This indicates that meat shortage even more plainly than the empty butcher shops do. Grocery shops appear fairly well stocked, but the prices are enormous. Practically everything is rationed, including clothing. Great quantities of potatoes and cabbages are on hand, and, counting the difference in exchange, they can be bought cheaper here than in England.  Practically no vegetables of any other kind are on hand.  No tinned vegetables are obtainable in any of the shops.  Fats do not exist. There is an almost total absence of soap.  They have a substitute which is said to be very poor. The bread is bad.  It is made of wurzel meal, as potatoes cannot be spared for meal. There is very little milk in the country."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Germany's solution to its economic woes would be a massive emigration to the United States that would both create a new market for Germany to revitalize its industrial capacity and facilitate <a href="/wiki/Remittances" class="mw-redirect" title="Remittances">remittances</a> back to Germany as the new immigrants prospered, Koehler believed.  Receiving Koehler's report, Admiral Sims replied, "My opinion of the value and the interest of this letter is such that... I have had it mimeographed for circulation among our forces here... I am also sending copies to the O.N.I. (Office of Naval Intelligence) and to certain officers....I should be glad if, as opportunity provides, you would continue similar observations and send them in to headquarters."<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Koehler's assessment of conditions in Germany were reprinted in American newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_light_cruiser_FRANKFURT_(1915-1921),_left,_and_the_battleship_BADEN_(1915-1921),_aground_in_Swanbister_Bay,_Scapa_Flow,_sometime_soon_after_the_majority_of_the_interned_German_battle_fleet_was_scuttled_on_21_June_1919.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/The_light_cruiser_FRANKFURT_%281915-1921%29%2C_left%2C_and_the_battleship_BADEN_%281915-1921%29%2C_aground_in_Swanbister_Bay%2C_Scapa_Flow%2C_sometime_soon_after_the_majority_of_the_interned_German_battle_fleet_was_scuttled_on_21_June_1919.jpg/300px-thumbnail.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/The_light_cruiser_FRANKFURT_%281915-1921%29%2C_left%2C_and_the_battleship_BADEN_%281915-1921%29%2C_aground_in_Swanbister_Bay%2C_Scapa_Flow%2C_sometime_soon_after_the_majority_of_the_interned_German_battle_fleet_was_scuttled_on_21_June_1919.jpg/450px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/The_light_cruiser_FRANKFURT_%281915-1921%29%2C_left%2C_and_the_battleship_BADEN_%281915-1921%29%2C_aground_in_Swanbister_Bay%2C_Scapa_Flow%2C_sometime_soon_after_the_majority_of_the_interned_German_battle_fleet_was_scuttled_on_21_June_1919.jpg/600px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1233" data-file-height="837" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_light_cruiser_FRANKFURT_(1915-1921),_left,_and_the_battleship_BADEN_(1915-1921),_aground_in_Swanbister_Bay,_Scapa_Flow,_sometime_soon_after_the_majority_of_the_interned_German_battle_fleet_was_scuttled_on_21_June_1919.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The light cruiser <a href="/wiki/SMS_Frankfurt" title="SMS Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>, left, and the battleship <a href="/wiki/SMS_Baden" title="SMS Baden">Baden</a>, aground in Swanbister Bay, <a href="/wiki/Scapa_Flow" title="Scapa Flow">Scapa Flow</a>, sometime soon after the majority of the interned German battle fleet was scuttled on June 21, 1919</div></div></div><p>Koehler made a follow-up inspection and report from Wilhelmshaven and Kiel on June 16, reporting that "The whole harbor of Kiel was filled with decaying men-of-war.  It is almost impossible to conceive how complete is the ruin that comes to men-of-war in just a few months of neglect, for these splendid ships of only six months ago are even now almost beyond recall..." Less than a week after Koehler wrote his report, the German navy in defiance of the terms of the Peace "diktat", <a href="/wiki/Scuttling_of_the_German_fleet_in_Scapa_Flow" class="mw-redirect" title="Scuttling of the German fleet in Scapa Flow">scuttled most of its ships interned at Scapa Flow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Reporting from Scapa Flow a week later, Koehler wrote, "All Germany and particularly naval officers are jubilant about the sinking of the German ships at Scapa Flow... Everywhere in Germany I heard the cry against the clause in the peace terms that provides for the trial of the Kaiser and others responsible for the war..."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Koehler saw that over-reaching by the other allies, particularly France, would be problematic for the next generation. "In those questions on which the United States has taken a different stand than the Allies, Germans attribute it to a latent friendship for Germany- a friendship somewhat disturbed these last years but that still exists. It is difficult to point out that the attitude of the U.S. is not that of favoring Germany but simply the earnest desire to do the right thing and bring about a peace that does not contain in the very peace terms the germs of another war.  That the U.S. took none of the surrendered <a href="/wiki/U-boats" class="mw-redirect" title="U-boats">U-boats</a> is well known in Germany and considered a good omen that the U.S. has no desire to take anything from Germany.  Extreme bitterness toward the English seems to be lost in their greater hatred of France. They say France has only one idea of peace- to ruin Germany utterly- and the only thing that can keep France from doing this is the British sense of fair play and this latent friendship from America....A restaurant manager asked the same question that greeted us on all sides:  He was surprised when I mentioned it seemed likely the places to which Americans would flock after peace had been declared were the battlefields at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ypres" title="Battle of Ypres">Ypres</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">Somme</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau-Thierry" title="Château-Thierry">Château-Thierry</a>, and not the spas of Germany...."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First_American_Officer_in_Berlin">First American Officer in Berlin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: First American Officer in Berlin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1919.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7f/Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1919.jpg/225px-Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1919.jpg" decoding="async" width="225" height="294" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/7f/Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1919.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="314" data-file-height="410" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1919.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hugo W. Koehler's 1919 US Passport photo</div></div></div><p>While newspapers reported that Hugo Koehler was the first American naval officer to enter Germany following the Armistice,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Koehler did not enter Germany until February 1919.  He does have the distinction of being the first American officer to enter <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> following the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a> on June 28, 1919, officially ending the war.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The Allied Naval Armistice Commission was at Hamburg embarked on the British cruiser, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Coventry_(D43)" title="HMS Coventry (D43)">HMS&#160;<i>Coventry</i></a>. The city was under <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a> and none of the British officers were interested in going ashore, unlike Koehler and <a href="/wiki/Chief_petty_officer" title="Chief petty officer">Chief</a> <a href="/wiki/Yeoman" title="Yeoman">Yeoman</a> Walter Dring, USN (1894–1984).  Despite warnings of the danger, and armed with a notebook and pencils, Koehler and Dring sauntered down the gangplank and strode to the <a href="/wiki/Hamburg-American_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Hamburg-American Line">Hamburg-American Line</a> pier.  Pushing through a crowd that was jostling for sugar and cigarettes, the pair were headed towards the main street, when two German military police on a <a href="/wiki/Sidecar" title="Sidecar">sidecar</a> motorcycle stopped and picked them up for questioning. Brought to German naval headquarters, they were interrogated by Admirals <a href="/wiki/Reinhard_Scheer" title="Reinhard Scheer">Scheer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franz_von_Hipper" title="Franz von Hipper">Von Hipper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_von_Reuter" title="Ludwig von Reuter">Von Reuter</a>.  Koehler explained that <i>Coventry</i> was the first inspection ship to arrive at Hamburg following the treaty and that the British officers preferred to remain on board.  The Germans were greatly amused that the British were reluctant to come ashore and gave the American naval spy and his <a href="/wiki/Scribe" title="Scribe">scribe</a> permission to travel to Berlin by night train, provided they remained confined to their compartment.  At 7 a.m. the following morning, Koehler and Dring arrived as the first American military to enter Berlin. They flipped a coin to see who would be first off the train, but "both landed on the platform at the same time in a heap".  Koehler hailed a cab, an emaciated horse drawing a cart with bare, iron wheels, and directed the driver to the <a href="/wiki/Adlon_Hotel" class="mw-redirect" title="Adlon Hotel">Adlon Hotel</a>, where he reminisced with the owner, <a href="/wiki/Lorenz_Adlon" title="Lorenz Adlon">Lorenz Adlon</a> (1849–1921) about meeting him in Berlin 25 years earlier with his grandfather, and gave the man two chocolate bars, soap and cigarettes.  After breakfast, the duo's sightseeing was interrupted by three policemen who escorted them back to the hotel.  With no papers other than their orders from London, they were directed to take the 6 o'clock train out of Berlin. But Koehler had other plans, and instead of going back to Hamburg and HMS <i>Coventry</i>, he took Dring to the best hotel in <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">Düsseldorf</a>.  From there they boarded a train to <a href="/wiki/Hanover" title="Hanover">Hanover</a>, then on to <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, where Koehler sought out British officers of the <a href="/wiki/British_Army_of_the_Rhine" title="British Army of the Rhine">Army of Occupation</a> who promptly locked them up as spies.  "Hugo looked like the Kaiser with that damn little mustache," complained Dring.  When they were released, they took a train to <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a> and then on to Paris, where Koehler told Dring that he was "going to duck for three or four hours before reporting to the <a href="/wiki/Embassy" class="mw-redirect" title="Embassy">embassy</a>."  Left to face the <a href="/wiki/Red_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Cross">Red Cross</a> alone and standing in line for breakfast with other servicemen, Dring was questioned and declared that he had just come from Germany.  Red Cross officials disbelieved his story and the <a href="/wiki/Military_police" title="Military police">MP</a>'s believed he was a spy, so Dring was again arrested and continued to go hungry.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_and_North_Russian_Operations">Rear Admiral Newton McCully and North Russian Operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Rear Admiral Newton McCully and North Russian Operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_circa_1921.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/16/Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_circa_1921.jpg/250px-Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_circa_1921.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/16/Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_circa_1921.jpg/375px-Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_circa_1921.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/16/Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_circa_1921.jpg/500px-Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_circa_1921.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2657" data-file-height="2983" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_circa_1921.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>R. Adm. <a href="/wiki/Newton_A._McCully" title="Newton A. McCully">Newton A. McCully</a>, ca. 1921</div></div></div><p>
When Dring finally got to the American embassy in Paris, officials asked where Koehler was.  The roving commander showed up a few days later and wrote his follow-up report on conditions inside Germany. On July 7, the adventure was over and Dring was ordered back to London.  Before he left, he introduced Koehler to Rear Admiral <a href="/wiki/Newton_McCully" class="mw-redirect" title="Newton McCully">McCully</a>, who had recently arrived from <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allied</a> operations in North Russia.  In Paris, Koehler was assigned as aide to McCully, the senior Navy member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Commission_to_Negotiate_Peace" title="American Commission to Negotiate Peace">American Commission to Negotiate Peace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> As a lieutenant commander in 1904, McCully had been a military observer embedded with the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Russian_Army" title="Imperial Russian Army">Imperial Russian Army</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a>, arriving at the front lines in <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Siberian_Railway" title="Trans-Siberian Railway">Trans-Siberian Railway</a>. Returning to the United States in 1906, McCully had submitted a lengthy report on his findings.<sup id="cite_ref-Kowner_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kowner-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In 1914, McCully was assigned as <a href="/wiki/Naval_attach%C3%A9" class="mw-redirect" title="Naval attaché">naval attaché</a> at St. Petersburg. By 1916, fluent in Russian and knowledgeable of the fluid political, military and social conditions there, he warned the State Department that food shortages, official corruption and a demoralized populus might soon force Russia out of World War One. In 1917 he witnessed the beginnings of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a>. Shortly after this, he was ordered back to sea duty in the Atlantic and promoted to rear admiral in September 1918. Less than a month before the Armistice, McCully was designated as Commander, U.S. Naval Forces in Northern Russia, and read his orders aboard the <a href="/wiki/USS_Olympia_(C-6)" title="USS Olympia (C-6)">USS&#160;<i>Olympia</i></a> on October 24, 1918.  With <i>Olympia's</i> departure for Scotland on November 8, McCully and a small contingent of officers and <a href="/wiki/Sailor" title="Sailor">bluejackets</a> were the American naval presence for the <a href="/wiki/Allied_intervention_in_the_Russian_Civil_War" title="Allied intervention in the Russian Civil War">Allied intervention in the Russian Civil War</a>, the struggle between the <a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolsheviks</a> and those who opposed them (often known as the <a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">"Reds"</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_movement" title="White movement">"Whites"</a>, respectively).<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>  The 8,000 American troops of the <a href="/wiki/American_Expeditionary_Force_Siberia" class="mw-redirect" title="American Expeditionary Force Siberia">American Expeditionary Force Siberia</a> sent by President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> to <a href="/wiki/Vladivostok" title="Vladivostok">Vladivostok</a> to guard the billion-dollar investment of American guns and equipment along the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Siberian_railroad" class="mw-redirect" title="Trans-Siberian railroad">Trans-Siberian railroad</a> and protect the <a href="/wiki/Czech_legion" class="mw-redirect" title="Czech legion">Czech legion</a> tried to remain in a defensive posture.  A larger force of British "<a href="/wiki/Tommy_Atkins" title="Tommy Atkins">Tommies</a>", freezing alongside the Czechs, fought among themselves as much as against the Bolsheviki.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The separate 5,000 troop <a href="/wiki/Polar_Bear_Expedition" class="mw-redirect" title="Polar Bear Expedition">Polar Bear Expedition</a> was sent to <a href="/wiki/Arkhangelsk" title="Arkhangelsk">Archangel</a> to guard the <a href="/wiki/Murman_Railway" class="mw-redirect" title="Murman Railway">Murman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Railway_(Russia)" title="Northern Railway (Russia)">Archangel-Vologda railways</a> and Allied supply stockpiles in the vicinity.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> American military action was deemed admissible only to assist the Czechs in defending themselves against armed Austrian and German prisoners that were attacking them and to steady efforts at self- government by the Russians. American policy was stated on August 3, 1918, the day after the Allied occupation of Archangel, "Whether from Vladivostok or from Murmansk and Archangel, the only present object for which American troops will be employed will be to guard military stores which may subsequently be needed by Russian forces and to render such aid as may be acceptable to the Russians in the organization of their self-defense." No interference with Russian political sovereignty or intervention in her internal affairs was planned.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Despite this directive, during their 19 months in Siberia, 189 soldiers of the American Expeditionary Force Siberia died from all causes, including combat.  The smaller American North Russia Expeditionary Force experienced 235 deaths from all causes during their 9 months imbedded during fighting near Archangel.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>  Through March 1919, McCully's men primarily operated ashore from <a href="/wiki/Murmansk" title="Murmansk">Murmansk</a> and engaged in intelligence gathering and reporting to Admiral Sims in London on the political, financial, military, naval, and economic conditions.  McCully reported that the Whites and Allied forces controlled about two-thirds of <a href="/wiki/Archangel_Oblast" class="mw-redirect" title="Archangel Oblast">Archangel province</a>, south to <a href="/wiki/Murmansk" title="Murmansk">Murmansk</a> and west to the border between <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karelia" title="Karelia">Karelia</a>. Concerning naval conditions there was little to report, for there had been no naval operations, the Bolsheviks not having a deep-water naval presence in North Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> With his familiarity of Russia and its language, unquestionably, McCully was the Navy's top "Russia man".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In a report to Admiral Sims in late February, McCully reported that the military situation in the Archangel region was precarious and that Allied forces along both the Archangel-Vologda railway and the Murman railway were insufficient. He urged stationing vessels at both Archangel and Murmansk and providing another to cruise along the 1,600 mile coast and visit other ports. During May through June, the patrol gunboat <a href="/wiki/USS_Sacramento_(PG-19)" title="USS Sacramento (PG-19)">USS&#160;<i>Sacramento</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(PG-19)</span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Protected_cruiser" title="Protected cruiser">protected cruiser</a> <a href="/wiki/USS_Des_Moines_(CL-17)" title="USS Des Moines (CL-17)">USS&#160;<i>Des Moines</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(CL-17)</span></a> and three <a href="/wiki/Eagle-class_patrol_craft" title="Eagle-class patrol craft"><i>Eagle</i>-class patrol boats</a> were detached to McCully, to supplement the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish American War">Spanish American War</a>-era, steam-schooner <a href="/wiki/USS_Yankton_(1893)" title="USS Yankton (1893)">USS&#160;<i>Yankton</i></a> that he had taken as his <a href="/wiki/Flagship" title="Flagship">flagship</a> in February.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> By order of the Secretary of the Navy dated June 30, two days after the signing of the peace treaty at Versailles, McCully was detached from his command in Northern Russia and directed to proceed to London.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>  Following the withdrawal of U.S. ground forces from Archangel and vicinity in July, with a backdrop of deteriorating morale and American public opinion, the last remaining U.S. naval vessel, <i>Des Moines</i> stood out from Archangel on September 14, 1919, and steamed down the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Dvina_River" title="Northern Dvina River">Northern Dvina River</a> for <a href="/wiki/Harwich,_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Harwich, England">Harwich, England</a>, marking the conclusion of American naval operations in North Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup></p><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 257.33333333333px"><div style="width: 257.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 255.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Memorial_at_US_Army_and_Navy_cemetery_at_Archangel_Russia_1919.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0f/Memorial_at_US_Army_and_Navy_cemetery_at_Archangel_Russia_1919.jpg/383px-Memorial_at_US_Army_and_Navy_cemetery_at_Archangel_Russia_1919.jpg" decoding="async" width="256" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0f/Memorial_at_US_Army_and_Navy_cemetery_at_Archangel_Russia_1919.jpg/576px-Memorial_at_US_Army_and_Navy_cemetery_at_Archangel_Russia_1919.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0f/Memorial_at_US_Army_and_Navy_cemetery_at_Archangel_Russia_1919.jpg/766px-Memorial_at_US_Army_and_Navy_cemetery_at_Archangel_Russia_1919.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1069" data-file-height="628" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Memorial at US Army and Navy cemetery at Archangel, Russia, May 30, 1919
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span id="With_McCully.27s_.22Mission_to_South_Russia.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="With_McCully's_&quot;Mission_to_South_Russia&quot;">With McCully's "Mission to South Russia"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: With McCully&#039;s &quot;Mission to South Russia&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1920.jpg/250px-Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1920.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="312" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1920.jpg/375px-Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/60/Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="431" data-file-height="538" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hugo Koehler in 1920 under orders to the U.S. State Dept. Mission to South Russia</div></div></div><p>On August 6, Koehler was sent on another fact-finding survey in Germany, where he inspected floating <a href="/wiki/Dry_docks" class="mw-redirect" title="Dry docks">dry docks</a>. Before returning to Paris, he was ordered to London to brief <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> at the <a href="/wiki/War_Ministry" class="mw-redirect" title="War Ministry">War Ministry</a> on conditions inside Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>  With the exception of Churchill, the allies had been reluctant to enter the Russian morass.  Foreign intervention in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Civil_War" title="Russian Civil War">Russian Civil War</a> would not have proceeded without Churchill's persistence. As he warned the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons" title="House of Commons">House of Commons</a> in March 1919, "<a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolsheviks</a> destroy wherever they exist but by rolling forward into fertile areas, like the vampire which sucks the blood from its victim, they gain means of prolonging their own baleful existence."<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In the spring of 1919, the army of Admiral <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Kolchak" title="Alexander Kolchak">Alexander Kolchak</a>, organizer of the <a href="/wiki/White_movement" title="White movement">"White"</a> resistance in <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> had advanced to the <a href="/wiki/Volga_River" title="Volga River">Volga River</a>. The forces of Russian general <a href="/wiki/Yevgeny_Miller" title="Yevgeny Miller">Yevgeny Miller</a> were fighting the <a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">"Reds"</a> as far as <a href="/wiki/Petrograd" class="mw-redirect" title="Petrograd">Petrograd</a>. From the west, a small army under General <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Yudenich" title="Nikolai Yudenich">Nikolai Yudenich</a> had also advanced on Petrograd, while in South Russia, the forces of General <a href="/wiki/Anton_Denikin" title="Anton Denikin">Anton Denikin</a> had begun to advance on <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>.  But by the summer, the advances of all the various White factions, and their fortunes, reversed almost simultaneously, except for those in South Russia that continued to advance into autumn. Seizing the opportunity to fill the void of the departed German army, newly independent <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> occupied parts of <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Eastern_Europe)" title="Galicia (Eastern Europe)">Galicia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruthenia" title="Ruthenia">Ruthenia</a>, before advancing into western <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Nestor_Makhno" title="Nestor Makhno">Nestor Makhno</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Anarchist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anarchist">anarchist</a> with a band of militarist peasants was attacking both Red and White troops. With the governments of Britain and America yielding to public pressure at home and withdrawing their troops from Archangel, Murmansk and <a href="/wiki/Olonets" title="Olonets">Olonets</a>, Miller's troops were left to face the Reds alone, and swift defeat. Yudenitch overreached his advance and the Reds repulsed his forces back to the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic states</a>, where they disbanded.  To the east, Kolchak's over-extended troops were trounced before they could reach the Volga and began a torturous retreat to Siberia, without supplies and forced to strip corpses for clothes and shoes.  </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Denikin1.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Denikin1.PNG/180px-Denikin1.PNG" decoding="async" width="180" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Denikin1.PNG/270px-Denikin1.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Denikin1.PNG/360px-Denikin1.PNG 2x" data-file-width="445" data-file-height="693" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Denikin1.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General Anton Denikin</div></div></div><p>Denikin reached within 200 miles of <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, and filled with <a href="/wiki/Hubris" title="Hubris">hubris</a>, debated which horse he would triumphantly ride into the city, but over-extended and with broken supply lines, in October, the Reds pummelled his forces at <a href="/wiki/Oryol" title="Oryol">Oryol</a> and forced him to retreat with his disintegrating army. </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Occupation_of_Kharkov_by_Denikin%27s_White_army,_ca._1918.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fc/Occupation_of_Kharkov_by_Denikin%27s_White_army%2C_ca._1918.jpg/300px-Occupation_of_Kharkov_by_Denikin%27s_White_army%2C_ca._1918.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fc/Occupation_of_Kharkov_by_Denikin%27s_White_army%2C_ca._1918.jpg/450px-Occupation_of_Kharkov_by_Denikin%27s_White_army%2C_ca._1918.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fc/Occupation_of_Kharkov_by_Denikin%27s_White_army%2C_ca._1918.jpg/600px-Occupation_of_Kharkov_by_Denikin%27s_White_army%2C_ca._1918.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="630" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Occupation_of_Kharkov_by_Denikin%27s_White_army,_ca._1918.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Occupation of <a href="/wiki/Kharkov" class="mw-redirect" title="Kharkov">Kharkov</a> by Denikin's White Army, ca. 1918</div></div></div><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_Army_soldiers_march_to_Kharkov,_1919.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ca/Red_Army_soldiers_march_to_Kharkov%2C_1919.jpg/300px-Red_Army_soldiers_march_to_Kharkov%2C_1919.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ca/Red_Army_soldiers_march_to_Kharkov%2C_1919.jpg/450px-Red_Army_soldiers_march_to_Kharkov%2C_1919.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ca/Red_Army_soldiers_march_to_Kharkov%2C_1919.jpg/600px-Red_Army_soldiers_march_to_Kharkov%2C_1919.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="510" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_Army_soldiers_march_to_Kharkov,_1919.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Red Army infantry marching to <a href="/wiki/Kharkov" class="mw-redirect" title="Kharkov">Kharkov</a>, 1919</div></div></div><p>Driven southward, his troops surrendered <a href="/wiki/Kharkov" class="mw-redirect" title="Kharkov">Kharkov</a> to the Bolsheviks on December 13, 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> By the end of that year, the struggle between the Bolsheviks and the Whites had driven thousands of retreating White Army troops and civilian refugees to the northern shore of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and into the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Crimean Peninsula">Crimean Peninsula</a>, where they could expect no mercy from the Bolsheviks.  The Peace Commission concluded on December 10, and while the other American staff members returned to the United States, McCully was ordered to London as a representative on the Commission on Naval Terms.  At the suggestion of <a href="/wiki/Admiral" title="Admiral">Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/Mark_L._Bristol" class="mw-redirect" title="Mark L. Bristol">Mark L. Bristol</a>, Commander, Naval Forces, Turkish Peninsula, McCully was proposed to lead a special mission for the U.S. <a href="/wiki/State_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="State Department">State Department</a> with the purpose of keeping the government informed of developments in that region and to protect American lives and interests.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_Koehler%27s_December_1920_Emergency_Passport_Application_to_State_Dept._Mission_to_Russia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0e/Hugo_Koehler%27s_December_1920_Emergency_Passport_Application_to_State_Dept._Mission_to_Russia.jpg/250px-Hugo_Koehler%27s_December_1920_Emergency_Passport_Application_to_State_Dept._Mission_to_Russia.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0e/Hugo_Koehler%27s_December_1920_Emergency_Passport_Application_to_State_Dept._Mission_to_Russia.jpg/375px-Hugo_Koehler%27s_December_1920_Emergency_Passport_Application_to_State_Dept._Mission_to_Russia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0e/Hugo_Koehler%27s_December_1920_Emergency_Passport_Application_to_State_Dept._Mission_to_Russia.jpg/500px-Hugo_Koehler%27s_December_1920_Emergency_Passport_Application_to_State_Dept._Mission_to_Russia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1620" data-file-height="1284" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_Koehler%27s_December_1920_Emergency_Passport_Application_to_State_Dept._Mission_to_Russia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lt. Cdr. Hugo W. Koehler's Emergency Passport Application for State Dept. Mission to Russia, December 17, 1919. Rear Admiral Newton McCully's application and photo is to the left</div></div></div><p>On December 23, 1919, <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Lansing" title="Robert Lansing">Robert Lansing</a> cabled McCully designating him Special Agent for the Department of State and instructing him to proceed with a detachment including Koehler, who was then also fluent in Russian as his second-in-command, and nine other naval officers and enlisted men, "to the south of Russia with a view, first, to make observations and report to this Department upon political and economic conditions in the region visited, and second, to establish informal connection with General Denikin and his associates." ("South of Russia" meaning the area roughly encompassed by <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a>.) Taking a train from Paris to Italy, on New Year's Day 1920, Koehler and McCully sailed aboard the steamer <i>Karlsbad</i> bound for <a href="/wiki/Salonika" class="mw-redirect" title="Salonika">Salonika</a>, Greece.  Six days later they arrived at <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>.  En route through Greece, Koehler characteristically took the opportunity to engage the locals and analyze the economic and social conditions.  In a letter to his mother, he showed remarkable astuteness in commenting on the economic imbalance of the Greek economy that led to its <a href="/wiki/Greek_government-debt_crisis" title="Greek government-debt crisis">debt crisis</a> nearly a century later. "I talked to .... a professor at the University of Athens, [who] spoke at great length about Greek claims to <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a>.  I asked what these claims were and what they were based on. They were all based on historical reasons, he answered but was vague as to the exact historical reasons.... I commented that... what Greece needed was raisins rather than historical reasons-- raisins and olive oil to pay for her tremendous imports being paid for only by loans and paper... But apparently the Greeks believe that there is no need for such mundane things as raisins and olives while loans are plentiful and they can get flour and automobiles for paper money...."<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h4><span id="Odessa.2C_February_1920"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Odessa,_February_1920">Odessa, February 1920</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Odessa, February 1920">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:VADM_Newton_McCully_and_LT_Hamilton_V._Bryan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/VADM_Newton_McCully_and_LT_Hamilton_V._Bryan.jpg/220px-VADM_Newton_McCully_and_LT_Hamilton_V._Bryan.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/VADM_Newton_McCully_and_LT_Hamilton_V._Bryan.jpg/330px-VADM_Newton_McCully_and_LT_Hamilton_V._Bryan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/VADM_Newton_McCully_and_LT_Hamilton_V._Bryan.jpg/440px-VADM_Newton_McCully_and_LT_Hamilton_V._Bryan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1265" data-file-height="1673" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:VADM_Newton_McCully_and_LT_Hamilton_V._Bryan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vice Admiral Newton McCully and Lt. Hamilton Bryan</div></div></div><p>
Vice Admiral Bristol detached one of the various destroyers in his command, to operate along the northern Black Sea coast to assist the Mission, enabling McCully to continuously maintain mail and radio communication. Bristol dispatched Lt. Hamilton V. Bryan to serve as McCully's agent at <a href="/wiki/Odessa" title="Odessa">Odessa</a> in the Ukraine, and he also kept Bristol informed of happenings.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>  On the morning of February 10, 1920, Koehler came ashore at <a href="/wiki/Odessa" title="Odessa">Odessa</a> <a href="/wiki/Lighthouse" title="Lighthouse">lighthouse</a> from the destroyer <a href="/wiki/USS_Talbot_(DD-114)" title="USS Talbot (DD-114)">USS&#160;<i>Talbot</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(DD-114)</span></a> on a mission to evacuate the few American citizens believed still in the city, which had been overrun by the Bolshevik Red Army in the <a href="/wiki/Odessa_Operation_(1920)" title="Odessa Operation (1920)">Odessa Operation</a> after General <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Shilling" title="Nikolai Shilling">Nikolai Shilling</a>, Denikin's appointed commander of White forces in the Odessa area, had failed to mount any defense and been among the first to evacuate.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Russian and British mission officers, along with 5,000 refugees were being evacuated by sea under the protection of the British cruiser <a href="/wiki/HMS_Ceres_(D59)" title="HMS Ceres (D59)">HMS&#160;<i>Ceres</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Negotiating his way up from the lighthouse keeper, who was loath to go into the city, to the captain of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Guard" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Guard">Red Guard</a>, Koehler quickly arranged to be taken to meet with General <a href="/wiki/Ieronim_Uborevich" title="Ieronim Uborevich">Ieronim Uborevich</a> of the Red Army, the victor of Odessa that had vanquished Denikin's Whites from the town.  Repeatedly questioned by a <a href="/wiki/Commisar" class="mw-redirect" title="Commisar">commisar</a> what <a href="/wiki/Triple_Entente" title="Triple Entente">Entente</a> <a href="/wiki/Men-of-war" class="mw-redirect" title="Men-of-war">men-of-war</a> were doing in the harbor and why they had fired on <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> troops, Koehler steadfastly and calmly parried the charges, insisting that no firing had occurred since <i>Talbot</i> anchored in the harbor, that the Americans were there for the sole purpose of evacuating <a href="/wiki/Refugees" class="mw-redirect" title="Refugees">refugees</a>, and that he understood the reason for prior naval gun fire was because Odessa had been occupied by "marauders and thieves" before the Red Army entered.  When Uborevich questioned Koehler about American opinion of Bolshevism and its recent victories, Koehler responded that he "had not been in America since the war, so he "did not know American opinion in detail, but that in general, I thought that American opinion was not impressed with the chance of success of any government based on the will of so minute a minority as that of the present Bolshevist regime."<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Koehler was initially told by Uborevich and a <a href="/wiki/Commisar" class="mw-redirect" title="Commisar">commisar</a> that they would need "word from Moscow" on his request to make contact with the Americans, that might take two or three days.  Koehler was able to expertly manipulate around the delay and avoid becoming an extended "guest" of the Reds.  When Uborevich asked Koehler what he thought of the recent Red victories, Koehler told him that his impression was that the Red advance was more an example of the weakness of Denikin's forces than the strength of the Red Army.  "No one made any comment on this reply and I became very definitely of the opinion that at heart they agreed with me."  </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Ukraine.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7d/Map_of_Ukraine.gif/350px-Map_of_Ukraine.gif" decoding="async" width="350" height="364" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7d/Map_of_Ukraine.gif/525px-Map_of_Ukraine.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7d/Map_of_Ukraine.gif/700px-Map_of_Ukraine.gif 2x" data-file-width="998" data-file-height="1038" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Ukraine.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of South Russia (Ukraine and Crimean peninsula)</div></div></div><p>Accompanied by a general, orderly and a guard, Koehler first went to the address of Mrs. Annette Keyser (1893–1971), a Russian-born composer, singer and widow of an American citizen.  As the grateful woman wrote a few years later to <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of the Navy">Secretary of the Navy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Denby" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward Denby">Edward Denby</a>, "I happened to be sick in bed with pneumonia and tonsillitis, and I wrote a letter to the American mission, imploring them to save me...  I could not believe my eyes when I beheld a tall man entering my room dressed in a black cloak, conveyed by two armed Bolshevists.  Is it possible?  An AMERICAN OFFICER, I exclaimed.... Lt. Cmdr. Hugo W. Koehler-- came close, he said, 'Yes, it is possible; here I am to help you, as I know you are sick."  I broke down, crying like a child, and begged the kind officer to take me to America, to my Mother, as I had no one in Odessa but my beloved husband's grave...  During the sad scene one of the armed Bolshevists took stations at the door, and the other, evidently knowing the English language, came closer to hear the conversation. Lieutenant Commander Koehler tried to quiet me, explaining that he had orders to take the refugees to Constantinople only.  The neighbors hearing this, advised me to remain, as I was too ill to travel.  Lieutenant Commander Koehler, also finding this best, advised me to stay.... Thinking that I was in need, he offered me money... and he assured me that I was not in danger, and if he found out that I was, he would come and take me with him.  After he left, my friend explained to me that his (Koehler's) life was in danger, as there was no government to be responsible if anything should happen to him.... [I] prayed to be in a position to come to the United States some day in order to find Lieutenant Commander Koehler and to thank him personally and to tell the world that there are still some noble and kind people who will endanger their lives to help a little, weak woman, a mere stranger."  The Secretary of the Navy wrote to Koehler that his "chivalrous efforts" were "the kind of service that makes life worthwhile."<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>From her studio in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="Los Angeles, California">Los Angeles, California</a>, Annette Keyser recalled a dozen years later, "I was ill with pneumonia at Odessa.... My condition was too serious to permit my removal and Commander Koehler came ashore and with the aid of Capt. [James] Irvine got a promise from the revolutionists that upon my recovery I would be permitted to leave.  This I did and was put on board a Turkish steamer and reached Constantinople safely."<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>  After Koehler had made arrangements with the Reds to secure Keyser's safety and eventual evacuation to Constantinople, with his Red escort he next visited the addresses of three American men, and determined that two had already made it safely out of Odessa; however, the third was believed to be in the city and "strongly suspected of pro-Bolshevik leanings".  Koehler was then taken back to Red headquarters, where he was able to delay his voluntary departure for a day, and make further observations as he walked ten miles through the besieged city. "I entered two food shops and although there was not a great abundance of supplies, both shops had customers buying food.  All money is current: <a href="/wiki/Soviet" class="mw-redirect" title="Soviet">Soviet</a>, Romanov, <a href="/wiki/Kerensky" class="mw-redirect" title="Kerensky">Kerensky</a>, even <a href="/wiki/Anton_Denikin" title="Anton Denikin">Denikin</a> army money, in accordance with the decree to the effect that shopkeepers are required to accept every kind of Russian money tendered to them.... Streets of the town were in deplorable condition. Numerous dead horses and dogs were lying about, but I saw no human bodies,... the Americans I was endeavoring to locate lived in widely separated quarters of the town, so I was able to go practically everywhere I wished... I was particularly on the lookout for signs of German influence, German officers or munitions, any trace of German activity, but failed to discover anything...."<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> About a week after Koehler's departure from Odessa, the Bolshevik government sent a long wireless dispatch to President Wilson and the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>, complaining that after "Captain Keller" had left the city and "given [his] word" that he would not fire on the town, "a murderous fire was opened up from [his] entire squadron and hundreds of innocent women and children were killed thereby."  As Koehler wrote to his mother about the deceitful fabrication, "And incidentally, although this message described me as a very terrible and very wicked man, I've always been grateful to the Bolsheviks for it, for in light of these tactics I was able to understand many things not clear to me before."<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Novorossiysk.2C_March_1920"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Novorossiysk,_March_1920">Novorossiysk, March 1920</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Novorossiysk, March 1920">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_aboard_USS_Galveston.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9a/Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_aboard_USS_Galveston.jpg/350px-Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_aboard_USS_Galveston.jpg" decoding="async" width="350" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9a/Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_aboard_USS_Galveston.jpg/525px-Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_aboard_USS_Galveston.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9a/Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_aboard_USS_Galveston.jpg/700px-Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_aboard_USS_Galveston.jpg 2x" data-file-width="738" data-file-height="433" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_aboard_USS_Galveston.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rear Admiral <a href="/wiki/Newton_McCully" class="mw-redirect" title="Newton McCully">Newton McCully</a> aboard <a href="/wiki/USS_Galveston_(CL-19)" title="USS Galveston (CL-19)">USS&#160;<i>Galveston</i></a> at <a href="/wiki/Novorossiysk" title="Novorossiysk">Novorossiysk</a>, March 1920</div></div></div><p> After the fall of Odessa to the Reds, Koehler and McCully returned to <a href="/wiki/Sebastopol" class="mw-redirect" title="Sebastopol">Sebastopol</a> and a short visit to the battle front.  Returning to <a href="/wiki/Novorossiysk" title="Novorossiysk">Novorossiysk</a> on February 20 aboard the cruiser <a href="/wiki/USS_Galveston_(CL-19)" title="USS Galveston (CL-19)">USS&#160;<i>Galveston</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(CL-19)</span></a>, they found the city flooded with refugees from Kharkov and <a href="/wiki/Rostov" title="Rostov">Rostov</a>.  Denikin blamed his subordinates for his military failures, particularly General <a href="/wiki/Pyotr_Wrangel" title="Pyotr Wrangel">Pyotr Wrangel</a>, who had advocated a concentration of White forces in the Crimea to make a last stand there in December, a plan that Denikin had rejected.  Now in March, it was a disastrous, headlong retreat into the port of Novorossiysk that Denikin had made no provisions for.  Instead, while many in the White army and navy had called on Denikin to replace the incompetent Shilling with Wrangel, Denikin had banished Wrangel, a respected and highly competent soldier, to <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>  The end of Denikin's army came amidst a brutal winter, and a <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">Typhoid fever</a> epidemic. On March 16, <a href="/wiki/Ekaterinodar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ekaterinodar">Ekaterinodar</a>, capital of the <a href="/wiki/Kuban" title="Kuban">Kuban</a> province about 80 miles northeast of Novorossiysk was captured by the Reds. On March 26, Red forces reached the vicinity of Novorossiysk, advancing along the railway as the British battleship <a href="/wiki/HMS_Emperor_of_India" title="HMS Emperor of India">HMS&#160;<i>Emperor of India</i></a>, the cruiser <a href="/wiki/HMS_Calypso_(D61)" title="HMS Calypso (D61)">HMS <i>Calypso</i></a> and the <a href="/wiki/French_cruiser_Waldeck-Rousseau" title="French cruiser Waldeck-Rousseau">French cruiser <i>Waldeck-Rousseau</i></a> fired their naval guns into the hills to support the withdrawal of Denikin's forces.  Around noon, fires broke out by the railway yards and waterfront, which soon became uncontrolled infernos, consuming buildings, warehouses, ammunition, oil and rolling stock worth hundreds of thousands of dollars. Tons of weapons, tanks, armored cars and munitions were pushed off the docks into the harbor. As Koehler described it, "Ships lying alongside the docks crowded on human cargo almost to the last inch of space and then, fearing the fire, moved away from the piers into the stream, although they made almost no impression on the multitude seeking to board.  Packed on the docks and beach, surrounded by raging fire, were thousands who had hoped and expected to be taken away, but who had been left behind for lack of ships to embark them.  These were mostly soldiers just arrived from the vanished front, but many women and children were seen among them."<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Rear Admiral McCully, a humanitarian and a naval officer, recommended to the State Department, that the Russian refugees from the <a href="/wiki/Caucus" title="Caucus">Caucus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuban" title="Kuban">Kuban</a> be granted asylum, but was turned down.  Although Admiral Bristol was also a humanitarian, and ultimately played a major role in the resettlement of displaced persons from the region, he did not then want to be saddled with thousands of refugees and ordered McCully to bring no more than 250 down the <a href="/wiki/Bosporus_Strait" class="mw-redirect" title="Bosporus Strait">Bosporus Strait</a> in his ships.  About 200 women and children were transported on <i>Galveston</i> and the destroyer, <a href="/wiki/USS_Smith_Thompson_(DD-212)" title="USS Smith Thompson (DD-212)">USS&#160;<i>Smith Thompson</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(DD-212)</span></a> to <a href="/wiki/Proti_Island" title="Proti Island">Proti Island</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Marmara" title="Sea of Marmara">Sea of Marmara</a>, with another thousand transported on American ships to the <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> On the morning of March 27, <i>Galveston</i> stood out from Novorossiysk, with 3-inch shells from Red shore batteries falling perilously close, as the beleaguered city fell to the Reds. Koehler wrote, "Several small boat trips were made from the <i>Galveston</i> in a last effort to rescue a few more women and children, but it was impossible to get any considerable number of them through the throng."<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Besides troops and refugees pouring into the Crimea, another 50,000 refugees were transported to the Bosporus on Russian ships.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Koehler described the plight of those who could not flee, but instead sought to negotiate with the Bolsheviks for surrender, "The Reds promised immunity to all except malefactors, on the condition the troops would march against the Poles; but immediately on the surrender being accomplished, the Reds began the usual slaughter of officers and stripped the soldiers of their clothes.  The <a href="/wiki/Cossacks" title="Cossacks">Cossacks</a> again took to arms, about 10,000 taking to the hills and about 2,000 escaping across the <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgian</a> border. Unable to take them along, about 700 children were drowned at the beach by their mothers, who then took to the hills with the men."<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 248px"><div style="width: 248px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 246px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Red_Cross_Officer,_Lt._L.M._Foster_with_mobile,_Army_field_kitchen_at_Novorossisk_Russia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bb/American_Red_Cross_Officer%2C_Lt._L.M._Foster_with_mobile%2C_Army_field_kitchen_at_Novorossisk_Russia.jpg/369px-American_Red_Cross_Officer%2C_Lt._L.M._Foster_with_mobile%2C_Army_field_kitchen_at_Novorossisk_Russia.jpg" decoding="async" width="246" height="180" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bb/American_Red_Cross_Officer%2C_Lt._L.M._Foster_with_mobile%2C_Army_field_kitchen_at_Novorossisk_Russia.jpg/554px-American_Red_Cross_Officer%2C_Lt._L.M._Foster_with_mobile%2C_Army_field_kitchen_at_Novorossisk_Russia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bb/American_Red_Cross_Officer%2C_Lt._L.M._Foster_with_mobile%2C_Army_field_kitchen_at_Novorossisk_Russia.jpg/739px-American_Red_Cross_Officer%2C_Lt._L.M._Foster_with_mobile%2C_Army_field_kitchen_at_Novorossisk_Russia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="952" data-file-height="696" /></a></div></div>

		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 244.66666666667px"><div style="width: 244.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 242.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Russian_children_refugees_on_Red_Cross_Relief_Ship_Sangamon_with_Lt._L._M._Foster_at_Novorossiysk_March_1923.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d9/Russian_children_refugees_on_Red_Cross_Relief_Ship_Sangamon_with_Lt._L._M._Foster_at_Novorossiysk_March_1923.jpg/364px-Russian_children_refugees_on_Red_Cross_Relief_Ship_Sangamon_with_Lt._L._M._Foster_at_Novorossiysk_March_1923.jpg" decoding="async" width="243" height="180" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d9/Russian_children_refugees_on_Red_Cross_Relief_Ship_Sangamon_with_Lt._L._M._Foster_at_Novorossiysk_March_1923.jpg/545px-Russian_children_refugees_on_Red_Cross_Relief_Ship_Sangamon_with_Lt._L._M._Foster_at_Novorossiysk_March_1923.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d9/Russian_children_refugees_on_Red_Cross_Relief_Ship_Sangamon_with_Lt._L._M._Foster_at_Novorossiysk_March_1923.jpg/727px-Russian_children_refugees_on_Red_Cross_Relief_Ship_Sangamon_with_Lt._L._M._Foster_at_Novorossiysk_March_1923.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1055" data-file-height="784" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
