</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pyotr_Wrangel,_portrait_medium.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Pyotr_Wrangel%2C_portrait_medium.jpg/200px-Pyotr_Wrangel%2C_portrait_medium.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Pyotr_Wrangel%2C_portrait_medium.jpg/300px-Pyotr_Wrangel%2C_portrait_medium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e3/Pyotr_Wrangel%2C_portrait_medium.jpg 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="580" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pyotr_Wrangel,_portrait_medium.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General (Baron) <a href="/wiki/Pyotr_Wrangel" title="Pyotr Wrangel">Pyotr Wrangel</a> "The Black Baron"</div></div></div><p>The debacle at Novorossiysk completely discredited Denikin, who fled the Crimea on March 21 and sailed to England. One of his last acts was to begrudgingly appoint General (Baron) Pyotr Wrangel as commander in chief of the White resistance.  On April 4, Denikin issued a proclamation to the <a href="/wiki/Don_Cossacks" title="Don Cossacks">Don</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terek_Cossacks" title="Terek Cossacks">Terek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuban" title="Kuban">Kuban</a> cossack representatives dissolving the democratic government that had been formed on February 4, but making no provision for representative institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Wrangel, known as the "Black Baron" by the Reds, immediately locked down the Crimea as the last enclave of the White movement, ruthlessly restoring discipline by executing all looters, agitators, speculators and <a href="/wiki/Commissars" class="mw-redirect" title="Commissars">commissars</a>, in one instance parading 370 men in front of him and then having them all shot. The remaining men were offered the alternative of joining the White Army.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> On April 10, Wrangel sailed with an expedition to <a href="/wiki/Perekop" title="Perekop">Perekop</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Azov" title="Sea of Azov">Sea of Azov</a> and after six days of heavy fighting had made a slight advance to secure a path for egress of the Crimean forces to the north, a tactical and symbolic advance that revived morale among the White troops and the confidence of the people that supported the resistance.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> With the reorganization of the White forces, on June 1, 1920, the "Black Baron" had an operational army of 40,000, consisting of <a href="/wiki/1st_Army_Corps_(AFSR)" class="mw-redirect" title="1st Army Corps (AFSR)">First Corps</a> under General <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Kutepov" title="Alexander Kutepov">Alexander Kutepov</a> at <a href="/wiki/Perekop" title="Perekop">Perekop</a> with 7,000 <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_Army" title="Volunteer Army">Volunteer Army</a> infantry, 46 guns, 12 tanks, 21 aeroplanes and 500 cavalry;  <a href="/wiki/2nd_Army_Corps_(VSUR)" class="mw-redirect" title="2nd Army Corps (VSUR)">Second Corps</a> under General <a href="/wiki/Yakov_Slashchov" title="Yakov Slashchov">Yakov Slashchov</a>, organized for a combined naval and military expedition to a port on the Sea of Azov, with 10,000 men in all, 58 guns, 3 aeroplanes, 5 armored motorcars and 400 cavalry; Third Corps under General A. Pisarev at the <a href="/wiki/Sivash" class="mw-redirect" title="Sivash">Sivash</a> Isthmus with 11,000 men total, 19 guns, 9 aeroplanes, 3 armored trains, and 1,960 cavalry; and Fourth Corps, composed of dismounted Kuban cossacks, 14 guns, and about 16,000 men in reserve near Sivash.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>  Wrangel also improved the naval assets of the White forces, with a Black Sea and separate Azov Sea <a href="/wiki/Flotilla" title="Flotilla">flotilla</a>. Receiving coal shipments from Constantinople in April and May to enable the ships to steam, the <a href="/wiki/Wrangel%27s_fleet" title="Wrangel&#39;s fleet">Black Sea ships</a> numbered a battleship, three cruisers, ten destroyers and eight gunboats, and in the Azov Sea the Whites had fifteen shallow-draft boats.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kuban_Cossacks_Russian_Civil_War.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6a/Kuban_Cossacks_Russian_Civil_War.jpg/300px-Kuban_Cossacks_Russian_Civil_War.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="108" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6a/Kuban_Cossacks_Russian_Civil_War.jpg/450px-Kuban_Cossacks_Russian_Civil_War.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6a/Kuban_Cossacks_Russian_Civil_War.jpg/600px-Kuban_Cossacks_Russian_Civil_War.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2656" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kuban_Cossacks_Russian_Civil_War.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kuban Cossacks during the Russian Civil War</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Baron_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_White_Army_soldiers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Baron_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_White_Army_soldiers.jpg/300px-Baron_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_White_Army_soldiers.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Baron_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_White_Army_soldiers.jpg/450px-Baron_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_White_Army_soldiers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Baron_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_White_Army_soldiers.jpg/600px-Baron_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_White_Army_soldiers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1125" data-file-height="559" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Baron_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_White_Army_soldiers.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Baron Pyotr Wrangel and White Army soldiers</div></div></div><p>Morale was also greatly improved, and while for the first time since the spring of 1918, the <a href="/wiki/Cossacks" title="Cossacks">Cossacks</a> did not figure as the prominent element of the White army, with a corresponding decrease in <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> superiority, the army was now made up of men who were determined to fight the enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-REGB60UPWMC_p._266-267_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-REGB60UPWMC_p._266-267-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> As Koehler wrote in his report, "The Cossacks are fighting to get back to their <a href="/wiki/Stanitsa" title="Stanitsa">stanitzi</a> in the Don and the Kuban- nor do they see much beyond that.  They do not loot now, but resent being deprived of (as they look at it) a well-earned privilege... Russia means nothing to them... They... fight any... power or regime or idea that interferes with their old priveleges."<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h4><span id="Melitopol.2C_June_1920"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Melitopol,_June_1920">Melitopol, June 1920</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Melitopol, June 1920">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Baroness_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_her_three_children_in_1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c9/Baroness_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_her_three_children_in_1920.jpg/300px-Baroness_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_her_three_children_in_1920.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c9/Baroness_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_her_three_children_in_1920.jpg/450px-Baroness_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_her_three_children_in_1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c9/Baroness_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_her_three_children_in_1920.jpg/600px-Baroness_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_her_three_children_in_1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="949" data-file-height="707" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Baroness_Pyotr_Wrangel_and_her_three_children_in_1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Baroness Olga Wrangel with her three children at Constantinople in 1920</div></div></div><p>On April 3, Koehler and McCully reached the Crimean port of <a href="/wiki/Yalta" title="Yalta">Yalta</a>, where Koehler met Baron Wrangel hours after the general had returned from Constantinople to take charge of the remaining men of the White Army, and then headed to <a href="/wiki/Sebastopol" class="mw-redirect" title="Sebastopol">Sebastopol</a>.  While her husband commanded the White forces in South Russia, Baroness Wrangel remained in Constantinople with her three children, where she helped the <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a> care for the thousands of Russian refugees who were driven from their homes by the collapse of the Denikin forces. Baroness Wrangel was a daily worker at the island of Proti where the Red Cross cared for a colony of more than a thousand of these homeless and destitute people. In early June, McCully and Koehler stood out on the destroyer <a href="/wiki/USS_Smith_Thompson_(DD-212)" title="USS Smith Thompson (DD-212)"><i>Smith Thompson</i></a> from Sebastopol for the Sea of Azov based on information that the White army was making a landing there. Entering the <a href="/wiki/Kerch_Strait" title="Kerch Strait">Kerch Strait</a> on June 6, the ship hugged the port shoreline to avoid the Red shore batteries on the starboard side.  They were taken under fire, and the first shot came close as the destroyer sped up to 25 knots and hugged the west side of the straight, making it through to reach the <a href="/wiki/Sivash" class="mw-redirect" title="Sivash">Sivash</a> front  and General Slashchov's troop landing at the small village of Kirilovkar.  McCully and Koehler went ashore, with McCully returning to the ship later that day with ten wounded Russian soldiers that were transported that night by the destroyer through the Kerch Strait to Sebastopol.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>  Koehler stayed ashore, and the next day he caught up with Slashchov at Radionovka, interviewing Bolshevik prisoners and villagers on the way. "Villagers were bitter enough in denunciations of Bolsheviks, but I noted no great enthusiasm for Wrangel's forces- the attitude was one of indifference- of a people who had known the worst and cared little one way or another..." <sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_armored_train_and_raiding_party,_ca._1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Red_armored_train_and_raiding_party%2C_ca._1920.jpg/300px-Red_armored_train_and_raiding_party%2C_ca._1920.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Red_armored_train_and_raiding_party%2C_ca._1920.jpg/450px-Red_armored_train_and_raiding_party%2C_ca._1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Red_armored_train_and_raiding_party%2C_ca._1920.jpg/600px-Red_armored_train_and_raiding_party%2C_ca._1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="937" data-file-height="528" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_armored_train_and_raiding_party,_ca._1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Red armored train and raiding party, ca. 1920</div></div></div><p>On daybreak of the third day, Koehler joined a small cavalry detachment headed toward the fighting in <a href="/wiki/Melitopol" title="Melitopol">Melitopol</a>. "Upon arrival at the outskirts of Melitopol we found Reds still held Melitopol station and were bombarding the town from armored trains at a distance, but the south Russian forces  had occupied the main part of town.  I continued on into the town and went at once to headquarters where I found General Tsichetski installed in the building hurriedly vacated a few hours previously by the Bolshevik Central Committee."  At his request, Koehler was given three cossacks to accompany him to the address he had earlier obtained from a prisoner for the headquarters of the Bolshevik Tchresvichaika. Breaking the locks, the group began collecting up the papers that seemed of value, until seeing a group of White cavalry galloping by, they learned they were caught in the middle of Red troops that had counter-attacked and reentered the city. Rather than try to flee, Koehler and his party commandeered a cart and hid with their load of papers by a flour mill for a few hours, until the Red troops themselves retreated with White cavalry on their flank. Koehler and his group rode on to Radionovka "with their loot".<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>  Within a few days, Slashchov had conquered the entire province and doubled Wrangel's White-held territory, secured enough grain to feed the Crimea and his cavalry's horses, and improved the standing of the White cause in Europe.  When Pisarev attacked at <a href="/wiki/Chongar" class="mw-redirect" title="Chongar">Chongar</a> and Kutepov's <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_Army" title="Volunteer Army">Volunteer Army</a> troops engaged the main Red forces at <a href="/wiki/Perekop" title="Perekop">Perekop</a>, the Reds were caught off guard and had to retreat to avoid being surrounded.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Red_Army_before_being_sent_to_the_Civil_War.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/The_Red_Army_before_being_sent_to_the_Civil_War.JPG/300px-The_Red_Army_before_being_sent_to_the_Civil_War.JPG" decoding="async" width="300" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/The_Red_Army_before_being_sent_to_the_Civil_War.JPG/450px-The_Red_Army_before_being_sent_to_the_Civil_War.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/The_Red_Army_before_being_sent_to_the_Civil_War.JPG/600px-The_Red_Army_before_being_sent_to_the_Civil_War.JPG 2x" data-file-width="965" data-file-height="714" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Red_Army_before_being_sent_to_the_Civil_War.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Red Army volunteers before being sent to the Civil War, ca. 1919</div></div></div><p>The day Koehler headed back to Crimea, <a href="/wiki/Leon_Trotsky" title="Leon Trotsky">Leon Trotsky</a> arrived in <a href="/wiki/Zaporizhia" title="Zaporizhia">Alexandrovsk</a> and announced that the <a href="/wiki/Tauride" class="mw-redirect" title="Tauride">Tauride</a> with its great stores of grain, had to be retaken, since a winter of starvation for the Reds had to be avoided at all costs. Koehler observed the White army equally needed the Tauride and its grain, "For the south too, will go hungry without this grain, and the army would be the first to starve... An army that has known cold and hunger as has this army, will fight hard before it leaves plenty to go back to cold and hungry."<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>  For Koehler's exploit in Melitopol, Rear Admiral McCully recommended him for award of the <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Service_Medal_(United_States_Navy)" title="Distinguished Service Medal (United States Navy)">Distinguished Service Medal</a> in July 1923 with the following citation, "During the capture of Melitopol by the Wrangel forces on June 10, 1920, he was in the advance guard [that] entered the town, immediately proceeding to the Bolshevik headquarters and began collection of their papers. While engaged in this work the town was recaptured by the Bolshevik troops and Lt. Cmdr. Koehler for several hours was cut off from the troops which he accompanied. He managed to conceal himself and in addition saved the papers which he had secured and which were of much value." The Bureau of Navigation declined to make the award, presumably not prepared to officially recognize an American naval officer in <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> Russia, much less, in <a href="/wiki/Combat" title="Combat">combat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Capelotti._pg._210,_fn._5_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capelotti._pg._210,_fn._5-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>  Each of Wrangel's corps had a chief of civil administration to organize the newly occupied territory.  In his report, Koehler recounted his interviews with the administrator in Melitopol, "He said his guiding idea was that this is a civil war, a struggle between Russians-- Russians of widely different ideas, to be sure-- but Russians; in short, the occupied area is not to be treated as conquered territory but must be administered so people will understand that the new regime is working for them and with them, not against them... [he] stated the first plan had been to give the land to the peasants without payment of any kind, but peasants would not believe that land received for nothing was actually theirs; ...[unless] they could pay actual money for the land, receive a receipt for such payment, and take home a paper with a large red seal that said the land was theirs..."<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Russian_Volunteer_Army_troops_guarding_the_warehouse_of_the_American_Red_Cross.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4f/Russian_Volunteer_Army_troops_guarding_the_warehouse_of_the_American_Red_Cross.jpg/220px-Russian_Volunteer_Army_troops_guarding_the_warehouse_of_the_American_Red_Cross.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="329" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4f/Russian_Volunteer_Army_troops_guarding_the_warehouse_of_the_American_Red_Cross.jpg/330px-Russian_Volunteer_Army_troops_guarding_the_warehouse_of_the_American_Red_Cross.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4f/Russian_Volunteer_Army_troops_guarding_the_warehouse_of_the_American_Red_Cross.jpg/440px-Russian_Volunteer_Army_troops_guarding_the_warehouse_of_the_American_Red_Cross.jpg 2x" data-file-width="647" data-file-height="969" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Russian_Volunteer_Army_troops_guarding_the_warehouse_of_the_American_Red_Cross.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russian <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_Army" title="Volunteer Army">Volunteer Army</a> troops guarding <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a> medical supplies, June 1920</div></div></div>
<p>In a letter to Dolly Gladstone, Koehler gently chided her for Britain's investment in Denikin, having lately turned its sympathy from the cause of the White Russians under Denikin's successor, Wrangel, to the Bolshevists, "I'm afraid your experiment in backing Denikin has been much more costly for you and for Russia and for the world than the simple L400,000,000 (in stores, etc.,) you ventured on the project... Poor old Denikin- simple, honest, and a patriot, but undoubtedly one of the stupidest men who ever came into power in any country. Surrounded by incompetents and dishonesty, badly advised by his allies, he distinguished himself by not one single sound or wise measure. If he had thrown dice for every decision he would have improved just fifty percent.  General Wrangel is of a different caliber: fine soldier, good general, honest, capable, full of courage and initiative, no great statesman, but he knows it and surrounds himself with men who can take care of that end of the game... His miracles continue. But he cannot beat bolshevism by force of arms- nor can any army.  Bolshevism fattens on military opposition. It will collapse without it... I like the Russians, immensely so, in fact, and we've become great friends.... General Kutepov (the commander of the First Corps) and I have become sworn brothers.  He is about the finest type of soldierman I've ever seen- and I'm very fond of him.  Many other of my friends are not as estimable, I am afraid, for about all the brigands, thieves, murderers, and similar pleasant scoundrels that disturb the peace hereabouts count themselves among my intimate friends... It is difficult to keep things in a land such as this, when the need is so great; one has almost a guilty feeling if one owns a sheet or an extra shirt. So long ago I gave away all I had, and now if I had a sheet or an extra shirt I wouldn't know what to do with it except to give it to some poor woman to make a child's dress... I'm afraid I've given you a rather chaotic picture of it all- but you might take the chaotic effect simply as a bit of realism, for life is indeed chaos here; it resembles reasonable conditions just about as much as that famous <a href="/wiki/Cubist" class="mw-redirect" title="Cubist">cubist</a> picture <a href="/wiki/Nude_Descending_a_Staircase,_No._2" title="Nude Descending a Staircase, No. 2">Nude Descending a Stair</a> resembles anything real.  In the first months I was here I accumulated great stacks of loot, from pictures and furs to old earrings and relics.  But I've decided to take none of it from Russia.  I've given it all away again."  Koehler's appeal on behalf of the suffering masses in Russia did not fall on deaf ears.  Rear Admiral McCully's July 15, 1920 report to the Secretary of State noted, "Recently representatives of a British Fund established by Lady Muriel Paget have established a hospital in Sebastopol, and a <a href="/wiki/British_Red_Cross" title="British Red Cross">British Red Cross</a> and Children's Relief Organization are also at work."<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="White_advances.2C_Summer_1920"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="White_advances,_Summer_1920">White advances, Summer 1920</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: White advances, Summer 1920">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Kutepov.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Alexander_Kutepov.jpg/230px-Alexander_Kutepov.jpg" decoding="async" width="230" height="325" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bf/Alexander_Kutepov.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="248" data-file-height="350" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Kutepov.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lt. Gen. <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Kutepov" title="Alexander Kutepov">Alexander Kutepov</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_Army" title="Volunteer Army">Volunteer Army</a></div></div></div><p>The decisive turn in the fortunes of Wrangel's army, was not the reorganization of his forces, as much as the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Soviet_War" title="Polish–Soviet War">Polish–Soviet War</a>.  The Bolsheviks, recognizing their vulnerability in a two-front war, did not seek to engage the Poles.  However, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Pi%C5%82sudski" title="Józef Piłsudski">Marshal Piłsudski</a>, the Polish head of state had plans to annex the Ukrainian and White Russian provinces. Negotiations broke down and on April 25 the Poles began their attack, achieving a major victory on May 6 with the fall of <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">Kiev</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> complained, "In the Crimea Wrangel gathered more and more strength. His troops consisted almost entirely of officers in the hope that in the first possible moment the army would be expanded by entrance of peasants. Wrangel's army was equipped with tanks, cannons, and airplanes better than all the other armies which had fought in Russia. While we fought the Poles, Wrangel gathered his forces."<sup id="cite_ref-REGB60UPWMC_p._266-267_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-REGB60UPWMC_p._266-267-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> While Wrangel's army tried to solidify its holdings in the northern Tauride, Koehler spent much of July and August sorting and translating the trove of documents he had snatched from Bolshevik headquarters in Melitopol, with a brief visit to the front on July 20.  The tide again turned against Wrangel's forces in the middle of August, when the Polish forces defeated the Soviets on the outskirts of <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>.  After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Warsaw_(1920)" title="Battle of Warsaw (1920)">Battle of Vistula</a>, with nearly 70,000 Red soldiers taken prisoner, the ensuing peace with Poland signalled the eventual end of Wrangel's regime, with the full force of the Red army now freed to focus on the Crimea.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> But the end was still a couple months away, and on August 27, Koehler left by train from Sebastopol to Melitopol, where he called on General Kutepov at his First Army headquarters.  "Even General Kutepov himself with all his indomitable energy plainly showed from overwork... He explained that although strictly military considerations might demand a withdrawal from the present extensive front on account of the heavy cost in casualties in holding so long a line against superior numbers, he felt that political conditions and loyalty to the inhabitants of the occupied territory who had aided Russian forces (and many of those younger men were now in the army), demanded that the Tauride be held at almost any cost."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Russian_soldiers_executing_civilians.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0f/White_Russian_soldiers_executing_civilians.jpg/300px-White_Russian_soldiers_executing_civilians.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0f/White_Russian_soldiers_executing_civilians.jpg/450px-White_Russian_soldiers_executing_civilians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0f/White_Russian_soldiers_executing_civilians.jpg/600px-White_Russian_soldiers_executing_civilians.jpg 2x" data-file-width="988" data-file-height="682" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Russian_soldiers_executing_civilians.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>White army soldiers executing two civilians</div></div></div>
<p>Perhaps Koehler did not see, or chose to overlook another side of Kutepov, his "sworn brother", who along with Pisarev, commonly executed people without trial and for minimal cause. Earlier that year, as military governor of Sebastopol, his number of executions and cruelty sparked complaints among the peasants and civic leaders. One particularly egregious instance, was the hanging of a young <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tartar</a> poet, accused of nothing but vague, Bolshevist sympathies.  Complaints were sent to Wrangel, and rather than investigate the claims, Wrangel summoned the mayor of Sebastopol before him. Recalling in his memoirs years later what he told the politician, Wrangel wrote, "I know all about your disagreement with General Kutepov, who is merely carrying out my orders. I am not going to discuss with you who is right, or which of us two has given orders. I am responsible to the Army and to the people, and I follow the dictates of my science. I am quite sure that were you in my place you would act differently; but as it happens destiny has given me and not you direction of the Russian Cause, and I will stop at nothing in the accomplishment of my duty, and I will not hesitate to cut down anyone who tries to stop me.  You protest because General Kutepov has hanged a score or so of men who were a danger to the Army and to the Cause. I warn you should the necessity arise I would not hesitate to increase the number by one, and that one would be you."  During the Russian Civil War, many White generals and a number of the Bolshevist leaders developed a great propensity for cruelty and summary executions.  They came to believe in violence for the sake of violence, to send a strong message to the enemy.  It likely was counterproductive, as it only alienated and frightened the silent majority of citizens, while firming the enemy's resolve.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Leaving Melitopol, Koehler went to Feodorovka and visited the headquarters of General Pisarev, Commander of Third Corps.  As Koehler sardonically described the meeting, "He reviewed the military situation and seemed confident of success of his encircling movement, begun some two days before with the object of breaking through in rear of the advancing Red column and then surrounding it. Present tactics, both Bolshevik and Russian, represent nothing so much as a giant game of cross-tag: each side attempts to cut in behind the other, then to outflank the other's flanking column, then again to outflank this outflanking, flanking column-- and so on..."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>  Koehler described the front line he visited in August 1920, "Whenever I arrived on the fighting line, I found the division commander and his chief of staff in the front line or ahead of it, instead of in their classic post two miles in the rear. I noted a general of artillery with a rifle wound in his leg and a saber cut in the head, certainly a most unorthodox proceeding, but here the artillery is immediately behind the infantry if not on the line with it.... Under cover of machine gun fire, the entire Russian line now took up the advance, moving in echelon at good speed... We now gained a slight eminence from which we had an excellent view of the entire movement, including the retreat of the Reds.  [The Whites] were outnumbered about 3 to 1, but in actual battle the Red superiority in numbers really had comparatively little consequence on this front; they had encountered some single regiments that were worth more than other entire divisions. However, in maneuvering and the possibility of exerting a general pressure all along the line, numbers were of course, very valuable.  The general said that regiments composed entirely of Communists invariably fought well, as also did the <a href="/wiki/Latvians" title="Latvians">Latvians</a> and certain international regiments made up mostly of <a href="/wiki/Magyars" class="mw-redirect" title="Magyars">Magyars</a>...."<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> On August 27, Koehler was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_St._Vladimir" class="mw-redirect" title="Order of St. Vladimir">Order of St. Vladimir, fourth class, with swords and bow</a> by Kutepov. A telegram from General <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Vitkovsky" title="Vladimir Vitkovsky">Vladimir Vitkovsky</a> that was found in Koehler's papers years later reads, "With the feeling of sincere pleasure I learn that you have been awarded, sir, with the Order of St. Vladimir, 4th Class, with swords and bow and congratulate you with this high award, which commemorates the day of your visit to the fighting line of my corps." This, and two other Russian Imperial decorations, were among the last awarded to a foreigner or a Russian for that matter. As Koehler's published historiographer wrote, "The strict neutrality of the American Mission, so scrupulously adhered to by Admiral McCully, was evidently less than a pressing concern for the Commander."<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 292.66666666667px"><div style="width: 292.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 290.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:-0.16666666666667px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kuban_Cossacks_in_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/Kuban_Cossacks_in_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg/436px-Kuban_Cossacks_in_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg" decoding="async" width="291" height="176" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/Kuban_Cossacks_in_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg/656px-Kuban_Cossacks_in_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/Kuban_Cossacks_in_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg/874px-Kuban_Cossacks_in_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1466" data-file-height="881" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Kuban Cossacks in the Russian Civil War, showing the variety of <a href="/wiki/Cherkesska" class="mw-redirect" title="Cherkesska">cherkesskas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Waistcoat" title="Waistcoat">beshmets</a> worn
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 243.33333333333px"><div style="width: 243.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 241.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:-0.16666666666667px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Russia_Volunteer_Army_Armored_train_Kalita.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2d/South_Russia_Volunteer_Army_Armored_train_Kalita.jpg/362px-South_Russia_Volunteer_Army_Armored_train_Kalita.jpg" decoding="async" width="242" height="176" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2d/South_Russia_Volunteer_Army_Armored_train_Kalita.jpg/544px-South_Russia_Volunteer_Army_Armored_train_Kalita.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2d/South_Russia_Volunteer_Army_Armored_train_Kalita.jpg/724px-South_Russia_Volunteer_Army_Armored_train_Kalita.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1156" data-file-height="838" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>South Russia <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_Army" title="Volunteer Army">Volunteer Army</a> armored train "Kalita"
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 208px"><div style="width: 208px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 206px;"><div style="margin:-0.16666666666667px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_Army_soldiers,_ca._1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/Red_Army_soldiers%2C_ca._1920.jpg/309px-Red_Army_soldiers%2C_ca._1920.jpg" decoding="async" width="206" height="176" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/Red_Army_soldiers%2C_ca._1920.jpg/465px-Red_Army_soldiers%2C_ca._1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/Red_Army_soldiers%2C_ca._1920.jpg/620px-Red_Army_soldiers%2C_ca._1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="846" data-file-height="717" /></a></div></div>

		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 260px"><div style="width: 260px">

			<div class="gallerytext">

			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 241.33333333333px"><div style="width: 241.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 239.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:-0.16666666666667px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_army_volunteers,_ca._1919.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ac/White_army_volunteers%2C_ca._1919.jpg/359px-White_army_volunteers%2C_ca._1919.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="176" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ac/White_army_volunteers%2C_ca._1919.jpg/540px-White_army_volunteers%2C_ca._1919.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ac/White_army_volunteers%2C_ca._1919.jpg/719px-White_army_volunteers%2C_ca._1919.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="1041" /></a></div></div>

<p>White army volunteers, ca. 1919
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Khabarovsk_during_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/07/Khabarovsk_during_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg/300px-Khabarovsk_during_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/07/Khabarovsk_during_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg/450px-Khabarovsk_during_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/07/Khabarovsk_during_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg/600px-Khabarovsk_during_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1519" data-file-height="1104" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Khabarovsk_during_the_Russian_Civil_War.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Khabarovsk" title="Khabarovsk">Khabarovsk</a> during the Russian Civil War</div></div></div><p>Koehler's conversations with peasants and townspeople in the Tauride yielded insight into the comparative failure of both the Reds and the Whites to improve the economic fortunes of the common man. Asking a farmer the price of wheat, as he complimented the man on the quality of his grain, Koehler was told, "Who could tell?" he answered. "He went on to explain that Bolsheviks took it for nothing, Wrangels forces offered sheaves of paper money for it- if that was standard, he added critically, it evidently must be worth something. Yet Bolsheviks came a long way for it, and Whites were extraordinarily anxious to get it, so it must have value for everyone except the man who had grown it." Koehler concluded that the conditions in "Red Russia" had been harder on the townspeople than the peasants.  "The more I learned directly of conditions in Red Russia... the more evident it becomes that it is townspeople who suffered most from the Bolsheviks and conditions brought about by the Red regime.  They gladly welcomed the Russian troops and quickly aligned themselves with the new Wrangel regime.  Peasants, although they suffered a certain loss from seizure of grain and horses, and although bitter against mobilization, nevertheless in general never suffered any hunger or the many requisitions of clothing, linen, household effects, and about everything else of value townspeople had to endure; nor have they seen at close hand the conduct and methods of the <a href="/wiki/Commissars" class="mw-redirect" title="Commissars">commissars</a>... In these years the man who produces from the soil is king- even in Red Russia... But the spirit of the townspeople is broken- their brains and wills are as weak and starved from lack of food as are their bodies- sufferings have left them exhausted.  Once these townspeople get sufficient food inside them to restore stamina, and when the interior weakness of bolshevism gives them an opportunity, they must restore commerce and industry, yet the latter would also restore the towns- and strengthened, people would no longer submit to the tyranny they have suffered for years. So even in its success, bolshevism contains the elements of its downfall.  The millworker in Alimovka expressed the idea of thousands when he said that formerly the Russians had a <a href="/wiki/Czar" class="mw-redirect" title="Czar">czar</a>, a fool, to rule them, [and] taxes were very bad, but bread was five <a href="/wiki/Russian_ruble" title="Russian ruble">kopeks</a> a pound and plentiful, and a man could get all the shirts, and shoes, and sugar and tea and tobacco he wanted. Now Russia was ruled by very clever men, [and] there were no taxes at all, but bread was 500 rubles a pound and scarce, and neither shirts nor shoes nor sugar nor tea nor tobacco were to be had."<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h4><span id="Crimea.2C_September.E2.80.93October_1920"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Crimea,_September–October_1920">Crimea, September–October 1920</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Crimea, September–October 1920">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ivan_Barbovich.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Ivan_Barbovich.jpg/180px-Ivan_Barbovich.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Ivan_Barbovich.jpg/270px-Ivan_Barbovich.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Ivan_Barbovich.jpg/360px-Ivan_Barbovich.jpg 2x" data-file-width="534" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ivan_Barbovich.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gen. Ivan Barbovich</div></div></div><p>Koehler was back at the front two weeks later, taking the train to <a href="/wiki/Melitopol" title="Melitopol">Melitopol</a> and meeting with General Kutepov, who told of the White's capture of <a href="/wiki/Zaporizhia" title="Zaporizhia">Alexandrovsk</a> and seizing military assets of armored trains, 14 steamboats, 2,000 horses, 1,000 railroad cars and 33 "hot" (operational) <a href="/wiki/Locomotives" class="mw-redirect" title="Locomotives">locomotives</a>, along with scores of Red prisoners.  Wrangel had reorganized his White army into Kutepov's First Army, consisting of the <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_Army" title="Volunteer Army">Volunteer Army</a>, old <a href="/wiki/Don_Cossacks" title="Don Cossacks">Don Cossacks</a> and the Second Army under General A.P. Dratsenko, consisting of the old Second and Third Corps and a <a href="/wiki/Terek_Cossacks" title="Terek Cossacks">Terek Cossacks</a> brigade. Wrangel also formed two independent <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> units, led by General Ivan Barbovich and the <a href="/wiki/Kuban" title="Kuban">Kuban</a> Cossack General Nikolai Babiev. At that time, Wrangel's forces totaled 43,900.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Kutepov was confident that there were no significant Bolshevik forces between Alexandrovsk, <a href="/wiki/Ekaterinoslav" class="mw-redirect" title="Ekaterinoslav">Ekaterinoslav</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kharkov" class="mw-redirect" title="Kharkov">Kharkov</a>, and felt he could take either town without great effort.  He expected to hold <a href="/wiki/Mariupol" title="Mariupol">Mariupol</a> that he had captured only long enough to destroy the Red naval base, capturing or killing all but 500 of the troops operating in the town.  Retreating from there, Kutepov drew Red forces north of Mariupol after him, and captured 4,000 Red troops on October 12, after outflanking them.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> After meeting with Second Army General Dratsenko, Koehler was optimistic about the prospects of the White army as late as early October. "To draw away the large reserves of Red troops now at <a href="/wiki/Kahovka" class="mw-redirect" title="Kahovka">Kahovka</a>, First Army has planned an advance from Alexandrovsk into the rear of <a href="/wiki/Nikopol,_Ukraine" title="Nikopol, Ukraine">Nikopol</a>, and as soon as the Reds have withdrawn from Nikopol, all Second Army except Vitkovsky's corps will cross (the <a href="/wiki/Dnieper_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Dnieper River">Dnieper River</a>) at Nikopol and advance towards <a href="/wiki/Berislav" title="Berislav">Berislav</a>, while Vitkovsky's 34th Division will attempt a crossing at the French <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastery</a> 25 <a href="/wiki/Verst" title="Verst">versts</a> below <a href="/wiki/Kahovka" class="mw-redirect" title="Kahovka">Kahovka</a>, and 13th Division ... will storm Kahovka... If this plan succeeds, the Russians will probably shift the whole First Army to the southeastern flank and attempt an advance into the <a href="/wiki/Donetz" class="mw-redirect" title="Donetz">Donetz</a>.... which if successful would mean the military power of bolshevism had come to an end."<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Russian_cavalry.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Russian_cavalry.jpg/300px-Russian_cavalry.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Russian_cavalry.jpg/450px-Russian_cavalry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Russian_cavalry.jpg/600px-Russian_cavalry.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3982" data-file-height="2886" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Russian_cavalry.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russian cossacks on the front. 1915</div></div></div><p>During a cavalry inspection, Koehler described witnessing the Cossack <i>lava</i> movement, "I had seen the <i>lava</i>, the famous Cossack attack, before, but never with the dash and spirit of [Second Brigade commander and <a href="/wiki/Don_Cossack" class="mw-redirect" title="Don Cossack">Don Cossack</a>], General P. M. Agaev. Forming his bodyguard and transport train into the "enemy", he sent them in one direction and his own force disappeared in a cloud of dust in the opposite direction. Thirty minutes later we noted, from our position on the top of an old <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tartar</a> <a href="/wiki/Burial_mound" class="mw-redirect" title="Burial mound">burial mound</a>, a mass of cavalry approaching in a solid formation.  This mass soon spread into a long, thin line, which came on at a gallop.  In the meantime, the enemy came and in a brilliant charge pierced the advancing line, which then split into two parts that retreated to the flanks, apparently in considerable disorder. At this point, the reserve hidden behind the advancing line suddenly came into sight, but on seeing the advancing enemy, it too wheeled and beat a hasty retreat with the enemy in hot pursuit. As the enemy pressed forward, the second reserve suddenly swept down and engaged the enemy in front, while the retreating first reserve, which had made a rather wide turn, now attacked on the enemy's flank while the two parts of the first line, which had retreated in apparent disorder, now came plunging in from the opposite flank and the rear, thus attacking the enemy from all sides at once.  The result was a melee of men and horses, sabres and lances, banners and streamers, a whirlwind of dust. If battle is half as dangerous as this maneuver appears, one wonders how there can be any enemies of Cossacks left." During the inspection of the Cavalry Corps, Koehler witnessed General Wigran's entire brigade, cavalry, artillery and machine gun detachments charge up the reviewing hillock, and led by the general, give a rousing cheer, "America, America".  Koehler learned it was the result of his giving a transcript to General Kutepov of a lengthy address by the Secretary of State that acknowledged the U.S. "strongly recoils [from] recognition of the Bolshevist regime," going on to say, the "United States feels that friendship and honor require that Russia's interests must be generously protected and... [we] have so instructed [our] representative in Southern Russia, Rear Admiral Newton A. McCully." During that time, Koehler heard a "great amount of favorable comment concerning this note and the work of the <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a>..."<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Russia_White_Volunteer_Army_infantry_1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Russia_White_Volunteer_Army_infantry_1920.jpg/300px-Russia_White_Volunteer_Army_infantry_1920.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Russia_White_Volunteer_Army_infantry_1920.jpg/450px-Russia_White_Volunteer_Army_infantry_1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Russia_White_Volunteer_Army_infantry_1920.jpg/600px-Russia_White_Volunteer_Army_infantry_1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="559" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Russia_White_Volunteer_Army_infantry_1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>White Volunteer Army infantry, ca. 1920</div></div></div><p>On October 6, Dratsenko's army crossed the Dnieper at <a href="/wiki/Khortitsa" class="mw-redirect" title="Khortitsa">Khortitsa</a>, to the surprise of the Reds who did not expect a crossing there, and giving a momentary advantage as Dratsenko's forces captured Red troops and threatened communication lines.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>  A couple days later, Koehler witnessed the crossing of the Dnieper by Kutepov with a shock force of three infantry and three cavalry divisions, writing in his report, "The crossing of the Dnieper on October eighth and the advance into the Ukraine was one of the most interesting operations I saw, as it perfectly illustrated General Kutepov's methods.  On the previous day I had seen the capture on Chertisa Island... former stronghold from which the old <a href="/wiki/Zaporogian_Cossacks" class="mw-redirect" title="Zaporogian Cossacks">Zaporogian Cossacks</a> directed raids against rich towns from Constantinople to Poland...At daybreak, as soon as the opposite shore could be made out, artillery began a brisk fire followed by all the noise the single machine gun company could produce. Twenty minutes later, at three of the most difficult places to get across- on account of the width and depth of the river-- the army began its crossing, Red artillery meanwhile pouring in an overwhelming fire on the ford being so carefully avoided.  Result was one division across at a cost of eleven men wounded and en entire cavalry corps and another division crossed with no casualties whatsoever.  Within forty minutes some nine hundred Bolshevik prisoners were busy hauling White Russian artillery across the river... I kept on with General Babiev's cavalry and by nightfall... counted over 3,000 prisoners..."<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Semyon_Budyonny%27s_Red_Army_1st_Cavalry.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/Semyon_Budyonny%27s_Red_Army_1st_Cavalry.jpg/300px-Semyon_Budyonny%27s_Red_Army_1st_Cavalry.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/Semyon_Budyonny%27s_Red_Army_1st_Cavalry.jpg/450px-Semyon_Budyonny%27s_Red_Army_1st_Cavalry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/Semyon_Budyonny%27s_Red_Army_1st_Cavalry.jpg/600px-Semyon_Budyonny%27s_Red_Army_1st_Cavalry.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1128" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Semyon_Budyonny%27s_Red_Army_1st_Cavalry.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Semyon_Budyonny" title="Semyon Budyonny">Semyon Budyonny</a>'s Red Army First Cavalry, ca. 1920</div></div></div><p>
Although peace negotiations between the Poles and Soviets only began on September 21, Wrangel knew he had to make his push across the Dnieper, realizing that Marshal <a href="/wiki/Semyon_Budyonny" title="Semyon Budyonny">Semyon Budyonny</a>'s <a href="/wiki/1st_Cavalry_Army" title="1st Cavalry Army">First Cavalry</a> would arrive from the Polish front to join with Red commander <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Frunze" title="Mikhail Frunze">Mikhail Frunze</a>'s <a href="/wiki/6th_Army_(RSFSR)" title="6th Army (RSFSR)">Sixth</a> and <a href="/wiki/13th_Army_(RSFSR)" title="13th Army (RSFSR)">Thirteenth Armies</a> and <a href="/wiki/2nd_Cavalry_Army" title="2nd Cavalry Army">Second Cavalry</a>, headquartered at <a href="/wiki/Kharkov" class="mw-redirect" title="Kharkov">Kharkov</a>, dramatically increasing the numerical advantage of the Reds.  As observed by Koehler, the White army's operation came "within an ace of success" but it was not to be. After the capture of 3,000 Reds on October 11, Babiev's cavalry outflanked the thorn of Kahovka while Wrangel's First Army under Kutepov north of Melitopol cut elements of the Soviet Sixth and Thirteenth armies to ribbons. Then key events conspired to doom the operation.  On October 13, Wrangel ordered a frontal assault by General Vitkovsky on Kahovka.  Inaccurate aerial reconnaissance reported sparse defenses; however, the city was heavily defended and Vitovsky's tanks were destroyed.  As Babiev's Cossacks were about to surround the city, he was killed by an artillery shell, thoroughly confounding and demoralizing his Cossacks, who began to retreat. This, in turn, caused the inept Second Army commander, Dratsenko, to order a general retreat back to the left bank of the Dnieper.  When the Whites regrouped on the left bank, they found that the Markov and <a href="/wiki/Lavr_Kornilov" title="Lavr Kornilov">Kornilov</a> divisions, General Babiev and his cavalry, Barbovich's corps, and the Sixth and Seventh Infantry divisions had been crushed and abandoned on the right bank.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> On top of these failures, news reached Wrangel that the Poles and Soviets had signed a peace treaty the previous day, October 12.  Wrangel's forces began a steady retreat and any designs on spreading into the Ukraine were finished. Koehler returned to Sebastopol on October 17, as once again, thousands of beaten and despondent Whites retreated toward the narrow Perekop Isthmus and Sivash straights that separated Crimea from the northern Tauride.  On the day of the Red's first offensive, October 28, they had 99,500 infantry and 33,700 cavalry to the White's 23,000 infantry and 12,000 cavalry- nearly a 4 to 1 advantage. They also outnumbered the Whites 2 to 1 in guns and machine guns. In a matter of days, the Reds reached the Tauride. Wrangel ordered all available men to defend Perekop and ordered Kutepov to return and attack from the West. Kutepov's forces did as ordered, but when a Red force broke through on the Ushun sector, Kutepov withdrew into Crimea rather than risk encirclement.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Russian_army_exodus_from_Crimea_1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/White_Russian_army_exodus_from_Crimea_1920.jpg/300px-White_Russian_army_exodus_from_Crimea_1920.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/White_Russian_army_exodus_from_Crimea_1920.jpg/450px-White_Russian_army_exodus_from_Crimea_1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/White_Russian_army_exodus_from_Crimea_1920.jpg/600px-White_Russian_army_exodus_from_Crimea_1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Russian_army_exodus_from_Crimea_1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>White army exodus from Crimea, ca. 1920</div></div></div>
<p>A week later, Koehler was in Yalta with McCully who wrote in his diary, "Summer is definitely over, although Koehler still goes in swimming." Wrangel had hoped that the tidal marshes at the neck of the Crimea would hold back the Bolsheviks, but a series of very low tides drained many and a cold spell froze over the rest, so that the Reds poured over, sending his remaining forces into full retreat to <a href="/wiki/Sebastopol" class="mw-redirect" title="Sebastopol">Sebastopol</a> and a desperate <a href="/wiki/Rearguard" title="Rearguard">rearguard action</a>. He described the end of his campaign, "I gave the order in October 1920 for retreat. The troops fell back by forced marches on the seaports and embarked according to a plan previously arranged. The civil population, those who served in the rear, the sick, the wounded, women and children, were the first to be put on board. The evacuation took place in perfect order. I inspected personally on the Cruiser <a href="/wiki/Russian_cruiser_Admiral_Kornilov_(1887)" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian cruiser Admiral Kornilov (1887)">Kornilov</a> the harbours used, and I was able to assure myself that all who wished to quit Russian soil found it possible to do so."<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>  At the time of the collapse of the White army, <a href="/wiki/Mustang_(military_officer)" title="Mustang (military officer)">"mustang"</a> rear admiral and early naval aviator <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Tate" title="Jackson Tate">Jackson Tate</a> was a "lowly ensign" attached to the <a href="/wiki/USS_Borie_(DD-215)" title="USS Borie (DD-215)">USS&#160;<i>Borie</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(DD-215)</span></a> off Sebastopol. He recalled writing a set of orders for himself "to escort the Princess Olga Sargieff Rostigieff, Rear Admiral McCully's secretary, out of the Crimea," a well-laid plan, until Koehler got wind of it. "Hugo Koehler tore up the orders and said HE was escorting the princess and I was assigned to escort out three children on the [destroyer] <a href="/wiki/USS_Overton_(DD-239)" title="USS Overton (DD-239)">USS&#160;<i>Overton</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(DD-239)</span></a>. There were over three thousand people aboard, mostly standing on deck. The children were not at all popular with the C.O. of the destroyer. They- and most of the refugees- had "<a href="/wiki/Cooties" title="Cooties">cooties</a>", the nickname at that time for body lice... I saw little of Koehler. He was a very dapper and dashing individual and quite a lady's man. He spoke excellent Russian and was very highly thought of by Admiral McCully."<sup id="cite_ref-Capelotti._p._168_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capelotti._p._168-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Sebastopol.2C_November_1920"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sebastopol,_November_1920">Sebastopol, November 1920</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sebastopol, November 1920">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Evacuation_of_Wrangel%27s_White_Army_from_Crimea_November_1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/The_Evacuation_of_Wrangel%27s_White_Army_from_Crimea_November_1920.jpg/300px-The_Evacuation_of_Wrangel%27s_White_Army_from_Crimea_November_1920.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/The_Evacuation_of_Wrangel%27s_White_Army_from_Crimea_November_1920.jpg/450px-The_Evacuation_of_Wrangel%27s_White_Army_from_Crimea_November_1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/The_Evacuation_of_Wrangel%27s_White_Army_from_Crimea_November_1920.jpg/600px-The_Evacuation_of_Wrangel%27s_White_Army_from_Crimea_November_1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="820" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Evacuation_of_Wrangel%27s_White_Army_from_Crimea_November_1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soldiers and cavalry of Wrangel's White army evacuate from Sebastopol, November 1920</div></div></div><p> His work on the Mission to South Russia completed, on November 1, 1920, Koehler sailed for <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>. After that, he made a yachting trip to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>.  The destroyer <a href="/wiki/USS_Overton_(DD-239)" title="USS Overton (DD-239)"><i>Overton</i></a>, was already at Sebastopol when the Bolsheviks defeated Baron Wrangel's White Army in November 1920. <i>Overton</i> distributed relief supplies, provided transportation and communication services, and relocated refugees. A single ship could not cope with the massive influx of desperate people fleeing the fighting; however, and the Americans furthermore feared for the safety of their people trapped in the war. McCully cabled a request to the State Department, asking that the United States be allowed to assist in the <a href="/wiki/Evacuation_of_the_Crimea_(1920)" title="Evacuation of the Crimea (1920)">Evacuation of the Crimea</a>. Not waiting for a response, he ordered Cdr. Alexander "Sandy" Sharp of the destroyer <a href="/wiki/USS_John_D._Edwards_(DD-216)" class="mw-redirect" title="USS John D. Edwards (DD-216)">USS&#160;<i>John D. Edwards</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(DD-216)</span></a> to load a group of refugees and stand out for Constantinople with a letter request to Admiral Bristol for more ships.  With 550 Cossacks, women and children gathered from the Sebastopol docks, Sharp's destroyer stood out for Turkey.  During the voyage, the Russians were spread out on the ship and ravenously wolfed down everything the navy cooks served up. Upon receiving the letter, Bristol gave emergency orders for the destroyers <a href="/wiki/USS_Fox_(DD-234)" title="USS Fox (DD-234)">USS&#160;<i>Fox</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(DD-234)</span></a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_Humphreys_(DD-236)" title="USS Humphreys (DD-236)">USS&#160;<i>Humphreys</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(DD-236)</span></a>, and <a href="/wiki/USS_Whipple_(DD-217)" title="USS Whipple (DD-217)">USS&#160;<i>Whipple</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(DD-217)</span></a> to cease various operations in the Black Sea and immediately stand out for the Crimea to assist in the evacuation. The Americans nonetheless required additional ships and cabled for reinforcements including <a href="/wiki/USS_St._Louis_(C-20)" title="USS St. Louis (C-20)">USS&#160;<i>St. Louis</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(C-20)</span></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Whipple_in_drydock_and_her_captain,_LCDR_Richard_F._Bernard_USN.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/USS_Whipple_in_drydock_and_her_captain%2C_LCDR_Richard_F._Bernard_USN.jpg/300px-USS_Whipple_in_drydock_and_her_captain%2C_LCDR_Richard_F._Bernard_USN.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/USS_Whipple_in_drydock_and_her_captain%2C_LCDR_Richard_F._Bernard_USN.jpg/450px-USS_Whipple_in_drydock_and_her_captain%2C_LCDR_Richard_F._Bernard_USN.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/USS_Whipple_in_drydock_and_her_captain%2C_LCDR_Richard_F._Bernard_USN.jpg/600px-USS_Whipple_in_drydock_and_her_captain%2C_LCDR_Richard_F._Bernard_USN.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="643" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Whipple_in_drydock_and_her_captain,_LCDR_Richard_F._Bernard_USN.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/USS_Whipple_(DD-217)" title="USS Whipple (DD-217)">USS&#160;<i>Whipple</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(DD-217)</span></a> in drydock at Constantinople, with her captain, Lt. Cdr. Richard F. Bernard USN (left)</div></div></div><p>  Standing up the <a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a> from Constantinople on November 13, 1920, <i>St. Louis</i> rendezvoused with destroyer <a href="/wiki/USS_Long_(DD-209)" class="mw-redirect" title="USS Long (DD-209)">USS&#160;<i>Long</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(DD-209)</span></a>, and U.S. steamships <i>Faraby</i> and <i>Navahoe</i>, and the four vessels worked with other ships to rescue the Americans authorized by McCully to escape. Besides McCully's party, the ships pulled out U.S. consuls and their archives, representatives of the American Red Cross and <a href="/wiki/Y.M.C.A." class="mw-redirect" title="Y.M.C.A.">Y.M.C.A.</a>, relief workers from other agencies, and approximately 1,400 Russian refugees from Sebastopol and Yalta in the Crimea, Novorossiysk, Russia, and Odessa.  On the morning of November 14, the destroyers dispatched by Admiral Bristol from Constantinople arrived at Sebastopol to find hundreds of mostly Russian and French boats in the harbor, many of which were crammed to the gunwales with fleeing White Russians. <i>Whipple</i>, Lt. Cdr. Richard F. Bernard, commanding, stood by to evacuate selected individuals bearing passes from McCully. <i>Whipple's</i> main battery was trained out and manned at all times. Armed boat crews carried evacuees out to the ship while her landing force stood in readiness. As her last boatload pushed off from shore, Bolshevik troops reached the main square and began firing on the fleeing White Russians. Whipple was the last American vessel out of Sebastopol, towing a barge loaded with wounded White Russian troops. When she was beyond range of the Red guns, she turned the tow over to <i>Humphreys</i>. Passing Admiral McCully's destroyer, he bellowed from the bridge through a <a href="/wiki/Megaphone" title="Megaphone">megaphone</a>, to Bernard and his crew, "Well done <i>Whipple</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> General Wrangel stayed on a <a href="/wiki/Quay" class="mw-redirect" title="Quay">quay</a> until all who wanted to leave Russia had done so, before he embarked on the <a href="/wiki/Russian_cruiser_Admiral_Kornilov_(1887)" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian cruiser Admiral Kornilov (1887)">Russian cruiser Admiral Kornilov (1887)</a> and sailed into exile, eventually settling in Belgium. It is estimated that between November 1920 and the end of 1921, the Reds executed between 50,000 and 150,000 Russians in the Crimea.<sup id="cite_ref-Capelotti._p._168_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capelotti._p._168-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> <i>St. Louis</i> returned her evacuees to Constantinople on November 16. The following day, her crew formed boat landing parties to distribute food among refugees quartered on board naval transports anchored in the Bosporus.  General Wrangel moved nearly 150,000 Russian refugees and crewmen on board 80 former Imperial Russian Black Sea Fleet ships and merchantmen into exile, initially to the harbor at Constantinople. Lt. Hamilton V. Bryan supervised sailors and marines, including those from <i>St. Louis</i>, who helped care for these people, until many of the exiles sailed for <a href="/wiki/Bizerte,_Tunisia" class="mw-redirect" title="Bizerte, Tunisia">Bizerte, Tunisia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_and_his_seven_adopted_Russian_children.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ab/Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_and_his_seven_adopted_Russian_children.jpg/300px-Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_and_his_seven_adopted_Russian_children.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ab/Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_and_his_seven_adopted_Russian_children.jpg/450px-Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_and_his_seven_adopted_Russian_children.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ab/Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_and_his_seven_adopted_Russian_children.jpg/600px-Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_and_his_seven_adopted_Russian_children.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1169" data-file-height="943" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rear_Admiral_Newton_McCully_and_his_seven_adopted_Russian_children.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rear Adm. Newton McCully and his seven adopted Russian children, with Navy Secretary Josephus Daniels, January 1921</div></div></div><p>During his ten months in Russia, Rear Admiral Newton McCully had seen great suffering among the children in South Russia, particularly those that were orphans.  He decided to adopt seven of them, and would have taken a dozen more if he could have.  Navy Secretary <a href="/wiki/Josephus_Daniels" title="Josephus Daniels">Josephus Daniels</a> referred to McCully's benevolence as "the big act of a big man with a big heart". McCully left Yalta on <i>Overton</i>, the last U.S. ship to leave the Crimea; his orphans had departed earlier on another destroyer. Over the next few weeks, McCully again tried to no avail to get the State Department to offer asylum to additional Russians. Travelling home with his young charges and their 29-year old governess on the oiler <a href="/wiki/USS_Ramapo_(AO-12)" title="USS Ramapo (AO-12)">USS&#160;<i>Ramapo</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(AO-12)</span></a>, McCully wrote in his diary, "We are off on the biggest adventure I ever undertook- an old bachelor with seven children." The admiral was required to post a $5,000 bond for each child, while they were detained at <a href="/wiki/Ellis_Island" title="Ellis Island">Ellis Island</a>. Ultimately, the admiral conquered the bureaucracy and as a solution to filling "the big job of supplying the necessary feminine influence in their lives", the admiral married their governess.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Admiral_Mark_L._Bristol.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Admiral_Mark_L._Bristol.jpg/250px-Admiral_Mark_L._Bristol.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="342" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Admiral_Mark_L._Bristol.jpg/375px-Admiral_Mark_L._Bristol.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Admiral_Mark_L._Bristol.jpg/500px-Admiral_Mark_L._Bristol.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1076" data-file-height="1472" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Admiral_Mark_L._Bristol.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Admiral Mark L. Bristol</div></div></div><p>In Constantinople, the city was deluged with the displaced Russians from the Crimea. Admiral Mark Bristol set up a disaster relief committee to coordinate the various American relief efforts that continued for many months. Sailors from the admiral's flagship, <i>St Louis</i>, worked with his wife, Helen, and her committee of women and constructed a soup kitchen in the train station at <a href="/wiki/Stamboul" class="mw-redirect" title="Stamboul">Stamboul</a>.  They fed 4,000 refugees daily that were being brought in for housing in makeshift camps.  Restroom facilities and a dressing area for the women refugees were set up. Doling out hot chocolate, tea and bread, Helen Bristol and her women, worked in rain and mud to keep their canteen open past midnight. Ultimately 22,000 Russians were cared for by the Americans and housed in different parts of the city.  Much larger contingents of British and French aid workers did similar good.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
Appropriately, Koehler returned to the United States from Europe aboard the <a href="/wiki/RMS_Aquitania" title="RMS Aquitania">RMS&#160;<i>Aquitania</i></a>, nicknamed "Ship Beautiful" for the society passengers she carried, sailing from <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a> and arriving at New York with 3,000 passengers on January 30, 1921, after a rough six-day, eleven-hour passage averaging 20 knots.  Also making the crossing were Sir <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Shackleton" title="Ernest Shackleton">Ernest Shackleton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctic" title="Antarctic">Antarctic</a> explorer and author, Sir <a href="/wiki/Philip_Gibbs" title="Philip Gibbs">Philip Gibbs</a>, British <a href="/wiki/War_correspondent" title="War correspondent">war correspondent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vice_Admiral" class="mw-redirect" title="Vice Admiral">Vice Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/Harry_M._P._Huse" title="Harry M. P. Huse">Harry M. P. Huse</a>, USN, Sir <a href="/wiki/Newton_Moore" title="Newton Moore">Major-General Sir Newton James Moore KCMG</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clare_Sheridan" title="Clare Sheridan">Clare Sheridan</a>, sculptress and first cousin of Sir <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, who had been in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> sculpting <a href="/wiki/Bust_(sculpture)" title="Bust (sculpture)">busts</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Lenin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leon_Trotsky" title="Leon Trotsky">Trotsky</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The British Foreign Office had loudly opposed Sheridan's trip to Russia, denying her a British passport and almost forbidding the journey.  Koehler met her on the ship as she was sailing to America to give lectures on her Russian experience.  On the first day of her arrival in New York, Koehler took care of her young son, Dick, while she met with reporters, agents and others.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clare_Sheridan,_Hugo_Koehler_and_others_aboard_Aquitania1921.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Clare_Sheridan%2C_Hugo_Koehler_and_others_aboard_Aquitania1921.jpg/250px-Clare_Sheridan%2C_Hugo_Koehler_and_others_aboard_Aquitania1921.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="363" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Clare_Sheridan%2C_Hugo_Koehler_and_others_aboard_Aquitania1921.jpg/375px-Clare_Sheridan%2C_Hugo_Koehler_and_others_aboard_Aquitania1921.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Clare_Sheridan%2C_Hugo_Koehler_and_others_aboard_Aquitania1921.jpg/500px-Clare_Sheridan%2C_Hugo_Koehler_and_others_aboard_Aquitania1921.jpg 2x" data-file-width="953" data-file-height="1385" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clare_Sheridan,_Hugo_Koehler_and_others_aboard_Aquitania1921.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Clare_Sheridan" title="Clare Sheridan">Clare Sheridan</a>, Hugo Koehler (holding up her son, Dick), <a href="/wiki/Harry_M._P._Huse" title="Harry M. P. Huse">Rear Adm. Harry Huse</a> and others, aboard <a href="/wiki/RMS_Aquitania" title="RMS Aquitania">RMS&#160;<i>Aquitania</i></a>, bound for <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> from <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a>, January 1921</div></div></div><p> In her diary, <i>Mayfair to Moscow</i>, published in 1921, Sheridan described Koehler as "heaven sent", writing that "Koehler promised to be at my side in case of need. He was amazingly kind and put up with infinite boredom and waiting on our account."<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> In a letter Koehler wrote from Poland in 1922, he mentioned an incident that reminded him of the "famous Mrs. Sheridan at whom the Foreign Office thundered so loudly... and yet be it known (although this, of course, is closely guarded) that she was an agent for the British Intelligence Service."<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was speculated, but later disproved, that Koehler may have been able to smuggle the Imperial family out of Russia.  This was the subject of the book <i>Rescue of the Romanovs</i> by New York journalist Guy Richards, that was published in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Beginning in July 1921, the monthly periodical <a href="/wiki/The_World%27s_Work" title="The World&#39;s Work">The World's Work</a> published a series of four articles by Hugo Koehler describing his observations during the Russian Civil War in 1920 and the defeat of the anti-Bolshevists. Koehler's belief that the Russian people would soon work through and cast off Bolshevism for a prosperous, free-market economy, while in line with the pro-business theme of the host publication, proved to be overly optimistic.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Naval_Attach.C3.A9_to_Poland"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_Attaché_to_Poland">Naval Attaché to Poland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Naval Attaché to Poland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>
