Koehler was promoted to permanent lieutenant commander on June 3, 1921, and appointed naval attaché to the American <a href="/wiki/Legation" title="Legation">Legation</a> at <a href="/wiki/Warsaw,_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="Warsaw, Poland">Warsaw, Poland</a>, in August of that year, to aid the State Department in monitoring the beginning of international relations between divided-Russia and Poland.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Koehler described the practical aspect of his job, as spending a year "combing <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> from one end to the other with no purpose more definite than to see what's really happening on the theory that might give us some idea of what's going to happen." His passport, signed by <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Evans_Hughes" title="Charles Evans Hughes">Charles E. Hughes</a> contained over sixty <a href="/wiki/Travel_visa" title="Travel visa">visas</a> stamped during his assignment.  From 1795 to 1918 and the collapse of its neighboring nations, Poland had been variously controlled by <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Russia">Imperial Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>. The new republic of Poland that was outlined by President Wilson in the thirteenth of his <a href="/wiki/Fourteen_Points" title="Fourteen Points">Fourteen Points</a> in January 1918, and agreed at <a href="/wiki/Versailles_Peace_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Versailles Peace Treaty">Versailles</a> the following year, included parts of the former Russia, the <a href="/wiki/Congress_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Congress Kingdom">Congress Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kresy" title="Kresy">Kresy</a>. The diverse ethnic groups of White Russians, <a href="/wiki/Lithuanians" title="Lithuanians">Lithuanians</a>, Poles and <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians" title="Ukrainians">Ukrainians</a>, along with small concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> made up the new country.  The area of the new state ceded from Germany included the agrarian regions of <a href="/wiki/Pomeranian_Voivodeship_(1919%E2%80%931939)" title="Pomeranian Voivodeship (1919–1939)">Pomerania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Posen" title="Province of Posen">Posen</a>, plus the industrial regions of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Silesia" title="Upper Silesia">Prussian Upper Silesia</a>.  From <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a> areas of <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Eastern_Europe)" title="Galicia (Eastern Europe)">West Galicia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silesian_Voivodeship_(1920%E2%80%9339)" title="Silesian Voivodeship (1920–39)">Austrian Silesia</a> were designated for the new Polish state.  The goal of establishing a unified nation from these diverse regions, with differing taxes, varying public education (to the extent it existed) and transportation infrastructure, was compounded by the destruction of six years of war on both cities and farmlands.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1921.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/22/Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1921.jpg/300px-Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1921.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/22/Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1921.jpg/450px-Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1921.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/22/Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1921.jpg 2x" data-file-width="552" data-file-height="445" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_W._Koehler_in_1921.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hugo W. Koehler in 1921</div></div></div>
<p>In a letter to Capt. William Galbraith of the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Naval_Intelligence" title="Office of Naval Intelligence">Office of Naval Intelligence</a> in early November, 1921, Koehler wrote of driving from Warsaw to Danzig, where he "passed in and out of Germany and Poland so often that we soon lost count of the number of frontier stations."  Motoring from battle-scarred, <a href="/wiki/Brest-Litovsk" class="mw-redirect" title="Brest-Litovsk">Brest-Litovsk</a> he wrote that, "The most interesting thing there was the scrawl on the wall of the room where the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Brest-Litovsk" title="Treaty of Brest-Litovsk">Bolshevik treaty with Germany</a> was signed. 'Neither Peace, nor War.' it reads, and is signed 'Leon Trotksy, December 1917, Brest'- certainly not a bad estimate of the situation, especially so, considering the date."  Heading on towards <a href="/wiki/Baranowicze" class="mw-redirect" title="Baranowicze">Baranowicze</a>, Koehler's party travelled through miles of burned land, for which he observed that,  "[I]n accordance with age-old Russian strategy the armies laid waste the countryside as they retreated... Wonderfully thorough they were, these poor Russians, in destroying their own houses and fields."<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Arriving at the border, they found "a long line of prairie schooners coming in. 'Is this Poland? Are we out of Russia? God be praised!' came again and again in the same jabbering Russian, Polish and German.  It happened that in this crowd were German colonists from Russia. They had been in Russia for many generations, they had never mixed with the Russians but kept close together in their little farm colonies along the <a href="/wiki/Volga_River" title="Volga River">Volga</a>.... I talked to one peasant who was under twenty-seven although he looked over forty- and  small wonder, for he had tramped over five thousand versts and during the last stretch had dragged the cart himself, as the Bolsheviks had taken away his horse. I examined the cart he had brought all this distance and with so tremendous an effort. An old bed, bits of sacking, an assortment of battered pots and pans, an old sheepskin, part of a wolfskin, rags, nine potatoes, a handful of radishes, some pieces of tallow- nothing else. The complete inventory would not net a dollar; he explained that he had had a cow but the Bolsheviks had taken it away from him at the border. Yet he was much better off than thousands, for having been out in the open he was in comparatively good health although drawn and wasted, whereas the others who had come back by train were feeble and diseased and bleeding from bites. The children of course, were the most tragic sights.  They are young only when they smile."<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
In his travels through Germany, when Koehler met German women with babies, he would ask them if they were raising them to be <i>canonenfutter</i> (cannon fodder).  Invariably, they would respond, " 'No,' indignantly.  But on further talking, they would just as invariably intimate that these boys would have to someday fight as their fathers did, because the French would not let them do otherwise.<i>Frankreich muss noch weinen</i>." ("The French will be made to weep again"), Koehler heard over, and over.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> He saw that Germany was selling its goods below the actual cost of production, and that while full employment might postpone the "crash", when it came it would be harder due to Germany's weak financial condition.  "To pull through, Germany will need statesmanship wiser than the greed of manufacturers that has been the directing force since the Armistice. As it is, the German people seem blighted with the curse of false leaders, for the interests now at the helm, though more greedy than the old, are less farsighted; and again the German people will suffer for their lack of ability in choosing leaders...." A French officer travelling with Koehler for part of his journey, expressed bitterness seeing "all the smiling fields, and all the beautiful villages, and all the sturdy Germans working so industriously, all the busy factories at <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">Düsseldorf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Essen" title="Essen">Essen</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ruhr" title="Ruhr">Ruhr</a>..." while "he thought of devastated northern France."  The sight of the German resolve and energy "struck terror to the soul of my French friend," prompting Koehler's conclusion that, "The tragedy is not only that the French do not realize that by keeping an army beyond their means they are weakening themselves, but the fear and terror that they all feel makes it impossible for them even to understand it until that terror is removed."<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_Naval_Attache_Passport.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_Naval_Attache_Passport.jpg/300px-Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_Naval_Attache_Passport.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_Naval_Attache_Passport.jpg/450px-Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_Naval_Attache_Passport.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_Naval_Attache_Passport.jpg/600px-Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_Naval_Attache_Passport.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5017" data-file-height="2993" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_Naval_Attache_Passport.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lt. Cdr. Hugo Koehler's Diplomatic Passport Application, Naval Attaché to Poland, August 1921</div></div></div>
<p>Journeying through Poland, Koehler engaged in discussions of the viability of the new Polish republic.  "And over and over again one hears it stated that if there had been any real vitality in the idea of a Polish nationality, the Poles would long ago have thrown off the yoke of the oppressors they hated so bitterly.  But however sound these statements may be in themselves, I do not find they quite fit the Polish situation. It is all very well to say that a country should itself throw off the yoke if it aspires to nationhood, but when all is said and done, once Poland was partitioned, it was really impossible for Poland to rise until at least one of the oppressors had fallen. It is true that a country must itself achieve its independence, since the fundamental character of independence is that it cannot be received as a gift.... Poland has been reborn and has a fine start in life, but whether or not Poland will ever arrive at manhood depends entirely on the Poles. There is no royal road to knowledge, we were told as children, nor is there any royal road to manhood or statehood."  Koehler recalled the "feeling in General Wrangel's army when the Poles were advancing to <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">Kiev</a>.  Although Wrangel's men were fighting for existence against the Bolsheviks, in the hardest and bitterest kind of fight, still there was a time, at the height of the Polish advance, when the feeling in Wrangel's army was such that they would almost have made common cause with the Bolsheviks rather than see the Poles advance into Russia."<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a letter to his mother, Mathilda, written in July 1922, Koehler recounted that, "In the forest region there are many Jews in the little villages that here consist usually of a single row of houses around a square instead of the single, very broad street of the Russian style.  I heard many complaints against the Jews: that they did no real work, no farming, no cutting of wood, yet they became rich on the labors of the peasants. 'If they do not farm and do not cut wood, then what do the Jews do?' I asked a peasant. 'Oh, they buy stolen logs and trade stolen horses and sell vodka,' was the answer. 'You wicked <a href="/wiki/Anti-Semitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Semitic">anti-Semitic</a> <a href="/wiki/Propagandist" class="mw-redirect" title="Propagandist">propagandist</a>!' I reproached him; but his only reply was that just nine days before, his brother's horse had been stolen and found at a Jew's house seventeen versts away, and that currently everybody knew that Jews bought the stolen logs and cattle as well as the horses. 'But the peasants must steal the logs first in order to sell them to the Jews,' I answered. 'Yes,' he admitted, 'but if the Jews did not buy, the peasants would not steal.'  I told him the parable about the pot and the kettle, but he was not impressed."<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Koehler recalled a particularly humorous encounter with frontier posts and visa stamps that again demonstrated his prodigious ability to persuade and manipulate.  Motoring to <a href="/wiki/Kovno" class="mw-redirect" title="Kovno">Kovno</a>, about ninety minutes after crossing the <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuanian</a> frontier and a small army post where Koehler's <a href="/wiki/Retinue" title="Retinue">retinue</a> had asked directions, they came upon a line of soldiers with <a href="/wiki/Bayoneted" class="mw-redirect" title="Bayoneted">bayoneted</a> rifles gesturing urgently.  An officer approached and told them they would need to go back to the frontier outpost for Koehler to have his passport stamped. Not wanting to backtrack over "very bad roads" and delay the trip for this bureaucratic exercise, Koehler "refused point-blank to go back but added that I would be glad to go to any station in the direction of Kovno, my destination."  Ultimately taking the phone himself, he "talked to various regimental commanders, divisional commanders, and goodness knows what, all of whom repeated that the frontier was closed inasmuch as Poland and Lithuania were at war, and that I could therefore not have passed the frontier.  In reply I suggested that the mere fact I was in the middle of Lithuania ought to be sufficient evidence that I had crossed the frontier.  I replied that I would not go willingly and that under the circumstances I considered that the same <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a> that applied to <a href="/wiki/Blockade" title="Blockade">blockades</a> also applied to a frontier; that is, that just as a blockade in order to be binding must be effective, so it was with a frontier."  Standing his ground, Koehler's suggestion that he should be allowed to proceed to divisional headquarters (on the way to Kovno) was finally agreed to.  Reaching divisional headquarters, "the first result was more discussions, telephoning, arguing, and beseeching. But the <a href="/wiki/Upshot" class="mw-redirect" title="Upshot">upshot</a> of it all was that instead of proceeding to Kovno, I was told I should have to wait where I was until the Lithuanian Foreign Office had authorized the visa of my passport... However, as this process promised to take a good many hours if not days and weeks, and as the discussion had already lasted some four and a half hours, I cast about for a happy idea." Koehler matter of factly suggested  to the general's <a href="/wiki/Adjutant" title="Adjutant">adjutant</a> that he had only heard the Polish side of the controversy with Lithuania, and that it might be very interesting on the journey to Kovno to have "a really thorough explanation of the Lithuanian side of the case. The effect of this gentle hint was electric! The adjutant dashed off to his colonel, and within three minutes the answer came back that I could proceed at once to Kovno, and that, if I wished it, an officer would accompany me and show me the way." Koehler arrived at Kovno without further incident.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_in_the_World%27s_Work_magazine,_1921.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1d/Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_in_the_World%27s_Work_magazine%2C_1921.jpg/250px-Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_in_the_World%27s_Work_magazine%2C_1921.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="322" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1d/Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_in_the_World%27s_Work_magazine%2C_1921.jpg/375px-Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_in_the_World%27s_Work_magazine%2C_1921.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1d/Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_in_the_World%27s_Work_magazine%2C_1921.jpg/500px-Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_in_the_World%27s_Work_magazine%2C_1921.jpg 2x" data-file-width="936" data-file-height="1204" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lt._Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_in_the_World%27s_Work_magazine,_1921.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lt. Cdr. Hugo Koehler, pictured in the <i>World's Work</i> magazine in 1921</div></div></div><p>While he was in Poland, it is likely that Koehler coordinated with Polish General <a href="/wiki/Jozef_Pilsudski" class="mw-redirect" title="Jozef Pilsudski">Jozef Pilsudski</a>, the dominant leader in the newly independent Polish state, to support the White Army in Russia in its unsuccessful struggle against the Red Army.  In this assignment he met the <a href="/wiki/Papal_Nuncios" class="mw-redirect" title="Papal Nuncios">Papal Nuncios</a> to Poland and Germany, Archbishops Ambrogio Damiano Achille Ratti and Eugenio Maria Giuseppe Giovanni Pacelli.  The unfounded speculation surrounding these meetings, was that the Vatican and Koehler were discussing the potential for restoration of the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburg">Habsburg</a> empire, relative to the rumor that Koehler was the "lost" crown prince. The following year, Ratti became <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XI" title="Pope Pius XI">Pope Pius XI</a>, and on his death in 1939, Pacelli was elected to the papacy, taking the name <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XII" title="Pope Pius XII">Pope Pius XII</a>.  Years after Koehler's death, his widow Matilda told a <a href="/wiki/Confidant" title="Confidant">confidante</a>, "I think it was a shame how they got his hopes up."<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> In February 1922, Koehler was witness for his friend <a href="/wiki/Hugh_S._Gibson" title="Hugh S. Gibson">Hugh S. Gibson</a>, American minister to Poland, at Gibson's wedding to Ynès Reyntiens, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Belgian" class="mw-redirect" title="Belgian">Belgian</a> Major Robert Reyntiens, who had been <a href="/wiki/Aide-de-camp" title="Aide-de-camp">Aide-de-camp</a> to <a href="/wiki/King_Leopold_II" class="mw-redirect" title="King Leopold II">King Leopold II</a>, in Bruxelles.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>  During the week of October 15, 1922, American diplomatic representatives in Middle and Western Europe, including Koehler, met in Berlin at a conference called by the U.S. Ambassador to Germany, <a href="/wiki/Alanson_B._Houghton" title="Alanson B. Houghton">Alanson B. Houghton</a>, to discuss the current state of affairs in their particular countries and work towards more cohesive cooperation in their missions.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Two days before his assassination on December 16, 1922, Koehler's personal friend, the newly elected, first Polish president <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Narutowicz" title="Gabriel Narutowicz">Gabriel Narutowicz</a> had expressed great interest to him in the work done in America to establish a national park system, and asked Koehler for information concerning policies and methods, with a view towards establishing a system in Poland patterned after the <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a> in order to preserve Poland's extensive forests, and in particular, those in the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian Mountains</a>. Narutowicz asked Koehler to prepare articles for publication in Poland concerning the American national parks.<sup id="cite_ref-EpUmAQAAMAAJ_1922._p._216_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EpUmAQAAMAAJ_1922._p._216-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Returning to the United States several days later, on leave before his next assignment, Koehler visited his mother, Mathilda, in Davenport, Iowa, during the <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a> holidays and gave an interview to the local paper. Titled "Worse Times Coming in Germany", Koehler saw Germany at the beginning of a period of economic <a href="/wiki/Deflation" title="Deflation">deflation</a> following the hyperinflation that had existed since the Armistice.  While the current economic situation made it doubtful there would be any significant rise in exports to Europe, Koehler believed there was great future trade expansion potential since the old and new European nations did not believe the U.S. was "animated by ulterior motives".  He noted that America loans money to those nations, not "loans" in the form of imported goods made in Britain as the British did. He saw Germany paying the price of Anglo-French political maneuvers in the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, where the English were defeated in their diplomatic backing of Greece, as the French were victors in their backing of Turkey.  That victory gave the French the courage to insist on their strong army policy towards the Germans.  The balance of trade that was formerly in favor of Germany, was now against it, which augured ill for German prosperity.  He reaffirmed his belief that Russia "will eventually escape domination of the bolshevik" but saw no possibility of ever reverting to a monarchy, since the peasants, who had "appropriated many things in addition to land" would resist it.  Koehler saw Poland as "economically wealthy" with its tremendous natural resources of timber, oil, coal and mining, but "financially poor" for supporting a very large standing army and a close alliance with France. Politically, he regarded the situation in Poland as "perplexing", with 17 political parties, and strong political minorities of Germans, Russians, Jews and Ukrainians. He noted that 90 percent of the emigration from Poland was Jewish. Koehler opined that while all European nations worried over the invasion threat of the <a href="/wiki/Red_army" class="mw-redirect" title="Red army">Red army</a>, they were "bluffed" since it was defensively, not offensively strong. "The Russian army has no artillery, no aviation force, no means of supply and while it could defend Russia effectively it could not campaign efficiently in an aggressive way," he stated. Commenting about "The Terrible Turk", Koehler felt it wasn't so "terrible" after all. "There isn't any danger in the present alliance between the Turk and the Bolshevik as far as a permanent threat against the peace of Europe is concerned. <a href="/wiki/Mohammedanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohammedanism">Mohammedanism</a> is fundamentally opposed to <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">Communism</a> and therefore these two countries cannot remain friendly," Koehler accurately predicted. "Communism is a good excuse for a row but a poor foundation for a stable government," he declared.  The recent assassination of the Polish president, Narutowicz, a Lithuanian by birth who had been conciliatory in resolving the boundary dispute with Lithuania that followed the Poles taking of <a href="/wiki/Vilna" class="mw-redirect" title="Vilna">Vilna</a> was a serious blow to the country's future, Koehler believed. "America has done an enormous amount of good for Poland and for other new countries of Europe in its relief work, work that will eventually react to the benefit of the United States. For two years we fed over a million children in Poland and they are tremendously grateful to us.  The American businessman doesn't pay much attention to his foreign trade. We are too indifferent to find out the real market conditions. But we have great latent possibilities there as a result of our national activities in the past few years, possibilities which we will eventually capitalize."<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_Navy_career">Later Navy career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Later Navy career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lt_Cdr_Hugo_W._Koehler_dress_uniform_with_decorations.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a7/Lt_Cdr_Hugo_W._Koehler_dress_uniform_with_decorations.jpg/220px-Lt_Cdr_Hugo_W._Koehler_dress_uniform_with_decorations.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a7/Lt_Cdr_Hugo_W._Koehler_dress_uniform_with_decorations.jpg/330px-Lt_Cdr_Hugo_W._Koehler_dress_uniform_with_decorations.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/a7/Lt_Cdr_Hugo_W._Koehler_dress_uniform_with_decorations.jpg 2x" data-file-width="386" data-file-height="511" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lt_Cdr_Hugo_W._Koehler_dress_uniform_with_decorations.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lt. Cdr. Hugo Koehler wearing Navy Cross, Russian Imperial and other foreign decorations, ca. 1925</div></div></div><p>Following his service in Russia and Poland, the remainder of Koehler's naval career was more typical. He left Davenport bound for <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> on December 29, 1922, with orders to the battleship <a href="/wiki/USS_Utah_(BB-31)" title="USS Utah (BB-31)">USS&#160;<i>Utah</i></a>, flagship of Battleship Division Six, <a href="/wiki/Scouting_Fleet" title="Scouting Fleet">Scouting Fleet</a>, based on the <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="East Coast of the United States">Atlantic Coast</a> where his billet was <a href="/wiki/First_lieutenant" title="First lieutenant">first lieutenant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> He stopped in Washington at the offices of the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_the_Interior" class="mw-redirect" title="Department of the Interior">Department of the Interior</a> to convey the Pole's interest in conserving their public lands to officials there. On his way to the West Coast for his next naval assignment, Koehler made it a point to stop at several national parks to gain personal knowledge before writing the articles that his late friend, the assassinated Polish president Gabriel Narutowicz had asked him to write.<sup id="cite_ref-EpUmAQAAMAAJ_1922._p._216_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EpUmAQAAMAAJ_1922._p._216-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> In June 1923, he was appointed aide to Vice Admiral <a href="/wiki/Henry_A._Wiley" title="Henry A. Wiley">Henry A. Wiley</a>, commander of the battleship divisions of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Battle_Fleet" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Battle Fleet">United States Battle Fleet</a> with his <a href="/wiki/Flagship" title="Flagship">flag</a> on the Battleship Division Five flagship, <a href="/wiki/USS_New_Mexico_(BB-40)" title="USS New Mexico (BB-40)">USS&#160;<i>New Mexico</i></a> based at <a href="/wiki/San_Pedro,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="San Pedro, California">San Pedro, California</a>. Ten years earlier, then Commander Wiley had been Koehler's commanding officer when he was an ensign aboard the Asiatic Fleet flagship, <i>Saratoga</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>  In November 1923, Wiley was president of the largest naval <a href="/wiki/Court_martial" class="mw-redirect" title="Court martial">court martial</a> to date, as eleven  officers of Destroyer Division 11 were prosecuted in San Diego for their actions that resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Point_disaster" title="Honda Point disaster">Honda Point disaster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>  Several months later, on June 12, 1924, disaster again struck the Battle Fleet when the forward Gun Turret No. 2 of the <a href="/wiki/USS_Mississippi_(BB-41)" title="USS Mississippi (BB-41)">USS&#160;<i>Mississippi</i></a> exploded during gunnery practice off San Pedro, killing 48 sailors in the worst U.S. Navy peacetime disaster to date.  The next evening, Koehler, as vice-secretary of the battleship divisions, made the announcement of the large funeral service to be held near the Los Angeles harbor and that the next of kin of all those killed had given permission for their remains to be held for the joint memorial service, with both <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> chaplains taking part.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> A week after the explosion, a board of inquiry conducted aboard <i>New Mexico</i> and presided over by her commanding officer, Captain <a href="/wiki/Yates_Stirling,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Yates Stirling, Jr.">Yates Stirling, Jr.</a>, delivered its findings of "unavoidable accident" to Vice Admiral Wiley and made recommendations to avoid such future accidents, including greater precautions in handling the magazine powder and equipping gun turrets with escape hatches.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
On October 30, 1924, Wiley shifted his flag from <i>New Mexico</i> to the recently constructed battleship <a href="/wiki/USS_West_Virginia_(BB-48)" title="USS West Virginia (BB-48)">USS&#160;<i>West Virginia</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The following month, both Wiley and Koehler gave an interview to the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a> for an article titled, <i>Coast Defenses Planned to Make Southland Safe- War Dept. Expects to Have Los Angeles Invulnerable to Enemy Attack</i>.  "As long as the Battle Fleet is out here, Los Angeles is as safe as <a href="/wiki/Omaha" class="mw-redirect" title="Omaha">Omaha</a>. Take away the fleet and I wouldn't give a nickel for it," Wiley stated.  Koehler, referred to in the article as Wiley's aide and "one of the Navy's best men on military strategy", expanded on the statement, "As long as this fleet is in being the Pacific Coast needs no coast defenses. The point of defending our country is not in waiting until the enemy crosses the sea, but to get out and whip the tar out of him before he gets here. No amount of coast defenses, however modern or of what immense range, would save the Pacific Coast if the fleet were not in being. It was thought in 1914 that the defenses of <a href="/wiki/Namur" title="Namur">Namur</a> were absolutely invulnerable, that they were the most modern in the world, yet the Germans cracked them in forty-six hours. The same was true at <a href="/wiki/Liege" class="mw-redirect" title="Liege">Liege</a> and <a href="/wiki/Verdun" title="Verdun">Verdun</a>.  On the other hand, land fortifications are sometimes essential. They are essential at Hawaii because Hawaii is vital to the existence of the Battle Fleet and they are vital at <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a> because they can forbid the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> During Koehler's time aboard <i>West Virginia</i>, the battleship made a cruise to the <a href="/wiki/South_Pacific_Ocean" class="mw-redirect" title="South Pacific Ocean">South Pacific</a>.  He held that assignment until September 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Thereafter, he remained at <a href="/wiki/Balboa,_Panama" title="Balboa, Panama">Balboa, Panama</a>, where he directed the Balboa Naval Transmitting Station, a VLF-transmitting station for relaying orders to submarines that had begun service around 1915. While in Panama, Koehler's mother, Mathilda, visited him.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_Koehler_at_Naval_War_College.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/39/Hugo_Koehler_at_Naval_War_College.jpg/350px-Hugo_Koehler_at_Naval_War_College.jpg" decoding="async" width="350" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/39/Hugo_Koehler_at_Naval_War_College.jpg/525px-Hugo_Koehler_at_Naval_War_College.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/39/Hugo_Koehler_at_Naval_War_College.jpg/700px-Hugo_Koehler_at_Naval_War_College.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1204" data-file-height="485" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_Koehler_at_Naval_War_College.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lt. Cdr. Hugo Koehler, member of teaching staff at the Naval War College, 1926-27</div></div></div>
<p>He was assigned as an instructor at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_War_College" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Naval War College">United States Naval War College</a> in Newport, Rhode Island on April 17, 1926, and remained there until October 1927.  In this capacity he was able to share the lessons he learned from his experiences in Russia and Poland with future admirals in the U.S. Navy, including <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Spruance" class="mw-redirect" title="Raymond Spruance">Raymond Spruance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> On April 29, 1927, he delivered a lecture, "Developments in Russia" to the graduating class at the Naval Academy.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> He gave lectures on the techniques in intelligence gathering under the new method of instruction that started with Rear Admiral <a href="/wiki/William_V._Pratt" title="William V. Pratt">William V. Pratt</a>'s tenure as president of the college. Pratt admired Koehler for his "great mental ability," remarking that the Commander was "different, a clash of wits and brains. To be with him, was always mental refreshment for me." <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Tate" title="Jackson Tate">Jackson Tate</a> described Koehler as, "very much of a specialist... rather than... regular Navy".  Koehler's exploits were widely known and his legend grew as his naval career diminished to the ordinary. Rear Admiral <a href="/wiki/Kemp_Tolley" title="Kemp Tolley">Kemp Tolley</a>, who was a young officer in the Asiatic Fleet during the 1930s, recalled an anecdote of Koehler's time in South Russia, "[Admiral McCully] had notified one of the destroyers in his command (in the Black Sea) that he would be aboard shortly. So the OOD (officer of the deck) was of course alerted to watch out for any approaching boat. Naturally, he was much astonished to hear shouts in the water some time later- from McCully and Koehler. They had swum out in the buff."<sup id="cite_ref-Capelotti._pg._15_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capelotti._pg._15-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Matilda_Pell_Koehler_1920%27s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7c/Matilda_Pell_Koehler_1920%27s.jpg/250px-Matilda_Pell_Koehler_1920%27s.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7c/Matilda_Pell_Koehler_1920%27s.jpg/375px-Matilda_Pell_Koehler_1920%27s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/7c/Matilda_Pell_Koehler_1920%27s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="436" data-file-height="326" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Matilda_Pell_Koehler_1920%27s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Matilda Bigelow Pell Koehler, ca. 1920's</div></div></div><p>On June 2, 1927, Koehler married Matilda Bigelow Pell (1895–1972), the divorced wife of former <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a> <a href="/wiki/Congressman" class="mw-redirect" title="Congressman">Congressman</a> <a href="/wiki/Herbert_C._Pell" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbert C. Pell">Herbert C. Pell</a> (1884–1961), whom he had met several years earlier.  They were married at the Madison Ave. Baptist Church on East Thirty-First St. in New York, having obtained a marriage license only hours before.  Other than the minister, Rev. George C. Moor, the only others present were the bride's brother, Anson Bigelow and his wife.  The marriage came as a surprise to most of their society friends, since the previous week, Matilda Pell was adamantly denying rumors of a pending marriage to a naval officer. She had obtained her divorce from Herbert Pell in Paris, the previous March.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>  Pell remarried in Paris a couple weeks later.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Coincidentally, on the day of his marriage, Koehler was promoted to commander. </p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Admiral_Henry_A._Wiley_and_his_flag_secretary,_Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_aboard_USS_Texas_in_1928.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ad/Admiral_Henry_A._Wiley_and_his_flag_secretary%2C_Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_aboard_USS_Texas_in_1928.jpg/300px-Admiral_Henry_A._Wiley_and_his_flag_secretary%2C_Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_aboard_USS_Texas_in_1928.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ad/Admiral_Henry_A._Wiley_and_his_flag_secretary%2C_Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_aboard_USS_Texas_in_1928.jpg/450px-Admiral_Henry_A._Wiley_and_his_flag_secretary%2C_Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_aboard_USS_Texas_in_1928.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ad/Admiral_Henry_A._Wiley_and_his_flag_secretary%2C_Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_aboard_USS_Texas_in_1928.jpg/600px-Admiral_Henry_A._Wiley_and_his_flag_secretary%2C_Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_aboard_USS_Texas_in_1928.jpg 2x" data-file-width="840" data-file-height="667" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Admiral_Henry_A._Wiley_and_his_flag_secretary,_Cdr._Hugo_W._Koehler_aboard_USS_Texas_in_1928.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adm. <a href="/wiki/Henry_A._Wiley" title="Henry A. Wiley">Henry A. Wiley</a> and his flag secretary, Cdr. Hugo W. Koehler, <a href="/wiki/USS_Texas_(BB-35)" title="USS Texas (BB-35)">USS&#160;<i>Texas</i>&#160;<span class="nowrap">(BB-35)</span></a>, Nov. 7, 1927</div></div></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_Koehler_and_his_son_Hugh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/aa/Hugo_Koehler_and_his_son_Hugh.jpg/250px-Hugo_Koehler_and_his_son_Hugh.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/aa/Hugo_Koehler_and_his_son_Hugh.jpg/375px-Hugo_Koehler_and_his_son_Hugh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/aa/Hugo_Koehler_and_his_son_Hugh.jpg/500px-Hugo_Koehler_and_his_son_Hugh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="585" data-file-height="702" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_Koehler_and_his_son_Hugh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hugo Koehler and his son, Hugh, 1929</div></div></div><p> On September 3, 1929, their only child, Hugh Gladstone Koehler, was born.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>  Named for Koehler's close friend, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Gladstone,_1st_Viscount_Gladstone" title="Herbert Gladstone, 1st Viscount Gladstone">Herbert Gladstone, 1st Viscount Gladstone</a>, Hugh Koehler became an investment banker and lived in Connecticut, before he died in 1990 shortly before his 60th birthday.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Matilda's only child from her previous marriage, <a href="/wiki/Claiborne_Pell" title="Claiborne Pell">Claiborne Pell</a> (1918–2009), later served as a <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senator" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senator">United States Senator</a> from <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a> for 36 years (1961–1997).
</p><p>On November 3, 1927, Koehler was assigned as flag secretary to the staff of the commander-in-chief of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Fleet" title="United States Fleet">United States Fleet</a>, Admiral Wiley,<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> whom he had served under twice before and who was impressed with Koehler's character and code of conduct.  Recalling the incident early in Koehler's career where he had personally funded the payroll for other officers in the Asiatic fleet, Wiley stated years later, "It was commonly understood that Koehler went deep into his own pocket to relieve these young people.  They were not particular friends of his. I know of no incident that could better illustrate the man's generous nature."<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>  This was Koehler's final duty station, since he announced his resignation at the end of December 1928, and requested a separation date of February 14, 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post_Navy_life">Post Navy life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Post Navy life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Gladstone,_1st_Viscount_Gladstone_and_Hugo_Koehler.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e2/Herbert_Gladstone%2C_1st_Viscount_Gladstone_and_Hugo_Koehler.jpg/220px-Herbert_Gladstone%2C_1st_Viscount_Gladstone_and_Hugo_Koehler.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="283" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e2/Herbert_Gladstone%2C_1st_Viscount_Gladstone_and_Hugo_Koehler.jpg/330px-Herbert_Gladstone%2C_1st_Viscount_Gladstone_and_Hugo_Koehler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e2/Herbert_Gladstone%2C_1st_Viscount_Gladstone_and_Hugo_Koehler.jpg/440px-Herbert_Gladstone%2C_1st_Viscount_Gladstone_and_Hugo_Koehler.jpg 2x" data-file-width="676" data-file-height="870" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Gladstone,_1st_Viscount_Gladstone_and_Hugo_Koehler.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Herbert Gladstone, 1st Viscount Gladstone and Hugo Koehler</div></div></div><p>Ever the romantic, Koehler's explicit request that his retirement from the Navy take effect on <a href="/wiki/Valentine%27s_Day" title="Valentine&#39;s Day">Valentine's Day</a> telegraphed at least one motive for resigning his naval commission.  It was a gift to his wife, since Koehler realized that even if he landed his dream assignment, naval attaché to Moscow, the aristocratic Matilda, would not appreciate life in Russia or the long months when he would be assigned sea duty.  After his resignation from the Navy, Koehler, assumed the life of a socialite. His friend, Lord Gladstone, had warned Koehler against retiring from the Navy without a challenging, second career alternative, adding, "If you were in our Navy, you would now be an admiral."<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>  The man who had been a driven professional became an avid <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dilettante" class="extiw" title="wikt:dilettante">dilettante</a>. The Koehlers divided their time between an apartment at 510 <a href="/wiki/Park_Avenue" title="Park Avenue">Park Avenue</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport</a>, where they spent most of the summer along with other members of New York society, and purchased an estate "cottage", "Wyndhurst", that they later sold in 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>  Koehler raised orchids and tried his hand at pineapples and bananas in a small greenhouse.  He bought expensive antique furniture and gold <a href="/wiki/Snuff_box" class="mw-redirect" title="Snuff box">snuff boxes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Unfortunately, Koehler's purported Austrian trust income had ceased once he married Matilda.  In August the previous year, Koehler and his brother and four sisters each received an approximately $18,000 distribution following the termination of a trust fund that had been set up by their father.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Crash" class="mw-redirect" title="Wall Street Crash">Wall Street Crash</a> in 1929, money suddenly became tight.  As one acquaintance said, "They both thought the other had money and both were fooled."<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Claiborne_Pell_and_his_half-brother_Hugh_Koehler_in_London_1933.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/80/Claiborne_Pell_and_his_half-brother_Hugh_Koehler_in_London_1933.jpg/300px-Claiborne_Pell_and_his_half-brother_Hugh_Koehler_in_London_1933.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/80/Claiborne_Pell_and_his_half-brother_Hugh_Koehler_in_London_1933.jpg/450px-Claiborne_Pell_and_his_half-brother_Hugh_Koehler_in_London_1933.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/80/Claiborne_Pell_and_his_half-brother_Hugh_Koehler_in_London_1933.jpg/600px-Claiborne_Pell_and_his_half-brother_Hugh_Koehler_in_London_1933.jpg 2x" data-file-width="918" data-file-height="594" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Claiborne_Pell_and_his_half-brother_Hugh_Koehler_in_London_1933.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>15-year old Claiborne Pell and his half-brother, Hugh Koehler, summer 1933, London</div></div></div><p>  The couple moved to England, where they spent most of the year, and Hugo fully embraced the lifestyle of the British <a href="/wiki/Gentry" title="Gentry">gentry</a> with his many society friends there. While living in England, Koehler travelled to <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> and had multiple meetings with <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> in 1933.  The substance of these meetings, that were probably set up by <a href="/wiki/British_Intelligence" class="mw-redirect" title="British Intelligence">British Intelligence</a> is not known, but Koehler was impressed with Stalin from their conversations. Koehler had predicted the rise of Stalin in 1920, when he wrote, "All Russia is on the lookout for this dictator and every new leader that crops up is examined in the light of his aptitude for the job."<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Given Koehler's penchant for intrigue, he may have been asked to perform other intelligence assignments during his retirement.  In 1934, Hugo, Matilda, his fifteen-year old stepson, Claiborne Pell and five-year-old son, Hugh, returned to America, in large part because Herbert Pell did not want his son raised as an Englishman. In 1936, the Koehlers purchased <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=9ypjVu3aVFc">"Eastover"</a>, a thirty-acre estate off Wapping Road in <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth,_Rhode_Island" title="Portsmouth, Rhode Island">Portsmouth, Rhode Island</a>, with a 1,000&#160;ft. waterfront on the <a href="/wiki/Sakonnet_River" title="Sakonnet River">Sakonnet River</a>, where Hugo planted raspberries, built a sea wall, tended a flock of sheep and made a rose garden for Matilda. His son, Hugh, had chickens, a dog and a pony named "Broadway Bill".<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>  Known as "Commander Koehler", he and Matilda Koehler were frequently mentioned in the society pages of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a> and they continued to maintain their apartment on Park Avenue in New York city.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> While the Koehler's and their sons were part of the wealthy Newport social circuit, attending dinner parties and giving them at "Eastover", Matilda was bored by Hugo's navy friends, and her Newport crowd was unnerved by the mysterious and overly exotic Commander. While Koehler's eccentricities probably delighted Stalin, Kutepov and scores of long-forgotten cossacks, the Newport set was not enchanted by them. During one party at "Eastover", Koehler bit the rung of a chair in two, to demonstrate to Matilda that it was "weak" and of "inferior construction".  He would consume entire lamb chops, bone and all, to the disgust of his fellow diners.  As Koehler's historiographer wrote, "He could easily have done the Greek waiter's trick of picking up a small, dinner table in his teeth, and dancing with it, without spilling the wine from the brimming carafe."<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_and_Matilda_Koehler.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/83/Hugo_and_Matilda_Koehler.jpg/220px-Hugo_and_Matilda_Koehler.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/83/Hugo_and_Matilda_Koehler.jpg/330px-Hugo_and_Matilda_Koehler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/83/Hugo_and_Matilda_Koehler.jpg/440px-Hugo_and_Matilda_Koehler.jpg 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="904" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_and_Matilda_Koehler.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hugo and Matilda Koehler</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_speaking">Public speaking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Public speaking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Koehler was a popular, local speaker in Portsmouth and Newport Rhode, Island during the late 1930s and up until his death, sounding the warning against the rise of <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascism</a> in Europe and Asia and the danger of <a href="/wiki/Isolationism" title="Isolationism">isolationism</a>.  In an address titled, "<a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>- For What?", in January 1938, Koehler spoke to the "Monday Night Club" in Newport about his personal experiences and observations in Russia during 1920-21 and his meeting with Stalin in 1933. Stalin predicted to Koehler that America would achieve <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a> more quickly and directly than the Soviet Union. Koehler recalled Russia in the years after World War I, when the White Russians and Bolsheviks battled for control of armies driven into the sea as they tried to reach the <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a>, and of the great famine and atrocities that were "unhappily too true."  He opined that the Bolsheviks were spurred on by an idealism of <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">liberty</a> that they had never enjoyed, put their hearts into the fight, and won.  Sent by the State Department to Russia, Koehler described his trip disguised as a Jewish <a href="/wiki/Peddler" title="Peddler">peddler</a>, on foot across Russia to Poland, and his studies of the masses.  Among the good points of the Bolshevik program, he pointed to the work-to-eat edict, education of the masses, and hospitalization for the children.  But he condemned the segregation and disruption of families, organization of an industrial production program and abolition of the <a href="/wiki/Labor_union" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor union">labor unions</a>.  Koehler said that the Bolsheviks considered unions and family life as a "menace" to <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a>. "There is more inequality in Russia today than under the old regime," he declared.  Recalling his interview of Stalin, who asked questions about America, and warned Koehler to  "Read Marx", he told of the Soviet leader predicting the U.S. would become a socialist state in the near future.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 1938, Koehler addressed the Newport Art Association on the subject "Is Europe Headed for War; Are We?" and made the initial point that "<a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> will probably continue to have victories of the kind that lead to defeat." His second point was that in a year Japan had "succeeded in giving <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> a unity that not all the centuries gave her."  Koehler asserted that the Chinese with a <a href="/wiki/Bayonet" title="Bayonet">bayonet</a> is superior man to man to the Japanese. The Japanese have agreed with the Fascist powers and the German advisor to <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kaishek" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiang Kaishek">Chiang Kaishek</a>, General <a href="/wiki/Erich_von_Falkenhayn" title="Erich von Falkenhayn">Von Falkenhayn</a>, who had been  forced to withdraw from China because of pressures from Japan, told Hitler that Germany "has been backing the wrong horse" in Asia. Koehler observed that a militant Italy was a lesser threat than Germany with its greater population and resources.  He considered life under <a href="/wiki/Bolshevism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevism">bolshevism</a> to be worse than life under <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascism</a>.  Concerning the immediate problem of Hitler's annexation of the <a href="/wiki/Sudetenland" title="Sudetenland">Sudetenland</a>, Koehler reasoned that <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a> would get little support from the other nations of the <a href="/wiki/Little_Entente" title="Little Entente">Little Entente</a> for fear of exposing themselves to attack from Germany.  Britain, however, would not fail to be on the side of France if war broke out.  He believed that under advisement of his high command, Hitler would "continue to capitalize on the fear of war and keep Europe in turmoil for some time to come and up to this day he has gained as much by bluff as he could get by war and without the disastrous cost of war."  Koehler's closing remark was half-accurate as history has bore out, "He will not resort to war. War would be the end of Hitler."<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mrs._Hugo_W._Koehler_with_Mr._and_Mrs._James_Russell_Lowell_at_Belmont_Racetrack,_September_1939.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/80/Mrs._Hugo_W._Koehler_with_Mr._and_Mrs._James_Russell_Lowell_at_Belmont_Racetrack%2C_September_1939.jpg/220px-Mrs._Hugo_W._Koehler_with_Mr._and_Mrs._James_Russell_Lowell_at_Belmont_Racetrack%2C_September_1939.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/80/Mrs._Hugo_W._Koehler_with_Mr._and_Mrs._James_Russell_Lowell_at_Belmont_Racetrack%2C_September_1939.jpg/330px-Mrs._Hugo_W._Koehler_with_Mr._and_Mrs._James_Russell_Lowell_at_Belmont_Racetrack%2C_September_1939.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/80/Mrs._Hugo_W._Koehler_with_Mr._and_Mrs._James_Russell_Lowell_at_Belmont_Racetrack%2C_September_1939.jpg/440px-Mrs._Hugo_W._Koehler_with_Mr._and_Mrs._James_Russell_Lowell_at_Belmont_Racetrack%2C_September_1939.jpg 2x" data-file-width="607" data-file-height="755" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mrs._Hugo_W._Koehler_with_Mr._and_Mrs._James_Russell_Lowell_at_Belmont_Racetrack,_September_1939.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Matilda Koehler with Mr. and Mrs. James Russell Lowell at <a href="/wiki/Belmont_Park" title="Belmont Park">Belmont Park</a>, September 23, 1939</div></div></div><p>Speaking before the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Reserve_Association" title="Fleet Reserve Association">Fleet Reserve Association</a> in February 1939, Koehler claimed that following the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Pact" class="mw-redirect" title="Munich Pact">Munich Pact</a>, Britain was actually in a better position since <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a> had succeeded in garnering the support of the British people, including the Labourites.  He stated that <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> was "bluffing" in September 1938, and would not have forced a war without <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy's</a> support. Overly optimistic, Koehler believed that France had the "strongest army" in Europe and was allied with Britain, but did not want to provoke a war.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> In an April, 1939 address to the Men's Club of the <a href="/wiki/United_Congregational_Church_(Newport,_Rhode_Island)" title="United Congregational Church (Newport, Rhode Island)">United Congregational Church (Newport, Rhode Island)</a>, Koehler predicted that if war were to break out, the United States would be drawn into it.  "<a href="/wiki/Mussolini" class="mw-redirect" title="Mussolini">Mussolini</a> is afraid of war and Hitler doesn't want it," he said.  Taking questions from the audience, Koehler at that time believed that the dictator nations would achieve their goals without engaging other nations in war.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Koehler delivered an address to the Newport Electric Corporation's Men's Club on October 14, 1939, and warned that if the current arms embargo by the United States were not lifted, the war in Europe would be over within a month.  Great Britain and France could not continue without U.S. assistance in munitions and supplies. On the other hand, Koehler observed, Hitler "is in a jam" since Germany has to conserve her supplies for a possible later fight with Russia.  Hitler's alliance with Russia came at a "stiff price" by alienating <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> entirely and weakening Germany's support in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>.  Had Spain gone along with Germany, <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Franco</a> could have mobilized an army on the border and forced <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> to do likewise, drawing men from the German front.  "Great Britain", he pointed out, "made a smart move in its trade pact with Russia, in that it can take what it needs, at the same time, keeping these imports and supplies from Germany. "Stalin," he said, "is only interested in Russia, and will be with any country that can put gold into Moscow."  Koehler offered that the present British government was "in poor hands" with <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Chamberlain</a> as <a href="/wiki/British_prime_minister" class="mw-redirect" title="British prime minister">prime minister</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_Simon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir John Simon">Sir John Simon</a> as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">head of the treasury</a>, since both men believed in keeping arms spending low.  Aside from Britain "blundering" in its negotiations with Germany preceding the war, Koehler observed that Britain made a strategic error in "building battleships, which are only good to fight other battleships.  A blockade could be effected as well with destroyers." He reasoned that Germany had not used its <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">air force</a> to break the embargo due to the high cost of ammunition, as it would require many planes dropping hundreds of bombs to sink a warship.  Koehler considered Mussolini "wise" to stay out of the conflict because Italy's "army is not strong enough to make a good showing."  As to <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>, they are no longer the "towers of strength" due to airplanes which could launch bombing attacks in the darkness.  Koehler saw the war on the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Western Front (World War II)">Western Front</a> as being a "perfect stalemate" due to the <a href="/wiki/Maginot_Line" title="Maginot Line">Maginot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siegfried_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Siegfried line">Siegfried lines</a>, overestimating in particular the false impregnability of the Maginot line. As to the German <a href="/wiki/U-boat" title="U-boat">U-boat</a> and aerial offensive, he thought it "possible but not probable" that Britain's blockade would be broken.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_Koehler_circa_1941.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Hugo_Koehler_circa_1941.jpg/225px-Hugo_Koehler_circa_1941.jpg" decoding="async" width="225" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Hugo_Koehler_circa_1941.jpg/338px-Hugo_Koehler_circa_1941.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Hugo_Koehler_circa_1941.jpg/450px-Hugo_Koehler_circa_1941.jpg 2x" data-file-width="964" data-file-height="1272" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_Koehler_circa_1941.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hugo Koehler, shortly before his death in 1941</div></div></div><p>In a talk before the Newport Art Association in September 1940, Koehler announced, "We are already in the war, even though we are not technically at war, in fact we've been in the war ever since that last November when Congress modified the <a href="/wiki/Neutrality_Acts_of_the_1930s" title="Neutrality Acts of the 1930s">Neutrality Laws</a> in favor of <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">the Allies</a>.  We are in as bad a state today as the <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> were before their collapse, the basic cause of which was the easy indifference of the masses of the people, their shortsighted unwillingness to get down to hard work and to make the sacrifices that an intellectually honest analysis indicated as indispensable. Do we find within ourselves any evidence of a real willingness to work and to make real sacrifices?" Koehler questioned his audience.  "The <a href="/wiki/American_Youth_Congress" title="American Youth Congress">American Youth Congress</a> inculcates an underlying mood of sullen rebellion in youth against their elders for failure to see to it, that the pleasant and easy-going conditions of these years are continued indefinitely.  It would be to rely on the mood of a lot of spoiled children to rely on the youth of today even in part, for the defense of the country. No amount of external armaments can give us safety until first we are strong internally. We need sufficient force to ensure that our help will enable the <a href="/wiki/British_fleet" class="mw-redirect" title="British fleet">British fleet</a> completely to control the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>.  Until our planned two-sea navy is ready we should accept the idea that our front lines should not be on our own doorsteps, but close enough to those of the enemy to make the <a href="/wiki/Blockade" title="Blockade">blockade</a> really effective, and now."<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Addressing a musician's union local in January 1941, Koehler urged, "We've got to get in, the sooner the better for our defense." He warned that if <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">Britain</a> where defeated, the "problem" would be left with the United States.  Calling <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Gompers" title="Samuel Gompers">Samuel Gompers</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_Labor" title="American Federation of Labor">American Federation of Labor</a> "one of the greatest leaders of this country", Koehler spoke of the importance of music in life.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
In one of his last public addresses on the "International Situation", given at Newport before a group of 250 at the "Monday Night Club" in April 1941, Koehler encouraged his audience that, "We as individuals will pour out our treasures until it more than hurts, but it will be worth it."  He warned that America must make its stand clear on the <a href="/wiki/Lend-lease" class="mw-redirect" title="Lend-lease">lend-lease</a> program, referring to it as a "magnificent gesture", and saying that in the First World War, the "<a href="/wiki/Altruism" title="Altruism">altruism</a>" of the United States was "not appreciated" and was overridden by the national and economic policies and the political expedients of Europe and the East.  He called for a "definite policy this time, when the U.S. has the power to demand guarantees of future world stability."  He noted that at the end of this war, (<a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>) the world will either be <a href="/wiki/Totalitarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Totalitarian">totalitarian</a> under Hitler or <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democratic</a> under Britain. "It is time for clear, hard, <a href="/wiki/Yankee" title="Yankee">Yankee</a> thinking," he said.  Concerning the <a href="/wiki/Balkan" class="mw-redirect" title="Balkan">Balkan</a> situation, then the key point of the conflict, Koehler concluded that the various groups were unified for mutual protection: "the <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a> are soldiers (as good as the French in World War I, since they "fight best in a forlorn cause"), the <a href="/wiki/Croats" title="Croats">Croats</a> businessmen and the <a href="/wiki/Slovenes" title="Slovenes">Slovenes</a> are the workers in the Balkans," he observed.
</p><p>Koehler accurately prophesied the Russians as "a tremendous problem because of their cross and double-cross trickery; their fear that a German invasion would conquer them; and their threat from <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> in the east."  "Russia", Koehler continued, "is terrified and subservient to Germany and professes a benevolent neutrality for Japan and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>. As to Turkey as a nation, and not the <a href="/wiki/Levantines" class="mw-redirect" title="Levantines">Levantines</a> in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, Koehler praised their "honesty and determination of purpose". <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, he said, "is no longer worthy of discussion" and the countries that Germany had overran, "may be noisy but they cannot and will not revolt".<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_burial">Death and burial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Death and burial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Matilda_Koehler_gravesite.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/80/Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Matilda_Koehler_gravesite.jpg/225px-Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Matilda_Koehler_gravesite.jpg" decoding="async" width="225" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/80/Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Matilda_Koehler_gravesite.jpg/338px-Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Matilda_Koehler_gravesite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/80/Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Matilda_Koehler_gravesite.jpg/450px-Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Matilda_Koehler_gravesite.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1210" data-file-height="1613" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Matilda_Koehler_gravesite.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gravesites of Hugo, Matilda and Hugh Koehler</div></div></div><p>With Koehler's hereditary kidney condition rapidly deteriorating, on May 1, 1941, he and Matilda leased "Eastover" to Ralph Stonor and Mildred Sherman, better known as  <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Stonor,_5th_Baron_Camoys" title="Ralph Stonor, 5th Baron Camoys">Lord and Lady Camoys</a> and occupied the guesthouse nearby.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>  Hugo Koehler died of <a href="/wiki/Bright%27s_Disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Bright&#39;s Disease">Bright's Disease</a> (kidney failure) at the age of 54 on June 17, 1941, at his home on 510 Park Avenue in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> He did not live to see America enter the Second World War.  Koehler's father, Oscar, had died of the same <a href="/wiki/Congenital" class="mw-redirect" title="Congenital">congenital</a> condition at the age of 45,<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> diminishing the possibility that Hugo was other than his natural-born son. Shortly before his death, Koehler asked Claiborne Pell and Maggie Potter to attend to his desk and spare that sad task from Matilda.  All they found was a drawer of unpaid bills, the envelopes unopened, and a packet of <a href="/wiki/Sonnets" class="mw-redirect" title="Sonnets">sonnets</a> that Potter had written for him years earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Hearing of his death, his old <a href="/wiki/Extramarital_sex" title="Extramarital sex">paramour</a>, Dolly Gladstone, remarked, "[I]t is impossible to realize that I shall never see him again. It is as if the sun had ceased to shine." <sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Before he died, Koehler confided to a friend, that he had "missed the boat" and should have gone to Moscow instead of settling for retirement in Newport. But he knew that continuing his naval career would "crucify" Matilda and he simply could not hurt her. "I made a terrible blunder, all the things I should have been but wasn't".<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Although she loved him, Matilda was a private woman and after Hugo's death she burned the extensive letters that he had written her. In January 1942, Matilda sold their estate, "Eastover", to Captain and Mrs. Marion Eppley.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Koehler is buried with his wife and son in the Berkeley Memorial Cemetery by <a href="/wiki/St._Columba%27s_Chapel_(Middletown,_Rhode_Island)" title="St. Columba&#39;s Chapel (Middletown, Rhode Island)">St. Columba's Chapel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Middletown,_Rhode_Island" title="Middletown, Rhode Island">Middletown, Rhode Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> In November 1942, 25-year old Lt.(j.g) <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, then attached to the <a href="/wiki/Motor_Torpedo_Boat_Squadrons_Training_Center" title="Motor Torpedo Boat Squadrons Training Center">Motor Torpedo Boat Squadrons Training Center</a> at <a href="/wiki/Melville,_Rhode_Island" title="Melville, Rhode Island">Melville, Rhode Island</a>, was mourning the death of his close, childhood friend, <a href="/wiki/U.S._Marine_Corps" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Marine Corps">Marine</a> Second Lieutenant George Houk Mead, Jr., who had been killed in action at <a href="/wiki/Guadalcanal" title="Guadalcanal">Guadalcanal</a> that August and posthumously awarded the <a href="/wiki/Navy_Cross" title="Navy Cross">Navy Cross</a>.  Accompanied by a female acquaintance from a wealthy Newport family, the couple had stopped in Middletown at the cemetery where Hugo Koehler had been buried the year before. Ambling around the plots near the tiny St. Columba's chapel, Kennedy paused at Koehler's granite cross grave marker  and pondered his own mortality, hoping out loud that when his time came, he would not have to die without religion. "But these things can't be faked," he added. "There's no bluffing."<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Two decades later, President Kennedy and Koehler's stepson, <a href="/wiki/U.S._Senator" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Senator">U.S. Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/Claiborne_Pell" title="Claiborne Pell">Claiborne Pell</a> had become good friends and political allies, although they had been acquaintances since the mid-1930s during their "<a href="/wiki/Salad_days" title="Salad days">salad days</a>" on the same Newport <a href="/wiki/Debutante" title="Debutante">debutante</a> party "circuit" and when Pell had dated JFK's sister, Kathleen ("Kick") Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Personality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_W._Koehler_and_his_step-son,_Claiborne_Pell.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/Hugo_W._Koehler_and_his_step-son%2C_Claiborne_Pell.jpg/220px-Hugo_W._Koehler_and_his_step-son%2C_Claiborne_Pell.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="322" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/Hugo_W._Koehler_and_his_step-son%2C_Claiborne_Pell.jpg/330px-Hugo_W._Koehler_and_his_step-son%2C_Claiborne_Pell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/Hugo_W._Koehler_and_his_step-son%2C_Claiborne_Pell.jpg/440px-Hugo_W._Koehler_and_his_step-son%2C_Claiborne_Pell.jpg 2x" data-file-width="557" data-file-height="816" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo_W._Koehler_and_his_step-son,_Claiborne_Pell.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A young <a href="/wiki/Claiborne_Pell" title="Claiborne Pell">Claiborne Pell</a> with his step-father, Hugo Koehler</div></div></div><p>Hugo Koehler, by all accounts, had a highly engaging and charismatic personality. <a href="/wiki/Claiborne_Pell" title="Claiborne Pell">Claiborne Pell</a> wrote of his stepfather, "From the time I was nine years old, I lived most of the time, for ten months of each year, in the same house as my stepfather, Commander Koehler.  I loved the man.  He was a very gifted, very unusual person. On many occasions, I have met people who knew something about his exploits and from them, as well as from my mother and from what I could note myself, I have been convinced that a fine book could be- and should be – written about him. Whoever wrote it, however, would have to dig into areas that are not easy to come by."<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Although the theory that Koehler was the illegitimate son of a Habsburg prince, separated at birth, is dubious speculation; he was undeniably a dashing, swashbuckling figure enveloped in mystery.
