</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wilhelm_Von_Brincken_marriage_to_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c8/Wilhelm_Von_Brincken_marriage_to_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg/220px-Wilhelm_Von_Brincken_marriage_to_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="381" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c8/Wilhelm_Von_Brincken_marriage_to_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg/330px-Wilhelm_Von_Brincken_marriage_to_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c8/Wilhelm_Von_Brincken_marriage_to_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg/440px-Wilhelm_Von_Brincken_marriage_to_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="608" data-file-height="1052" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wilhelm_Von_Brincken_marriage_to_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wilhelm von Brincken at his wedding to Milo Abercrombie in October, 1915</div></div></div>
<p>In October 1925, while Koehler was commanding the Balboa Naval Transmitting Station at the Pacific end of the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal_Zone" title="Panama Canal Zone">Panama Canal Zone</a>, rumor about his engagement to a particularly colorful, San Francisco socialite and <a href="/wiki/Divorc%C3%A9e" class="mw-redirect" title="Divorcée">divorcée</a> made national news.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>  Koehler, reported to be "the wealthiest officer" in the Navy, was romantically linked to Milo Abercrombie (1895–1977), niece of <a href="/wiki/John_Abercrombie_(Congressman)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Abercrombie (Congressman)">John W. Abercrombie</a>, U.S. Congressman from Alabama. Milo was the former wife of the convicted World War One German spy and later, prolific <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">Hollywood</a> <a href="/wiki/Movie_actor" class="mw-redirect" title="Movie actor">movie actor</a> <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_von_Brincken" title="Wilhelm von Brincken">Wilhelm von Brincken</a>.  Abercrombie, acclaimed by noted portraitist <a href="/wiki/Harrison_Fisher" title="Harrison Fisher">Harrison Fisher</a> as "California's greatest beauty", had married von Brincken in 1915 when he was a German military attaché in San Francisco.  She divorced him in 1919 while he was imprisoned at <a href="/wiki/McNeil_Island_Corrections_Center" title="McNeil Island Corrections Center">McNeil Island Federal Penitentiary</a> on <a href="/wiki/Puget_Sound" title="Puget Sound">Puget Sound</a>.  Von Brincken was sentenced to serve two years in the <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93German_Conspiracy_Trial" title="Hindu–German Conspiracy Trial">Hindu–German Conspiracy Trial</a> for plotting to foment an <a href="/wiki/Insurrection" class="mw-redirect" title="Insurrection">insurrection</a> against British <a href="/wiki/Colonial_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Colonial rule">colonial rule</a> in India, this sentence to run concurrently with a similar conviction for his alleged participation in bombing and dynamiting plots against the government of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Following her divorce from von Brincken, Milo changed her and their two children's last names back to her maiden name so they would not be "ashamed".<sup id="cite_ref-:4_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>  In 1920, Abercrombie married a U.S. naval officer, Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Lyman_K._Swenson" class="mw-redirect" title="Lyman K. Swenson">Lyman K. Swenson</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Saint_Mary_of_the_Assumption_(San_Francisco,_California)" title="Cathedral of Saint Mary of the Assumption (San Francisco, California)">St Mary's Cathedral</a> in San Francisco.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>  Swenson introduced Milo to Koehler in <a href="/wiki/Honolulu" title="Honolulu">Honolulu</a> when both men's ships were stationed there.  In May 1925, Milo obtained an <a href="/wiki/Interlocutory_decree" class="mw-redirect" title="Interlocutory decree">interlocutory decree</a> of divorce from Swenson, to become final in May 1926.  In Panama, Koehler read the news stories claiming that he was engaged to marry Milo Abercrombie the following year, in June 1926.  Brushing it off, Koehler tersely told the press, "Some error,"  while Abercrombie did not take it so lightly.  "I have been deeply humiliated", she told reporters, her eyes "wet with tears".  "This is a most unkind blow of fate.  I cannot possibly understand how this false rumor got about."<sup id="cite_ref-:4_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>  Later, in a bitter child <a href="/wiki/Visitation_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Visitation rights">visitation rights</a> battle in 1927 that went all the way to the <a href="/wiki/California_Court_of_Appeals" class="mw-redirect" title="California Court of Appeals">California Court of Appeals</a>, Abercrombie lost custody of their children, Lyman, Jr. ("Robert") and Cecelia, after making baseless accusations that Swenson had molested their four-year-old daughter.  The appellate court excoriated Abercrombie, "[I]n furtherance of a manifest determination to prevent him from ever seeing the children again, under any circumstances, she was instrumental in inspiring and promoting a scheme directly involving one of the children which had for its obvious purpose the ruination of respondent's character as a man, the bringing about of his complete disgrace as a naval officer, and the destruction of the love and affection which his children had theretofore manifested toward him." <i>Swenson v. Swenson</i> (1929) 101 Cal.App. 440.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Lyman Swenson remarried in 1929 and in November 1942, Captain Swenson was killed in action when his <a href="/wiki/Cruiser" title="Cruiser">cruiser</a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_Juneau_(CL-52)" title="USS Juneau (CL-52)">USS&#160;<i>Juneau</i></a> was sunk during the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Battle_of_Guadalcanal" title="Naval Battle of Guadalcanal">Naval Battle of Guadalcanal</a> with the loss of all but 10 of its crew of 673, including the <a href="/wiki/Sullivan_Brothers" class="mw-redirect" title="Sullivan Brothers">five Sullivan Brothers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>  Von Brincken died suddenly in January 1946, after appearing in nearly 75 movies following his release from prison in 1921, often cast as a German <a href="/wiki/Villain" title="Villain">heavy</a> or spy.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 328.66666666667px"><div style="width: 328.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 326.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:LCDR_Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/15/LCDR_Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg/490px-LCDR_Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg" decoding="async" width="327" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/15/LCDR_Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg/735px-LCDR_Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/15/LCDR_Hugo_W._Koehler_and_Milo_Abercrombie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="948" data-file-height="581" /></a></div></div>

<p>False report of Lt. Cdr. Hugo Koehler's engagement to marry Milo Abercrombie, October 1925
</p>

</ul>
<p>Margaretta "Maggie" Wood (Potter) (1899–1985), daughter of Rear Admiral <a href="/wiki/Spencer_S._Wood" title="Spencer S. Wood">Spencer S. Wood</a> (1861–1940), was one of the many attractive and socially prominent women that had romantic trysts with Hugo Koehler.  It would not be inaccurate to say that Koehler loved women.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>
Maggie Potter met him in 1921 at a Washington dinner hosted by her father, the same year that Koehler met then-married Matilda and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_C._Pell" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbert C. Pell">Herbert Pell</a>.  Wood was twenty-one and Koehler a confident, worldly man of thirty-five. "I fell madly in love with him and I think that he loved me as much as I loved him," she was quoted in <i>Rescue of the Romanovs</i>.  They had a three-month affair before Koehler was detached to Warsaw as U.S. naval attache and were engaged to be married on his return; however, Koehler broke off the engagement telling her that she must "forget him".  Despite this, Potter remained friends of both Koehler and his wife, and maintained correspondence with him as a confidante until his death.  Near the end of his life, Koehler had reaffirmed his <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> faith and received the <a href="/wiki/Last_rites" title="Last rites">Last rites</a>.  Maggie Potter gave Matilda her <a href="/wiki/High_Renaissance" title="High Renaissance">High Renaissance</a> <a href="/wiki/Crucifix" title="Crucifix">crucifix</a> that was placed in Koehler's hands before he was buried.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>  "I think I knew him better and saw him more truly than did most of his other friends. He was a puzzle for two reasons: his brain was vastly superior to our brains, and he simply did not fit into the American scene.  Physically he was tall, but squarely built and very strong.  When he walked, with rather short steps, he carried his head high, slightly thrown back, and he never watched his feet, as Americans invariably do. He was strikingly handsome, even in his last years, when he was not well and his hair had turned white and his waist had thickened.  He spoke English in his deeply pitched voice with a faint German accent.  He was one of the best raconteurs and conversationalists I have ever listened to, although this excellence led him to the sin of pontificating, and when there was no one well-informed enough for him to sharpen his wits against, he could be really insufferable."<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly before her death in 1985, Maggie Potter recorded her thoughts about Koehler, "As I look back, it is evident that after Hugo's resignation from the Navy, I was his confidante; he would talk and I would listen.... In his apartment in Washington, when I first knew him, there was a bookcase and its three or four shelves were filled, not with books, but with photographs of women.  I do not think they were trophies; he genuinely liked and appreciated women and never denigrated ability in a female as many men of that period did....  He said it was stupid for us in Washington to associate with only our friends, who think as we do. One should seek out the obscure midwestern congressman and find out what he thinks, what his motives are.  Of course, Hugo was right....  I think I saw him truly.  I saw him as he appeared to most people: worldly, flamboyant, out of scale with Matilda's New York and Newport <a href="/wiki/Milieu" class="mw-redirect" title="Milieu">milieu</a>; but also I saw what was behind his protective exterior, a deeply loving man, full of compassion and insight, always ready to help someone else...."<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="For_further_reading">For further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: For further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1992 a collection of Koehler's letters and dispatches to the State Department and the Office of Naval Intelligence, painstakingly researched, compiled and edited by P. J. Capelotti was published as <i>Our Man in the Crimea: Commander Hugo Koehler and the Russian Civil War.</i> The book expands upon Capelotti's earlier research and doctoral dissertation on the subject.  It represents the culmination of years of research by Capelotti, and also Koehler's extant writings and the correspondence that Maggie Wood Potter had compiled before her death. Both Potter and Capelotti took on their respective projects at the behest of Koehler's stepson, Senator Claiborne Pell.  Capelotti's anthology of Koehler's writings focuses largely on his time in Russia in the years immediately after the First World War.  Pell remained determined during and after his long political career, to explore the rumors and shadowy experiences of his step father.  Pell's efforts included communicating with the <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese" class="mw-redirect" title="Archdiocese">Archdiocese</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> in an effort to confirm the existence of an Austrian trust, that Pell believed had been terminated upon Koehler's marriage to his divorced mother; enlisting <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> advisor <a href="/wiki/Zbigniew_Brzezinski" title="Zbigniew Brzezinski">Zbigniew Brzezinski</a> to investigate Koehler's possible connection to the <a href="/wiki/Habsburgs" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburgs">Habsburgs</a> and retaining an Austrian researcher.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Russian_Cossacks,_ca._1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/White_Russian_Cossacks%2C_ca._1920.jpg/300px-White_Russian_Cossacks%2C_ca._1920.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/White_Russian_Cossacks%2C_ca._1920.jpg/450px-White_Russian_Cossacks%2C_ca._1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/White_Russian_Cossacks%2C_ca._1920.jpg/600px-White_Russian_Cossacks%2C_ca._1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="666" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Russian_Cossacks,_ca._1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>White Russian Cossacks, ca. 1920</div></div></div><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Foreword" title="Foreword">foreword</a> to Capelotti's book, <a href="/wiki/Rear_Admiral" class="mw-redirect" title="Rear Admiral">Rear Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/Kemp_Tolley" title="Kemp Tolley">Kemp Tolley</a>, naval historian and author of numerous articles and three books on the subject of the U.S. Navy's activities in the Pacific, China, and the Soviet Union, and who assisted Capelotti in the review of his manuscript, wrote about Koehler, "From time to time- not often- there appears on the world's screen an individual of extremely sharp insight, broad powers of observation and analysis, and an unusually lively curiosity.  Such an individual was Hugo William Koehler- bold, adventurous, urbane, generous, and with a savoir faire that gained him ready entrance to the highest circles of international society. Something of a prophet, he foresaw the rise of Hitler's Germany, the ensurance at <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Versailles</a> of a new world war, and <a href="/wiki/Bolshevism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevism">bolshevism's</a> inevitable collapse.  In that cataclysmic time of world war and civil war, I know of no other American who so intimately associated with Russians of all levels, White and Red.  Koehler slept, ate, and galloped over the steppes like a cossack with them, week in and week out, at ease with their personal quirks and characteristics.  What those Russians are like and what they might do was revealed with stunning clarity by Commander Hugo Koehler."
</p><h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Decorations">Decorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Decorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Commander Hugo Koehler was awarded these orders, decorations and service awards:
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navy_Cross_citation">Navy Cross citation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Navy Cross citation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>The Navy Cross is presented to Hugo W. Koehler, Lieutenant Commander, U.S. Navy, for distinguished services in the line of his profession for duty in connection with preparation of submarine chasers for duty in the war zone and subsequently their operation in the Irish Sea and off the coast of Ireland.</i><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dates_of_rank">Dates of rank</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hugo_W._Koehler&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Dates of rank">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</li>

</li>
